Prologue

‘Long live the forgiving summer, and stay the deadly winter.’
Amber rays from the setting sun kissed the majestic Bergendal Mountains, as the deep sapphire waters from the old sage ocean veined through them, like they had for millennia. 
Townspeople were industriously working to increase their food storage and insulate their humble homes in preparation for the winter that was just around the autumn bend. Bergendalers were well-familiar with the preparations they needed to do before the first snowfall, for generations had gone before them, from season to season, in the same manner. Life for the northerners had been the same year after year, generation after generation, with only small variances sprinkled through the monotonously repetitive days and nights.
‘Long live the forgiving summer, and stay the deadly winter’ the northerner’s had pleaded their Norse gods for century after century, but no such plea was ever granted, for the balance in Midgard needed to be kept. 
The deceptively pure white winter was as ruthless as one’s nemesis, and though many might be granted a merciful death by her this year, many more would live to tell the tales of her prowling, silent assassinations. Winter changed boys' hearts into men and girls' innocence into wisdom – if one dared to stare winter in the face and stand up to the fears she forced into each mortal’s delicate heart. But winter was not here yet, only a looming promise soon to arrive. 
The Northlandic Castle stood on top of a soft sloping hill in the center of the valley. A soaring square-shaped impenetrable outer-wall with circular watchtowers at each corner protected the castle from possible attacks from Vik people. 
From each of the watchtowers hung blue, red and gold Bergendal crest ensigns. Rectangular shaped banners displayed a red serpent dragon holding a golden torch of light in one hand and a diamond sword in the other. Freedom, Balance and Light were King Olav’s mottos for his reign. Around the beast was one blue square, signifying Midgard’s four protecting Sentinors and one brown square signifying the four corners of Midgard. Above the dragon hovered a circle half-shaded navy, half-shaded light yellow, representing the crucial balance between day and night, light and darkness, progress and rest. 
Both the inside castle and the outer wall had been built out of a rare grey marble that carried a sheen and sparkled when it came in contact with sunlight, making it look lighter than it really was. Seven round, dark-grey steeples towered the skies on the inside keep, where the Sun Queen had resided for centuries.
‘Long live the deadly summer, and stay the deadly winter.’ But this coming winter would not be like other winters before. For this winter would be an eternal one, instigated by a dark being whom no one knew existed, and whose power was so consuming, that not even the gods of Midgard themselves could destroy. 



1

The Northlandic Kingdom  
Year 1007
The Escorts
 

I do not want to do this, Lucia thought, as she led the burial torch procession down the fog-filled streets of autumnal Bergendal. It was the day before the Late Summer Festival, a day that had been started in honor of Queen Maud when she had finally, after fifteen years of failing to produce an heir, conceived her first child, Lucia. Now Queen Maud was dead, and Lucia would be crowned Queen on her eighteenth birthday come December. 
I hate my father.
Lucia’s father, King Olav, had forced her to lead the town’s people through the city’s filthy, narrow streets. All those who mourned the passing of her mother, Queen Maud, reverently followed her in the snaking light parade. 
I just want to be alone right now. 
Her light blond hair gleamed in the light of the torches and unmercifully lit up her tear-streaked face in the night. 
Why did my mother have to die? 
She felt utterly alone now.
Citizens whispered sorrowfully about the tragic news they had heard earlier that day from the town crier’s lips. “The Queen of The Northlandic Kingdom is dead!” he had said, weeping as he had broadcast the tragedy from farm to farm, house to house, door to door. “The Sun Queen is dead!”
The procession ended in the castle courtyard in front of the unlit kindling and log pyramid structure. Lucia squeezed her torch in through the openings of the stacked wood, causing it to catch fire. She waited until the flames rose to the heavens and then signaled to Nora, her mother’s elderly handmaiden, to approach her. 
Nora curtsied, her kind eyes lowering to the ground. Nora had been Queen Maud’s handmaiden since before Lucia was born, and Lucia could not imagine a life without her. Nora was like a nurturing grandmother, always caring, always loving Lucia, even when she deserved it least. Her nearly silver hair was usually kept in a loose braid, and the deep grooves in her face stood as proof of all the happy and sad moments she had experienced in her lifetime.
“I am ready to retire for the evening,” Lucia said, taking Nora’s arm in hers. She pressed back the tears that were threatening to well up again.
When Lucia finally went to sleep that night, dreams of her childhood overcame her quickly. It was a welcome escape from her new reality of loneliness. 

*    *    *

The next morning, news came that one family’s longhouse had set fire by a torch from the light procession. The flames had passed too close to the straw thatch roof, lighting it and burning their humble home to the ground in a merciless flame. 
“The Queen is cursed,” the ill-fated family had declared, spreading the rumor like a raging inferno. It came to be believed through the city that night that Queen Maud’s spirit had been rejected from Valhalla, because she had been sympathetic to the new, Christian faith, and that she would continue to haunt Bergendal until a proper Norse burial had been performed. 
“Did you hear the news, Lucia? Did you?” Olav barged into her room red-faced with the guard who had delivered the gossip. “They say your mother is cursed. Cursed! How dare they?” His hands flailed in the air as he spoke. “No one grieves the loss of Maud more than I, and no one will be allowed to tarnish her memory!” He stood erect, like he always did when he was angry. 
What about me? I grieve her. Lucia watched her father flinging and shattering goblets, vases, and anything else he could get hold of, on to her black mahogany floor. She thought that he had gone slightly mad, as his hazel eyes filled with wrath. “Father, I need—I do not want to—” Tears, tears and more tears. Olav had never been a loving father to Lucia, and had even broken her arm in a fit of rage when she was just eight. She had hated him ever since. It frightened her that he was now going to be her only parent. She thought about Soren, her betrothed, and how she could not wait until she was married to him, so she would not have to deal with her father’s fury anymore. 
Olav trudged over to her bed. His hands hit his hips. “I need you to follow my instruction. You will need, as the future queen of the Northlandic Kingdom, to take your responsibility seriously. You will be crowned Queen on your eighteenth birthday. Stop feeling sorry for yourself! This is not about you!” He ran his long fingers through his salt and pepper hair.
Lucia’s tears fell onto her silk bed sheets. “I—I am sorry, father.” I miss mother. I hate him. She felt like such a child around her father, when all she wanted was to be treated like an equal, like an adult. Lucia snapped. “Just leave me alone!” she yelled, slamming her delicate fist into the bed. 
Olav stood speechless for a while, before he said, “You are an enigma, Lucia. Do you know that? One moment you are as sweet and innocent as a bird, and the next, you are like a vicious dragon, spewing fire. You need to work on your temperament before you become Queen.” He stormed out of her chamber.

*    *    *

Olav summoned Lucia to the throne room later that afternoon. She forced herself out of bed, knowing the consequences would be severe if she did not heed her father’s commands. Swollen-eyed and numb, she got dressed and went to meet her father. 
Arriving in the throne room, Lucia saw beams of dust-filled light shining in through the stained-glass windows, illuminating three murals on the opposite wall. Her Aunt Vilda and an elderly woman were speaking with her father by the thrones. 
Lucia knew the murals well, but studied them anyway. Do not think about Mother. Do not think about Mother… 
The first mural depicted the universe as a giant ash tree, Yggdrasil, and its nine realms: Asgard, Alvheim, Vanaheim, Midgard, Muspelheim, Svartalvheim, Nivlheim, Jotunheim, and Helheim. Its branches extended high into the heavens and stood on three roots that extended into Urd’s well, Mime’s well and into the spring, Kverg. Three giantess Norns from Jotunheim sat spinning the threads of fate of humans and gods in front of the well of fate, Urd. 
The fierce Dragon, Dreadbiter, slinked below the giant ash tree and fed off of dead mortals that fell his way. Sol and Mani graced the sky, one pulling the sun, one the moon, across the heavens in their chariots. The rainbow Bifrost connected Asgard to Midgard, allowing for the gods to access the land of the humans when they desired. 
“It must be an Norse burial, with a human sacrifice, or Allfather Odin will release his wrath on Midgard,” Lucia heard the elderly woman say. “Queen Maud deserves that much.” Mother! Mother! Tears welled up in Lucia’s eyes again. She could not stop them from coming.
The second mural depicted Ragnarok, Midgard’s final battle, when most men, darkelves, dwarves, and gods would be consumed by the gulf of non-existence. The sweltering red, fiery orange and charcoal black battle scene appeared to burn on the wall. Pained faces of nameless warriors battled the armies of the Empress of Darkness, Eiess. The three-year winter had beckoned in the wolf Skoll, who had devoured the sun, and his brother Hati, who had devoured the moon. In the corner a cock crowed, signaling that the final battle of Ragnarok had arrived. 
“Maud wanted a Christian burial,” Olav said. “But I agree, Odin and Thor will not be pleased if we worship this dead, crucified god of hers. Her dying wish, though, was to be buried outside the Bergendal Stave Church, and that wish, I will honor.”
“But Olav, you must realize that Maud is dead now, and you must be strong for her. She was wrong in thinking that this new, feeble religion is true. You know that, I know that, all of Midgard knows that!” Vilda, Olav’s older sister, said. Her fat-laden arms jiggled as she swung them.
Lucia wiped the tears from her face. She is dead. She is dead. She is dead. 
The third mural depicted the new world, as it would appear after Ragnarok. Only a few living beings remained and stood by a waterfall, drinking from a fresh, flowing spring. Olav had told Lucia her entire life that she was the Great Sentinor who was pre-destined to lead the battle of Ragnarok. What does that mean anyway? I am not her, she had always thought, every time he had mentioned it, and she certainly did not believe it now.
 “In case you have not noticed, the new faith is gaining many followers, Olav. I have even heard the Christian Bishop claim that we as humans are children of this God. The curse of Odin will fall on all of Midgard if you let these blasphemers continue,” the elderly woman said. “And if you bury Maud in Christian soil, you will be sending a very strong message that this type of worship is condoned by you as their King!”
Vilda was panting as usual, her heavy chest moving with every breath. “And Olav, you do not want to be known as the King who failed in leading his people and brought the wrath of the gods upon Midgard, do you?” she asked. 
Lucia listened intently to their conversation. Did my mother die because she betrayed Allfather Odin? Lucia did not know. 
“Lucia, come here,” Olav commanded. 
Lucia dragged her feet over to the stairwell, leading up to the two thrones. She had not entered the throne room since her mother had fallen down those very steps. She remembered the moment of the unimaginable dread. Maud had called Lucia into her chamber a few days after it had happened. Her mother’s pregnant belly had become more pronounced. Her lips had looked purple, and her cheeks had been a whitish-grey. 
 “I want to prepare you for the next phase in your life,” Maud had said solemnly, resting in her bed. “As future queen of the Northlandic Kingdom, you have a great responsibility. The Aesira Jewel will soon be in your hands, and you must protect it with your life.” Maud had closed her eyes and moaned.
The Aesira Jewel? Lucia had no idea what her mother was talking about. “Are you all right Mother?” Lucia had asked. “Should I go get someone for you?” 
“Not now, my love. Do you remember me speaking to you about your twin sister?” Maud had asked.
“Yes.” Lucia remembered her mother telling her about her twin and how she had died at birth.
“Her name means light, just like your name does.” Maud had grabbed Lucia’s hand. “I had a disturbing vision about you, Lucia. Great forces are seeking you. You will be given a choice that will have eternal consequences for you and your family. You must follow Christ, Lucia.”
“What do you mean?” Lucia had asked. Her mother had not made much sense, and she definitely did not want to follow this White Christ if this was his way of rewarding his followers. She thought her mother’s new religion was bizarre and weak. Who could worship only one God? And she thought it was strange to believe that even a god could be all-knowing or good all the time. Lucia’s faith had many gods, more gods than she could even keep track of.
“A choice must be made by you, and you alone. That choice, Lucia, is whether to do what is right, even when you think and know you have been wronged, or to deceive and selfishly take what is not yours.”
“But I know I will choose what is right,” Lucia had said. “I do not understand what you mean, Mother. You look unwell. Let me get Father.”
“You choose your path, Lucia. Soren is not meant for you, but for another.” Maud had closed her eyes again and moaned. “Just remember—that no matter what you choose to do—I will never love you any less.” Her breathing had become labored. “And remember, after Ragnarok, a new world will be born, and—all the others will vanish. This is—the future.” Suddenly, Maud had begun to scream. She had grabbed her stomach as her body started contracting. 
“Mother! Mother!” Lucia had yelled. 
Everything after that had been a blur, from when she had run to get her father, to when she had arrived back at the Queen’s Chamber and seen blood everywhere. Her father had shoved her back outside and left her alone to deal with her mother’s screams reverberating in the cold corridor. 
“I want to talk to my mother!” Lucia had begged. “I need to tell her something. What is happening? Please tell me.”
“Lucia, Lucia, look at me, focus,” Astrid had said, coming out into the corridor. 
But Lucia had not been able to focus. She had not even been able to breathe. The only thing she had been able to do was run, run to her room and throw herself on her bed. 
Nora had finally come to her. “Your mother—” 
Lucia had heard her beloved handmaiden start to cry. She had sat up from clenching her tear-soaked silk pillow. “Is she all right?” She had seen the answer in Nora’s pained face, and known that her mother was dead. 
“Lucia!” she heard her father yell. Her father’s voice brought her back to the present. “Sorry,” she said. I just want to disappear into oblivion.
Olav clutched Lucia’s elbow and guided her over to the elderly woman. “This is Ada, the angel of death. She is the country’s most gifted burial sage. I have appointed her to arrange the funeral,” he said. 
 Lucia twisted out of her father's painful grip.
Ada was a sturdy old woman with one deep-set, beady eye. The old wench wore a black patch over the other eye, probably to conceal an injured or missing eyeball and she dressed in layers of black and red. 
Frightened, Lucia thought Ada might be a sorceress or a demon, or quite possibly both in the form of a woman’s body.
“You will follow Ada wherever she goes while she is here,” Olav said to Lucia in his usual stern voice. 
Lucia curtsied out of politeness. I just want to be alone.
“A pleasure to meet you, the future queen of the Northlandic Kingdom.” Ada’s voice sounded as husky as a man’s. She shuffled over to Lucia, her crooked cane beating the floor. “Our first assignment together, Lucia, will be to select a volunteer to usher Maud into the next life.” She turned to Olav. “When can you have the handmaidens and servants gathered?” 
“I will send for them immediately,” Olav said.

*    *    *

After the castle’s entourage arrived, Ada lined them up in two rows. One row of sixty-four women mirrored the fifty-two men on the other side. They were all dressed in blue wool, Bergendal-crested, servant clothes.
Ada stood at the end of the ghastly silent, inward-facing rows. She handed Lucia her cane. “I need a volunteer—one of Queen Maud’s handmaidens or servants to join her in the after life.” She started walking down the center with her hands behind her back. Her emaciated face looked like a ghoul's in search of prey. “Surely, someone must have loved the Queen enough to escort her back home.” 
A cough echoed in the room.
Did no one love my mother? Lucia thought.
“Surely one of you could grant the late queen this kindness when she has given so much to you.” Ada turned around at the end of the line and gestured to Queen Maud’s empty throne. 
After a long pause, Astrid, one of the Queen’s personal handmaidens, stepped forward. She curtsied. 
Lucia gasped. No, not Astrid! Losing another loved one felt like salt on a raw wound, though she knew that her mother had loved Astrid so very much, and the handmaiden would make a great escort. Then, Lucia saw a second young maiden step forward from the line. Lucia did not recognize her. She stood about a foot lower than Astrid. 
“Ragnvei and I have decided to join our beloved Queen Maud in Valhalla.” Astrid adjusted her navy scarf, which covered her dark blond, braided hair. 
“Do you concede, Ragnvei?” Ada asked.
“Yes—uh, yes I do, Your Excellency,” Ragnvei said, lifting her gaze slightly, shaking her head ‘no’. Her curly ash-brown hair fell, as she grabbed the scarf off of her head and twisted it in her hands.
Lucia could not fail to recognize the look of fear in Ragnvei’s large blue eyes. Does she, or does she not want to be sacrificed?
“Princess Lucia, do you accept these two women to escort your mother, the Sun Queen, Maud of the Northlandic Kingdom, to Valhalla?” Ada asked.
Lucia hesitated. 
“Your Highness?” Ada pressed, her voice unfriendly.
 “Yes,” Lucia whispered.
“What?” Ada shouted.
“Yes,” Lucia said, louder.
“Then so be it!” Ada yelled. “You will both join Queen Maud in helping her spirit return to the halls of Valhalla!” 
The group of previously petrified onlookers breathed with unencumbered relief. 
 “Ivar and Arne, take these two courageous maidens to the Queen’s guest chambers where they will stay until their sacrifice and burial,” Ada said. “It is a great omen to us all to have two handmaidens usher the Queen’s spirit. May all here today be witness to that these young women volunteered of their own choice to be sacrificed and buried with Queen Maud. And may the funeral preparations begin!” 



2

Burial

Next morning, before sunrise, Ada stormed into the sacrificial chamber with Lucia. Ada had filled Lucia in on the, according to her, “sacrilegious events” that had happened during the night. Ragnvei had said that she wanted to be let out of her commitment in escorting Queen Maud. Apparently Gunnar, her lover, had visited her during the night and tried to talk her out of it. Ada seemed furious.
“I have spoken to the King about Ragnvei, and I want you to know two things,” Ada said, speaking to the two handmaidens. “One, the King and I have decided to move ahead with the burial regardless of Ragnvei’s hesitation, and two, you will be sacrificed in the most painful way possible, so we can gain the gods’ favor again.”
“No!” Ragnvei and Astrid said at once. 
Ragnvei knelt on the floor and clasped her hands together. “Please, please don’t do this! Just let me go! I want to live, especially now that I know Gunnar loves me. He told me so himself!”
Ada smacked Ragnvei’s hands down. “I heard he visited you last night. He should be put to death for having meddled with my sacred rituals. I will not entertain a handmaiden’s faulty opinion when the doom of Midgard is at stake. And there is nothing quite so dishonorable as offending the queen’s precious name. Her burial is a serious matter, not one to be trifled with, or made into a mockery.” She stepped closer to Ragnvei. “You do not deserve to live, you thoughtless, undisciplined, pitiful woman. You are a taker—no, a thief, defaming the queen’s good name. It is actually better for you and society that you are dead.”
Lucia thought her words were a little harsh. “But,” she interrupted Ada, placing her hands on Ragnvei’s shoulders. “I think, if she will move forward with the right outlook, she will be remembered as a true hero in all the Northlandic Kingdom. Would you agree, Ada?”
Ada paused. “Well—yes,” she finally said.
Ragnvei’s demeanor changed, Lucia could see. Her eyes gave up the fight, and she looked as if she had just realized that her death was inescapable. 
“So, these are the instructions you need to follow in preparation for the burial—” Ada said. “First, you will be given vision-inducing drinks to help your spirits become more susceptible to the messages from the gods. Then, you will be brought to the doorway of Valhalla. Then, your bodies will be prepared for the sacrifice. You will then be strangled and stabbed to death, and finally buried with Queen Maud,” Ada finished.
Astrid breathed heavily, and from the pale look on her face, Lucia could see that even Astrid had not received the information well.
Ada huffed. “Stop looking so gloomy, girls. It is an honor to die this way. Just think of all the heartache you will never have to experience. And remember, no virtue is higher than honor.” Ada took Lucia’s arm and led her into the hallway. “Now, we dress your mother,” she said.
Arriving at the door to the Queen’s Chamber, Lucia did not want to enter. “May I have a minute?” she asked Ada.
“Yes, but do hurry,” Ada said, squeezing through the double doors. 
Lucia did not want to go inside, did not want to face her mother’s dead body, fearing that her lifeless image would be all that she would remember forever. Instead, she traced the intricate workmanship on the copper doors and dark-wooden doorframe with her fingers. The doorframe was carved with serpents, dragons and swirly Norse patterns, and was decorated with warriors holding their weapons, standing in their extended longships. Southerners consider us Vik people, just because we share the same land. Lucia didn’t want to be associated with those berserkers.
Ada stuck her head through the door crack. “Well, come on then—”
“Yes,” Lucia said. She felt exhausted and had barely slept at all last night, afraid she would be visited by Mare Demons, who would fill her nights with visions of her dying mother. Lucia entered her mother’s chamber, jumping when the double doors closed behind her with a thud. She saw her mother lying in her bed, lifeless and gray, just as she had envisioned. I cannot do this. 
Ada wasted no time, and began directing the servants, handmaidens and seamstresses, stalking their every move as they scurried to and fro, attempting to fulfill the angel of death’s every request. 
“Oil for the Queen,” Ada said, coming up to Lucia. She blessed the small glass bottle and handed it to her. “Cover every part, and I mean every part, of your mother’s body with this.” 
Lucia stiffened.
“Do it now!” Ada grabbed Lucia by the hand and pulled her toward the corpse. “Your mother’s spirit needs to know that it is acceptable to you that she leave Midgard. You must let her go, Lucia, or she will never leave Midgard, and she will be miserable forever. Think of your mother—not yourself. You do want her to be happy, do you not?”
I am not ready to let her go yet. But Lucia still nodded. 
Ada opened the flask and poured some of its contents into Lucia’s hands. “Like so.” She rubbed the oil onto Maud’s blue skin.
Together, Ada and Lucia began rubbing the sweet-smelling oil onto the surface of the Queen’s cold remains. 
After a few strokes, Lucia started to feel lightheaded and queasy. “May I step out? I am feeling quite ill.” 
“No, no, no, no! We must finish the preparation of the body,” Ada said.
Just then, Lucia vomited all over the floor and her black mourning dress. 
Ada huffed. “Am I surrounded by incapable helpers? Pull yourselves together! Now, you—” She pointed to a handmaiden. “Clean this mess up and let us dress the body with these clothes here.” She handed the clothes to another handmaiden. “I expect you to contain yourself, Lucia, you hear? Now, go change. Nora, you go with her.”
“Yes,” Lucia said. She ran to her room with Nora, who helped her change her clothes.
“Let us get back. Try not to think about it, Lucia. It will all soon be over.” Nora said, her voice trembling, her hands shaking as she helped Lucia. She stopped Lucia in the hallway before they entered the queen’s chamber again. “I too miss your mother, Lucia. She was a wonderful person, as are you.”
Lucia hugged her thin frail body and cried in her arms for a moment. I do not ever want to let go.
Maud’s body was fully clothed by the time they returned. Her new burial outfit was an intricately hand-stitched dress made from the finest indigo silk. On her feet, they placed pointy, silk slippers, embroidered with Maud’s name on them. A team of handmaidens and guards lifted Maud onto her deathbed and draped the lower half of her body with silk sheets. 
“Now, her hair,” Ada said. “It needs to be combed, and then these flowers need to be interlaced into her hair like so.” She showed them.
The handmaidens started combing the Queen's long, golden hair. They picked up the wicker baskets, containing the white wax flowers, and intertwined them through Maud’s wavy hair. 
Lucia stepped closer and added a few flowers. Her mother looked so peaceful where she rested. She almost appeared to be sleeping, and Lucia thought that just maybe if she shook her roughly enough, she would be able to wake up her sleeping mother. “Where are you now?” she whispered. 
When Ada had finished dressing Queen Maud’s body, she summoned Olav. 
Vilda came instead and approached Lucia right away. Her hair was in a fancy up-do. Though she had never married, she always wore a married woman’s hair-do. “Your father refuses to come. He will not say why, but I think he fears it will bring back too many memories of when our mother died,” she said. “He never truly recovered from that, I think. Oh, and he told me to tell you that Soren will not be able to make it to the burial. He is in the Southlandic Kingdom again, fulfilling a commission.”
Lucia felt slightly disappointed, but decided not to care at this point. She had only met her betrothed, Soren, on a few occasions, and she had felt uncomfortable around him because he had spoken to her almost as if he already knew her. He had never acted inappropriately toward her, just a little too informal, Lucia thought, though he had seemed eager to get to know her. She was glad she would not have to worry about entertaining him during the worst time of her life.
Morning rays from the stained-glass window beamed upon the corpse. “Then let us begin,” Ada said.
I am not ready, Lucia thought. I am not ready to let her go.
Ada moved forward with the ceremony anyway, and the burial procession stood ready to start in the courtyard within the hour. “Not a single hair must be out of place!” Ada said. Her eyes searched the line-up. “The Queen’s spirit is waiting for us to make a mistake so she can remain here in Midgard and plague us. I am sure you would not want that blame on your heads, would you?” She encircled each wagon, each person, and each animal, as she passed them. Finally, after having checked everyone and everything, she nodded to Olav at the front-end of the procession.
At the head of the procession marched Bishop Peter, who was carrying the Bergendal crest ensign. Lucia had heard that Bishop Peter asked her father to be left out of the “barbaric,” in his opinion, sacrificial ceremony. He had said he was a man of the Christian faith and had put aside his pagan beliefs and rituals, and that his conscience dictated that he not involve himself in these Norse burial ceremonies. Her father’s reply had been simple, yet to the point: If the Bishop did not concede to be part of the burial, Olav would burn down the Bergendal Stave Church and banish Christianity from being taught in Bergendal. Bishop Peter had happily complied. 
The Christian Bishop should not even be here. He is a bad omen. Lucia could not understand why her father had let him participate, even though her mother had wanted it. Olav had never been a supporter of Christianity while she was alive, so why start now?
Twelve Deacons, lined up in two rows, followed Bishop Peter. Then, came Maud’s deceased body. Four black stallions pulled the open cart and displayed the body for all to see. Thousands of white and blue flowers blanketed the dray’s exterior, and white silk streamers hung from each of the four deathbed posts. King Olav and Vilda rode their horses behind the Queen’s wagon. Then came the two white oxen, which would be included as sacrifices in the grave. 
Behind the oxen, fourteen horses were individually hand-led by their own handmaiden. The horses would be ridden until exhaustion, and then sliced up and thrown into the burial with Queen Maud. 
Next came the last animal sacrifice, which were Queen Maud’s seven lundehund dogs. They were believed to possess the power to guide her back to Valhalla and ensure her a prosperous journey. Nora, their main caregiver, led the yelping hounds. 
Lastly, four of King Olav’s guards accompanied Ada, Lucia and the two sacrifices. When Lucia had entered the wagon, she saw that Ada had already given Ragnvei and Astrid goblets with hallucinogenic drinks, which they were both sipping. Lucia thought the ride in the carriage seemed like a never-ending journey to the valley of death. She kept glancing over at a very fidgety Ragnvei. The handmaiden clenched her sweaty hands around the goblet, looking out the carriage every few seconds. Maybe she is still thinking there is a way out. Lucia thought the girl was a traitor. 
“Why don’t you seem nervous?” Ragnvei asked Astrid. 
 “I have been blessed by the gods with peace in my soul. I am looking forward to escorting the Queen back to Valhalla and to going there myself,” Astrid said, her words sounding like a chant. 
“So, you’re not nervous?” Ragnvei asked, clenching her fists.
“Stop speaking,” Lucia said. She wanted to focus on her mother, and on trying to let her go. She peered out the window and saw to her dismay the skies lightning up in the distance. A thunderstorm was on its way to Bergendal. Lucia hoped they would finish the burial ceremony, before the rain descended, so that her mother’s grave would not be filled with rain.
Suddenly, an ax propelled through the air, making a quick swooshing sound, and hit one of the guards on the right side of his head, embedding itself in his cranium, half of his ear on one side and half on the other. 
Lucia jumped and Ragnvei screamed as blood splattered all over her face and dress. 
Ragnvei looked out into the crowd. “Gunnar!” she yelled.
Ada stood up. “Stop the procession!” Unable to spot the perpetrator, she stepped fearlessly out onto the dirt road. “Ivar! Find whoever did this and kill him!”
“Yes, my lady,” Ivar said. He stomped off in the direction the ax had come from. Finally, after what seemed like a lifetime, Ivar returned.
“Is he dead?” Ada asked.
“There will be no more trouble from Gunnar today,” Ivar said dryly. 
Ragnvei looked like she was going to go mad. “Oh Allfather, Allfather—” she repeated over and over, her body swaying back and forth.
Ada stepped back into the carriage and slapped Ragnvei across the face. “Be quiet, or I will kill you here and now, you coward. This is a disaster! It is like a living mare dream has descended upon us! I cannot believe the disrespect you have for the Queen and for the gods.” 
Astrid closed her eyes and started humming. A single tear rolled down her cheek.
Why cannot Ragnvei just accept her fate? Lucia thought. 
Nearing the church, more and more villagers joined in at the tail of the procession. The Escorts’s wagon stopped, and Lucia and the handmaidens stepped out. The queen’s wagon rolled up next to the giant longship, which had been almost completely immersed into the black, rich, and newly exposed soil. Ada led the sacrificial escorts and Lucia over to the far western side of the cemetery where the Valhalla doorway had been built. The first ceremony would involve the sacrificial escorts, Ragnvei and Astrid.
“Drink,” Ada commanded the two handmaidens, filling their gold, ruby-bejeweled goblets with more hallucinogens. 
They both drank deeply from the goblets. The guards lifted both girls up high into the air, so they could peer over the doorframe. 
“What do you see?” Ada shouted.
“I see the dead queen’s grandparents!” Astrid sang. 
How does she know what they look like? Lucia thought. She is surely lying.
“I see nothing,” Ragnvei said, her eyes already dead. “Only grass.” 
The guards laughed. By the look on Ada’s face, Lucia could see that she had not taken Ragnvei’s answer well. The guards lowered the girls down, and Ada bid them to drink from the hallucinogenic drink again. They both drank more, especially Ragnvei, Lucia saw. Again, the guards lifted the women up at Ada’s signal. 
“Now what do you see?” Ada shouted.
“I see all my relatives!” Ragnvei yelled. “I—I think I see my dead grandparents in the distance!” Her eyes squinted. 
The guards lowered the escorts down again to the ground and Ada gave them to drink one last time. 
Is Ragnvei playing along now? Lucia wondered.
Ragnvei stepped closer to Lucia. “My hands and feet are tingling,” she said, her body rocking back and forth. She laughed.
Yet a third time, the guards lifted the handmaidens up. “And now, what do you see?” Ada asked. 
“Valhalla, Valhalla, Valhalla,” the crowd chanted rhythmically in the background, cheering the women on.
“I see—I see—” Astrid said. “I see Queen Maud in the after world, beckoning for us to come to her!”
“Let us go to her!” Ragnvei said. “Paradise is green and beautiful.” Ragnvei started to cry. “My tears are tears of joy! I yearn to reunite with the queen and feel proud that I’ve been selected to help her on her journey to Valhalla. It is beautiful on the other side: green, magical and peaceful.”
“She is there!” Astrid said, looking over the tall doorpost. “She looks young and healthy, and has a babe, her unborn son, in her arms!”
Lucia desperately wanted to see her mother again.
The guards lowered the two handmaidens down. 
“It is time.” Ada smiled.
As Lucia, Ragnvei, and Astrid made their way over to the grave, the pathway of people cheered them on. 
“I have never felt like someone important, but now I feel like the most courageous handmaiden in all of Midgard,” Ragnvei said to Lucia. She swayed and stumbled forward, singing a happy tune.
Lucia felt better now. At least Ragnvei seems at peace. The gods will be happy. Almost at her mother’s grave, Lucia saw her father and Vilda leaning over her mother, crying as they held onto each other in their grief. She could not help her tears from coming now, flowing like streams of agony down her pained face. She ran up to her father and held him.
Ragnvei stopped in front of them. “I saw your queen, Your Highness, and she is more beautiful and healthy than ever. Your baby boy was with her, and all is well.” Ragnvei said. Then she looked at Lucia. “Don’t mourn, Princess. Your mother will be in Valhalla soon. We will take her there safely today.”
Lucia acknowledged her with a nod and gave her a hug. “Please tell her—I—uh, miss her,” she said, wiping the tears off her face.
Ada, over by the tent, opened the flap and signaled to the escorts to get inside. She looked over at Lucia and waved for her to join them.
Lucia looked up at her father. “Do I have to?” She had not thought she would actually have to participate in this part of the ceremony. When she saw her father’s stern face, she knew the answer and started to walk over.
“No, wait,” Olav said, grabbing her by the arm. “Wait until I tell you.”
Ada turned to the multitude and addressed them. “Any man who desires, may enter now and consummate these holy sacrifices with his seed.”
Eleven men made their way over into the tent. One by one, and every few minutes, Lucia saw the men come out. Some were adjusting their shirts and trousers as they exited. When the last man exited the tent, Ada signaled to Ivar and four other guards to enter with her.
“Go now.” Olav gave Lucia a little shove. Lucia entered the tent.
“Make sure you tell your guards and the men in the crowd to beat their swords and spears on their shields as loudly as they can, so the women and children do not hear the screams of the escorts,” Lucia heard Ada whisper into Ivar’s ear. Lucia’s stomach churned, and she feared what was coming. 
“Yes, my Lady.” Ivar nodded. 
Lucia saw the highly intoxicated women, moaning and worming, as their naked, ravaged bodies lay on two wooden tables. 
Ada took a rope from the tool table and gave it to the guard above Astrid’s head. He wrapped it loosely around Astrid’s neck, waiting for Ada’s signal. Ada nodded to the guard at Astrid’s feet, and he tightly gripped the handmaiden’s ankles, pressing them down into the table. 
“Ouuuch,” Astrid squealed.
Lucia heard the hammering sound of shields and swords colliding outside.
“May you, handmaiden and chosen escort of Queen Maud, fulfill your purpose this day as we release your spirit from your physical body to usher the queen’s spirit to Valhalla!” Ada’s voice sounded like a mystical trance. She walked over to the table again and picked up a wide triangular iron dagger. Back at Astrid’s side, Ada raised the dagger high above her head with both arms and plunged it into Astrid’s chest. 
Astrid let out a loud shriek and arched her back in pain. The guard above Astrid’s head pulled the rope tightly around Astrid’s neck. Ada plunged the knife again and again into Astrid’s chest until she no longer moved. Blood spilt from the table onto the green grass below. Ada looked at the lifeless sacrifice and smiled. “Now, the other,” Ada said, turning her attention to Ragnvei.
Lucia was in shock. She had not expected it to be so vicious a ritual, but she did not want to close her eyes in fear that it would make her look like a coward.
“Wait! What is this?” Ada yelled. She pulled Ragnvei up by the hair. “Is this what I think it is?” 
Lucia thought it looked like a small tattoo in the shape of a cross in the nape of Ragnvei’s neck.
“So?” Lucia said. “She has agreed to be sacrificed, and she is even happy to do it now.”
“No, she cannot and must not be sacrificed. The gods will never accept one who has the indelible markings of another god on her body,” Ada said.
“But my mother must make it to the other side. She needs both of her escorts. Think of all the things that can go wrong if she comes to haunt us or comes to haunt you,” Lucia said.
“It is better this way. Ivar!” Ada yelled. 
Ivar came back inside the tent and bowed.
“Dress this heathen. She is impure, and of no further value in this ceremony,” Ada said.
Shocked, Lucia ran out to her father and told him what had happened.
Olav paused for a moment. “Yes, Lucia, you are right. The queen does need to make it to Valhalla today. But the sacrifice cannot be impure, or your mother’s spirit will surely come to plague us.”
 “And having her buried in Christian soil will not bring on the gods’s wrath?” Lucia’s face turned beet red, her eyes fuming at her father.
Olav lifted his arm, looking as if he would strike Lucia. Lucia closed her eyes and prepared for the blow, but it never came. Instead, when she opened her eyes, she saw him enter the tent. She had never hated her father more.
When the crowd saw Ivar leading Ragnvei out of the tent alive, they started to jeer and throw rocks at her. Olav stepped forward. 
“We have found the sacrifice to be impure. We cannot sacrifice a Christian to our gods, now can we?”
Hypocrite, Lucia thought.
“Nay!” The crowd yelled, and then grew silent.
“Queen Maud has Astrid with her now. And knowing my queen, she could get to Valhalla all by herself, even if she were blindfolded and gagged!
The crowd laughed.
“But, the gods would be even more pleased if we had another volunteer, one of you perhaps?” Olav said. 
The crowd hushed; uneasy eyes glanced around. 
Quietly, one maiden walked up to the platform. “I will go!” she said. “I lost my family to the Vik people. I have nothing to live for here in Midgard, and I have been looking forward to the day when I can die with honor and join my family in Valhalla!” She knelt before Olav.
“Do you accept her offer?” Ada asked Lucia and Olav.
“Yes!” Olav said.
“Yes,” Lucia said, feeling pressured into accepting a secondary sacrifice.
The crowd roared. A faint chant started in the back and waived its way to the front of the crowd. “Sacrifice her! Sacrifice her! Sacrifice her!”
Ada fetched the dagger from the tent and lifted it into the air for all to see.
“Sacrifice her! Sacrifice her!” The chant grew louder and louder.
Grabbing the young maiden by her red hair, Ada sliced her throat and let her collapse to the ground. She gestured to the guards to come pick her up. “Now, we bury the queen!”
Along with the escorts, Olav sacrificed the fourteen horses, two oxen, and seven dogs, placing them in the burial ship with Maud. Maud’s body was now covered with sheets of silk, so Lucia could not see her face. She was glad she did not need to see her mother’s face covered with dirt.
On the bottom of the ship, they laid her crown, her royal jewels, brooches, hairpins, necklaces and rings, her most loved dresses and her bone-carved combs. King Olav had also included four of Maud’s five sleighs, her carved four-wheel wooden cart, and her favorite copper chests.
As Olav rejoined Lucia and Vilda on top of the platform, reverence filled the air. 
Ada started speaking. “In life, she was a Queen worthy of praise and admiration, a wife, a friend, and the mother to all. Maud will be remembered by us as a generous, kind, and great Monarch. In death, she will advance even further and go ahead of her family to prepare a home for them on the other, better side. Now, we lay her body to rest and to sleep forever. Her spirit continues eternally beyond the grave. May her family find peace in her absence and look forward to the day when they will meet her again. For life is not the beginning, nor is it the end. Death is not a beginning, nor the end, but a continuation of the eternities we have always been part of. When you look at the Auroras in the sky, remember your ancestors, remember the spirits yet to be born, for they are there, always watching, always existing, and always a part of your journey. Will you pray with me?” 
Silence permeated the somber crowd. 
“Allfather, Odin, we release Queen Maud’s spirit unto you this day. We thank you for the time we had her here. We thank you for the memories we have had with her that we cherish in our hearts, even more precious than gold. Allfather in Valhalla, we ask: give Queen Maud rest, health, and peace with you. And may we always remember her, as we remember you and all the gods, in our hearts and memories eternally.”
The clouds were quickly moving in, threatening to fill the sunken burial ship with its rain. All the king’s guards started throwing dirt, rocks and soil into the ship. Little by little, the onlookers disappeared into their houses and farms.
Lucia could not stop the tears from coming, but she did not care. These were the last moments she would have with her mother here in Midgard, and she knew she had to do the right thing and let her go to be with the gods. 
“I have thought for days of what I might say to you, Olav, but there are no healing words strong enough to lessen your pain,” Vilda said to Olav.
“I believe you of all people are probably the only one who truly understands,” Olav replied.
He must think I don’t have feelings, Lucia thought. She despised him for that.
“I am surprised you buried her in Christian soil,” Vilda said. “And that you used her inscription on the runestone too.”
“Some things need not be justified, Vilda. Not even to you,” Olav said.
Vilda grew silent, but Lucia could see anger brewing in her navy eyes.
As the rain began to descend more heavily, they remained still, resolute, and immobile, waiting loyally to the end until Maud’s burial was complete. 
When the guards finally finished, twenty-four stones were laid down in the shape of a longship to mark the outer borders of the Queen’s grave, the longship’s dragonhead and tail still protruding from the black, fresh earth. A large runestone was set atop the burial. 
Lucia read it out loud:

King Olav had this stone erected in memory of his wife Queen Maud who died in childbirth.
May God and God’s Mother help the souls of Queen Maud and her unborn son.
Inside this grave the Beloved Blessed Queen Maud rests.
Concealed she’ll remain, and to us buried ever after.
The King Olav, in Holy rituals,
his Northern Queen, his Jewel, he released and freed
with Sacred sacrificial rituals.
Consecrated are these writing for her.
For Jesus is the Savior and one Redeemer and Elect
Sanctified, Hallowed Being.
Having been born of Mary and the Father, pre-mortally Chosen and Destined to live for all mortals
every one so victorious, their lives end.
Yet thankfully these sorrowful tears will vanish.
And conquer, Jesus will, the Devil and Destroyer.
Mournful are All, and all are crying with hidden faces buried inside their palms.
Bergendal city Christian church.

A deep red serpent border curved around the edges of the runestone and framed the inscription on it. Maud’s favorite symbol, the Valknut triangular symbol of pre-life, mortal life and afterlife, was engraved on the runestone’s top and on the back. She had written the inscription herself immediately after her fall and had demanded Olav put it on her gravestone. 
Lucia did find some comfort in the fact that her father had honored at least some of the Norse burial practices.



3

outcast

Today I will die, Ailia thought
She carefully peeled back the distressed linen curtain of their humble two-room longhouse, and glanced out the window. A trail of black smoke followed the Surtorians, the Empress of Darkness’s, agents. Empress Eiess’s longships had arrived at the harbor right after dawn, and Ailia knew that Eiess would burn the city to the ground unless Ailia surrendered herself. No one but Ailia and her family knew who Ailia was, and most didn’t even know that such an evil being as the Empress of Darkness existed.
Eiess found us again. 
Ailia had been hiding out in Whiteheim with her husband, mother and daughter for the past few months, but now it became clear to her, that no matter where they hid in Midgard, Eiess would track them down.
“They are almost here! You must leave! Leave now, with Freydis before the Surtorians find us!” Ailia yelled to her mother, Edna. Ailia’s heart hammered like rhythmic thunder, as if it was threatening to burst through her chest. “Oh, may the gods be merciful today!” she whispered, terrified. Her husband should have been back by now, too, and without him here, they had little chance of survival.
Suddenly, Ailia woke up gasping. Then, she realized what she had just seen was her recurring mare dream. She breathed a sigh of relief, glad she had woken up before the worst of it had come to her. However, she couldn’t quite shake the same thoughts and feelings she always had after this dream. She felt disturbed and frightened, as if the dream had a little more earthly weight to it than any other time she had dreamt it. 
I must keep myself hidden from The Empress of Darkness. I need to find this man who was my husband, so we can—why were we running from Eiess? It had seemed so important just a brief moment ago. The feeling of utter urgency started to dwindle and the details of the dream started to fade. She shook her head to try and get any other remnants of the dream out of her mind. The idea of a horrible empress pursuing her always seemed to be the hardest feeling to shake.
Ailia flung the heavy reindeer throw off her slender body and sat up from her bed. This was the third time she remembered having the same mare dream, and she felt even more confused than she had the first and second time she had dreamt it. Who is this empress I keep dreaming of, and why is she after me? And who is this man who is my husband in my dreams? Ailia needed to find the answer to both questions, even though the thought of it thrust her into a state of fear far beyond the borders she had ever thought she’d be willing to delve. 
Ailia could hear her Aunt Unni working in the kitchen, and heard the crackling sound of a fire coming from the hearth. She rose from her bed quickly and went to the basin near her bed. Then she splashed some water on her face and got dressed. She threw on her rust red wool shawl over her narrow shoulders. It’s cold today, she thought, even in the house. 
“What would you like for me to do to help?” Ailia asked as she entered the small warm kitchen. As always, it was stocked with pots, pans, ladles and various types of fruits and vegetables. She grabbed her white work scarf and secured her waist-length auburn hair with it. 
“Good morning,” Unni said. Her strawberry-blond, gray-streaked hair was also tied up in a scarf. “The carrots need cleaning.” She pointed to a heap of the dirt-covered vegetables on the oak countertop. 
Ailia thought Aunt Unni looked great after having gained some weight over the summer. The harvest had been plentiful this year, and they’d make it through the bitter winter months, probably even with food to spare. 
“Yes, Aunt Unni,” Ailia said, picking up the first carrot.
“I couldn’t ask for a sweeter girl than you.” Unni hugged Ailia generously, like she always did at the start of a new day. 
‘Fiery, but too kind,’ were the words Ailia’s Uncle Brander had always used to describe Ailia. “Ailia is too nice for her own good, and needs to stand up for herself more,” he had also said once, and Ailia didn’t like it one bit. She didn’t like him telling her that she was kind but had no willpower of her own, because she felt he was calling her naïve, and naïve people were the same as obtuse people in Ailia’s opinion. 
“Do you think I’m naïve?” Ailia asked Aunt Unni.
“Where did that question come from all of a sudden?” Unni said.
“Uncle Brander says I’m fiery, but too kind,” Ailia said, frowning.
“Well, you are kind, not naïve, and just the right amount of kind. And the fiery side of you helps balance you out.”
Ailia smiled.
“But remember, we love you no matter what,” Unni said.
Ailia sat down and got to work cleaning the carrots. The hearth’s flames burned eagerly in the center of the kitchen floor, heating the chilly room and warming her cold body. 
“Where’s Sigrid? Usually she’s up by now.” Ailia was not sure what her family would do without their hard-working thrall. 
Brander had bought Sigrid from a group of nomads when Sigrid was only twelve years old; years before Ailia had been adopted by Aunt Unni and Uncle Brander. Unni and Brander didn’t treat Sigrid as a thrall but rather as a family member. And even though Sigrid had a labored gait, caused by her left leg being significantly shorter than the other, she could still outperform any other thrall from any other farm. 
“I sent her out to fetch some more water for the stew,” Unni said, glancing up at Ailia. She placed heaps of potatoes into a wicker basket. “You look worried. Is everything all right?” Unni asked. 
Ailia sighed. “I had a mare dream again last night. It’s probably nothing.”
“The same one you’ve had before?” Unni asked. She wiped the sweat off of her forehead and onto her ivory linen sleeve.
“Yes,” Ailia said. “Thankfully I woke up before the worst of the dream this time.”
“Why don’t you share the whole dream with me? I know you don’t like talking about it, but sometimes God sends us messages in times of great need.”
Ailia hesitated for a moment and then nodded. “All right. Maybe you can help me decipher its meaning.” Ailia picked up another carrot to clean and sighed deeply. She recounted the dream to Unni. “I feel so scared in my dream.”
“You still sound scared,” Unni said, chopping the vegetables for the stew. “Make sure you get all the dirt off the carrots. Was that the whole dream?”
“Yes,” Ailia said, feeling her hands tremble as she worked.
“And do you die at the end every time?”
“Yes,” Ailia said, pausing from cleaning the carrots.
“Keep cleaning, you are thinking too much on the dream. Today is the Late Summer Festival and if this stew is not ready in the next two hours, we will be the laughing-stock of the town,” Unni said slightly mockingly. “We need to have the festival meal prepared before the day starts. I don’t want to be cooking in this kitchen when we could be listening to stories or participating in the festivities. And Uncle Brander gets home soon and he’ll want breakfast right away.”
Ailia picked up her pace again. She continued recounting her dream. “I actually didn’t know the empress’s name until this last dream. Her name is Eiess. She has black hair and intense green eyes and seems young, but somehow ageless.” 
“Interesting,” Unni said.
“She always tells me she’s glad she finally found me and that now I only have one more life to live.” Ailia sighed.
“Hmmm…”
“Have you ever heard of Eiess?” Ailia asked.
“No,” Unni wiped her hands on her rough linen apron. “Potatoes next,” she prompted. “Is she from one of the other eight realms?”
“I don’t know.” Ailia started cleaning the potatoes, pondering on another unusual dream. “I know you and Uncle Brander don’t believe that I have lived before, but I think I might have. Iluxia told me.”
“Who?” Unni said.
“The leader of the lightelves, Iluxia. He told me in my dream a few weeks ago,” Ailia said. The dream where Ailia had met Iluxia had fortunately not been a frightening one. It had only been a vivid dream where she had met Iluxia at the entrance of the realm of Alvheim—a realm she knew little of. 
 “But I already know who I am,” Ailia had replied. “I’m just a girl living in Bergendal, trying to do my best in fulfilling my duty as a peasant and honoring my family.” 
Iluxia had smiled. “You have lived before, Ailia, and you have a grand purpose in life. Much grander than just being a peasant,” he had said. 
He had left Ailia feeling confused. Now she wanted to find out what he had meant. But why did he have to be so vague? Couldn’t he have just spelled it out for me? she thought whenever she pondered on the dream.
Unni smiled. “Well, I'm not here to tell you who you have or have not met or what you did or didn't do. Your uncle and I know your history as far back as seventeen years ago when you were brought to us by a soldier on a winter solstice day,” Unni said. “But our faith tells us that humans live only once and that's it. You know that, Ailia.”
Ailia didn’t know what to believe. She felt like she had lived another life even though she had no memory of it. Finally she said, “I hope I never dream of Eiess again. It was as if she sucked all the life out of me as I stood in her presence.” Ailia moved on to cleaning the next gnarly potato.
“Will you fetch me the ladle over there?” Unni asked, pointing toward it. 
Ailia went over and picked it up. “These dreams are not like other dreams. These dreams seem real, and they make me feel like I actually have a very important purpose in life.”
“Well, we all have an important purpose in life,” Unni said. 
Ailia smiled. “I know. But there is something more to these dreams, something that I just can’t explain.”
“Maybe you’re having all these mare dreams because of the recent Vik attack,” Unni said.
“Maybe,” Ailia replied. The Vik people had recently raided Bergendal. Fortunately Bergendal was one of the largest cities in the Northlandic Kingdom and had survived the attacks. “I cannot say those attacks have not affected me.” But Ailia knew Unni wasn’t grasping what she was trying to explain. And how could she? There was no way Ailia could describe to Unni exactly what she had seen or what she had felt.
“And with Queen Maud’s funeral yesterday the mare demons have certainly been active around here,” Unni said. “Those mare demons—I wish they would just leave us alone.”
Unni had always said that mare dreams came from mare demons whispering frightening words and conveying evil thoughts into peoples’ ears while they were sleeping. Ailia didn’t know if mare demons were why she was having frightening dreams. The dreams were just too vivid and potent for her to imagine them as just evil thoughts given to her by a mare demon.
“How did Queen Maud die exactly?” Ailia asked, dumping her carrots into the stew. No one had really spoken of it, and the only thing Ailia had heard about the queen’s death was that her spirit would be cursed forever if she didn’t receive a proper Norse burial. The things people make up, Ailia thought. She felt sorry for the queen, the king and especially for the poor princess, Lucia. People should show more concern.
“Well, I heard she fell down some steps, and since she was expecting a child, it had been so much worse,” Unni said.
“You met her once, didn’t you?” Ailia asked. She sat down on a wood stool Brander had just finished making.
“Yes,” Unni said, stirring the pot. “She came to our farm exactly one year to the day after you had been brought to our doorstep by Ivar. She said she had lost one of her lundehunds, and asked to search our farmlands for him. After some time, she knocked on our front door again. I brought you out with me because you had started crying the moment the queen arrived and would not stop no matter what I did. She asked if she could hold you and had a strange keen look in her eyes as she held you.”
“Did she say why she wanted to hold me?” Ailia asked. She had heard this story so many times, but loved to ask the same questions every time she heard it.
“No, but she asked me when you were born. I told her the gods had blessed us and a guard had brought you to us on winter solstice day. She said that her daughter, Princess Lucia, was born on the exact same day and that she was looking for a friend for Lucia.”
“I don’t remember meeting her at all,” Ailia said, all done with the potatoes.
Unni added the potatoes to the cauldron. “Well, of course you don’t. You were only a one-year-old then. That was the only time I ever met her. She seemed like a sad person, like she had some deep, dark hidden pain that she carried.”
“Why do you think she was so sad?”
“I don’t know,” Unni said. Then she smiled mischievously. “Maybe she was worried that she’d never get her daughter married off!”
“Yes, that would be something to—wait a minute. You’re talking about me, aren’t you?”
“If you would just accept one of the suitors we’ve introduced you to, I think I’d die of happiness,” Unni said with a loud huff.
Lately, their conversation always ended on this subject, and Ailia didn’t like talking about it one bit. 
“I don’t want to get married to just anyone, Aunt Unni,” Ailia said. “I have someone special waiting for me.” 
“Oh really? So you think you have a say in whom we choose for you to marry?” Unni asked, looking at Ailia, raising her right eyebrow.
Ailia leaned forward passionately against the wooden counter top. “Of course I do. I’ll let you know when I meet him, my soul mate,” she said.
“Oh? And what do you know about soul mates?” Unni asked.
“Plenty.”
“Like what?” Unni pressed.
“Well, I know that I have one,” Ailia said. “But in my dreams, I always get the distinct impression that I need to find him so that we can join together to defeat Eiess.”
Unni laughed. “Just let us know when you meet him then. We’ll want to be included in the dramatic marriage. But, if we find you a proper husband before then, you’ll be marrying him.”
Ailia grimaced. This past year several suitors had tried to win her heart, but none of them had interested her in the least. Most of them had tried to impress her with their brute behavior, showing off how great their hunting and fighting skills were, and Ailia didn’t like that type of conduct at all. It reminded her too much of the Vik men that had pillaged their city. She was beginning to think that she was being too particular, especially since all of the young women her age in the neighboring farms had been married off years ago. Thankfully, Unni and Brander hadn’t forced her to marry anyone yet, but Brander had started to seem a little impatient with Ailia and had even introduced her to three different men this last week. 

*    *    *

After all the preparations for the day had been made, and after the Late Summer Festival meal at Brandersgaard, Ailia was allowed to go meet up with some of her friends at the festival. She hadn’t told Unni that her friends had started ignoring her as soon as Ailia had opened up and told them about her mare dreams. Maybe they thought Ailia was a little crazy. But she decided that her friends weren’t going to prevent her from having a good time.
She got dressed in her hunter green wool tunic and grabbed her sheep mask on the way out. 
“I’ll be there shortly!” Unni hollered after her.
The first place Ailia wanted to go was to the masked dance at the Fest Hall. It was the town’s largest longhouse, and their communal place for meetings, governing assemblies and festivities. It had been built as a dome-covered longship, with a straw thatched roof and logs lining the outer walls. Ailia loved to dance, and she had never missed a dance since she had turned twelve, the age where all young maidens were allowed to attend their first official festival dance. 
When Ailia arrived at the Fest Hall, Bergendal fiddlers and men with falster pipes, lures, and drums were at the end of the hall, warming up their instruments. Three other people had arrived before her, one with a goat mask and two with cow masks. Ailia smiled and waved at them, but they didn’t even so much as acknowledge her. That’s strange, Ailia thought. Most Bergendalers were very warm and welcoming. Her friends, or former friends, hadn’t arrived yet. She hoped when they did that they would include her in their circle tonight. She sat down to wait on one of the benches that lined the eastern wall.
“Are you the girl who is cursing Bergendal with all these mare demons?” A young redheaded boy, wearing a dog mask came up and asked. 
“What?” Ailia said, feeling embarrassed.
“My big sister says you are a bad omen, and she says that you are cursing Bergendal by bringing all these mare demons here,” the young boy continued.
Now Ailia recognized the boy. He was one of Ailia’s friend’s younger brothers—Siri’s brother. “I’m not cursing Bergendal with anything,” Ailia said. She folded her arms across her chest and frowned. “It was just a scary dream.”
“My dad says mare dreams are messages from the dark realms, and that you are attracting beings from the dark realms here to Bergendal. He says you’re cursed.” He pointed his finger at her.
“Stop that!” Ailia said. “You’re embarrassing yourself, and you’re embarrassing me!”
“You are cursed!” he yelled, still pointing his finger at her. A few more people had arrived, stopping to stare. Ailia thought it strange that no one came over and reprimanded the misbehaving boy or came to stand up for her. Finally, Siri came over. Her light ash brown hair was pulled up in a braided up-do.
“Is it true? Did you really tell your brother that I’m a bad omen just because of my dreams?” Ailia asked her.
“I thought you knew by now, Ailia. We all believe that,” she said, stepping next to her brother. She squinted her eyes and her lips. “You are a bad omen—and cursed.”
“What are you talking about? I’m still the same person. I can’t believe how shallow you are, if you are going to hold my dreams against me,” Ailia said.
“My father has told me to stay away from you. I should have realized that you are cursed sooner. I mean, even your name is so strange,” Siri said.
“My name?” Ailia looked around the hall, and everyone who had arrived was staring at her. Even the musicians had stopped warming up their instruments and glared at Ailia across the murky room. Ailia smiled nervously. “I’m not cursed,” she said. “I’ve just been having some frightening dreams. That’s all.”
“And you said you believed that you have lived before too. Our chieftain probably wouldn’t be too happy to hear about that. He’ll be here later tonight, and if you stay here for the dance, I’m afraid I’ll have to tell him everything you told me,” Siri said.
Ailia couldn’t believe that Siri had ever claimed to be her friend. How could she do this? They had known each other for five years, and Ailia had worked so hard to help Siri feel welcome in Bergendal when her family moved here from another town. Ailia didn’t want to continue to make a scene, so she decided to go home. Heading for the exit, she heard Siri yelling after her.
“Don’t think this is over, Ailia. You must turn from your ways, or I’ll make sure you’ll be banned from Bergendal for good.”
Ailia took the shortcut home to Brandersgaard running through the Bergendal forest. She swallowed her tears back again and again all the way home. The thin pathway she followed wound its way between soaring pine trees, moss-covered rocks, fallen branches and grasses. The cool autumn wind streamed across her face. 
She hopped across the narrow most part of the Bergendal River, stepping on the black and green rocks that protruded above the rushing water. She didn’t stop to say hello to any of the people who were on their way to the festival, and she didn’t even stop when she ran into Unni, who had just left Brandersgaard and was headed to the dance. She just wanted to get away for a while, find a quiet place to think, to clear her mind and find her balance again. She stormed inside, sat down by the loom and started embroidering a tablecloth she had almost finished. 
“Is everything all right?” Unni asked, coming in the front door.
“I think I’m sick,” Ailia said sullenly. “Please, just go to the festival. I’ll be fine.”
“Very well. Are you sure I can’t help you with anything before I go?”
“No, I just need to rest,” Ailia said. “Please, go have a great time without me. There will be many more festivals like this one for me to attend.” Ailia smiled to Unni, and Unni turned around and left. But something told Ailia that she wouldn’t be attending any festivals for a long time. She stopped sewing, let the tablecloth fall to her lap and started sobbing.


4

Never love

The next day, Uncle Brander seemed eager to get out of the house.
“Come on, Ailia, come swimming with us,” he said, dressed in his swim outfit, ready to walk over to Bergendal Lake with their neighbors. His thick arms were tan and his hairy chest and belly were as white as virgin winter snow.
“No, thank you. I would like to stay home,” Ailia said. She was afraid of water, though she didn’t know why, and stayed as far away from fjords and lakes as possible. Because of that fear, she had never learned how to swim properly. She rationalized that she didn’t need to know how to swim to become a good housewife. She had always believed that becoming a good housewife should be every Norse girl’s highest priority, so she didn’t feel too bad about being a non-swimmer.
“Your friends will be there,” Brander urged.
“No, not today,” Ailia said. She was content to sit in her chair, intent to not look up from her needlepoint. “You know I don’t swim. I don’t understand why you are asking me to come.” She didn’t want to run into anyone who had seen what had happened at the Fest Hall with Siri. Being called out as a curse in front of everyone was not something she wanted to experience again. She had not told Unni or Brander about the incident yet. They didn’t need more to worry about, she thought. She still couldn’t shake off her recurring drowning dreams either, dreams that had intensified over the last few days. “You go ahead. Maybe I’ll join you some other time.”
“Today is the last day we will be able to go. Next week, I’ll be out hunting and then winter will be here. Besides, you are nearly a grown woman. I thought you’d be over your childish fear of the water.” Brander seemed unusually disappointed and his mammoth body slumped as he spoke.
Ailia didn’t respond. She didn’t like disappointing Brander and would rather ignore him than disappoint him.
Brander shook his head. “Actually, I have someone I’d like you to meet,” he said softly.
Not one of his suitors again, Ailia thought. She was growing tired of the eligible—or in her opinion, not so eligible—men Brander presented to her. “Bring him over for repast,” Ailia said and smiled at him. “I’ll make him the best stew he’s ever eaten.” Brander rolled his eyes. He knew she hated making stew and every stew she had ever made had turned out to be a disaster. Cooking was her main homemaking weakness. “Don’t worry. I’ll get Unni to help me—I promise.”
“Yeah, yeah—” Brander said, heading for the door. “The man I wanted you to meet is the one you are marrying.”
Ailia thought maybe she had not heard him correctly. “What?”
“I’ve already agreed to it,” Brander said.
Ailia stood up, her embroidery falling to the floor. “What do you mean, you’ve already agreed to it?”
Just then, Unni walked in from the kitchen. “Did you tell her?” she asked Brander.
Brander nodded.
“Talk to him, Unni. You cannot just marry me off to someone I’ve not even met!” Ailia said.
“Ailia, you have a duty to us as your family to accept a suitable husband,” Unni said. “We have gone along with your non-interest in all the men we have brought to meet you for far too long. You’re almost eighteen and well past the age of when you should be married.”
“His name is Geir and he’s a chieftain from Solnes. He’s a little older than you. His wife died three winters ago and he’s been looking for a suitable wife ever since her death. I’ve known him for a few years. He’s a decent man and will be a good provider for you and your children.”
Ailia started to protest. She felt like her whole world was spinning, turning upside down and falling apart.
“I expect you to be warm and welcoming when he comes over for repast,” Brander said sternly.
The back of Ailia’s eyes burned and a lump grew in her throat. She knew she didn’t have a choice in whether or not to accept this proposal of marriage. She had to do her duty and honor her aunt and uncle’s decision no matter how she felt. 
“I didn’t get to choose my husband either, but we turned out happy,” Unni said. “It’s the way that it is and it’s for the best.”
Ailia was fuming on the inside, but didn’t know what to say, so she just blurted out the first thing that came to mind. “You aren’t even my real parents.” She regretted saying it immediately, for Unni and Brander had been nothing but kind and loving to her and had treated her like their own daughter. They had really been the only parents she had ever known since her parents had died when she was a baby.
Brander left without another word and Ailia plopped back down in her chair, extremely displeased. 
“Come help me clean up in the kitchen so we can prepare repast,” Unni said.
Ailia stood up and followed Unni into the kitchen. “I’m sorry about what I said about you not being my real parents,” she said hesitantly. “I do consider you my mother and Brander my father.”
“I understand Ailia; believe me, I do. When I found out that my parents were marrying me off to Brander, I considered running away,” Unni said.
“Truly?” Ailia asked surprised. 
“Don’t tell Brander, though. He still doesn’t know.” Unni smiled and then sighed. “I actually despised him. Of all the suitors my parents chose for me, he was the last one I wanted to marry.” She picked up a few bowls and cleaned off the tabletop with a rag.
“Why?”
“He hadn’t been very kind to me and he seemed so haughty,” Unni said.
“Brander?” Ailia thought that was strange, because Brander seemed the most jovial and most approachable man she knew.
“Later, I found out that he was just nervous around me because he liked me so much.” Unni’s eyes crinkled at the edges as she smiled. “Marriage is much more about a partnership than a feeling, Ailia. If you marry this chieftain, you will have a great partnership. We heard that his former wife was very happy and that is what we want for you—to be happy.” She pulled out some onions and salted lamb’s meat, placing both ingredients on the tabletop.
Ailia sighed. “I know what I must do and of course I’ll accept the marriage proposal. I just didn’t think that it would happen so soon.”
“It’s better to marry early. If you wait too long, you will be too set in your ways.” Unni pulled out a knife and started chopping up the meat in perfect little squares. Ailia stripped the onions of their skin, wondering what type of man this Geir was.

*	    *    *

“Ailia, I would like to introduce you to Geir,” Brander said, arriving with the guest a few hours later.
Ailia curtsied. “Welcome to our home. Pleased to meet you,” she said, lowering her gaze. Geir wasn’t bad to look at—average, Ailia would say—with a uniquely slender nose and small pouty lips. He was almost as tall as Brander, but much less muscular, thin even. His beard was a little too long for Ailia’s liking, but maybe, she thought, she could convince him to groom it shorter.
“He will be staying for repast, but then he needs to get back to his children in Solnes,” Brander informed.
Ailia did her best to kill a gasp. “Oh,” she said instead. “How many children?” She suffered a smile.
Unni glanced at Ailia, her eyes stern, telling Ailia to be cautious.
“Four,” Geir said. “All boys. Their ages are ten, eight, six and four. They are a handful and they need a strong mother to keep them from getting into mischief.”
“I see,” Ailia said. She knew immediately that she would never be that woman he was seeking. Ailia saw herself as more of a nurturer, not a commander.
“Shall we?” Unni gestured over to the hearth and they all sat down near the glowing flames. 
After the first awkward meeting with her future husband and after Ailia had filled everyone’s bowl with stew, she sat down next to Unni.
“So, tell us about Solnes,” Unni said.
Geir looked at her and scoffed. “I do not see why that is an important subject to talk about.”
“My apologies,” Unni said. “Of course not.”
“What I want to know, Ailia, is do you feel prepared to be the homemaker of my longhouse and do you think that you are capable of raising my four boys?”
Ailia balked.
“Of course she is,” Unni said.
“I know your opinion, woman. I need to hear it from Ailia’s own mouth,” Geir said.
Ailia froze. Of course she could do it, but it was so very hard to say so out loud, because she knew that in the innermost part of her spirit that she didn’t want to do it. She’d even do anything she could to get out of this arranged marriage. 
“Ailia?” Unni nudged.
“Why, yes, of course,” Ailia said. “I’ve trained my entire life to become the best homemaker I can be.” She felt her spirit revolting inside of her.
“Ailia, one more question and then we can go on to discuss other niceties. Are you able to bear children?” Gier asked.
Brander looked as if he wanted out of the conversation. 
“If you are asking me whether or not I have started my menstruation cycle, then, yes, I have. For a few years now,” Ailia said. 
“Ailia,” Brander said, looking angry.
“I’m sorry, was that not what you were referring to?” Ailia asked. Usually women only spoke about such things to each other, but since Geir was asking so directly, Ailia couldn’t help but be blunt in her response.
“Swell,” Geir said. “I think she’ll do, Brander.”
Brander nodded, looking enormously relieved. 
Ailia hated how Geir said that: I think she’ll do. She wanted her marriage to be so much more that just a partnership of duties and responsibilities. She couldn’t see love being a part of the relationship with Geir. She would have to forget trying to make a connection or even a caring bond with the man. He was probably just looking for a woman to raise his children and a woman to bring to his bed when he needed it. How could Brander have chosen him?  Ailia wondered.

*    *    *

After Geir left, Brander went outside to sharpen some knives and Ailia and Unni stayed inside to clean up the dishes from dinner. 
“I thought he seemed very—,” Unni said. She seemed to struggle with the right words to say. “—honest.”
Ailia rolled her eyes. “Sure,” she said. “Do you think he seemed a little too callous?”
“Well, Brander was that way in the beginning, too. I’m sure things will change once you’re married,” Unni said. “Just make sure you take good care of him in the lovemaking area.”
Ailia’s jaw dropped open in shock. She really didn’t want to talk with Unni about how to please her future husband. Ailia didn’t agree with Unni. Geir didn’t seem like a man who would change. However, she concluded that it wasn’t worth disagreeing with Unni right now. There was nothing Ailia could say or do that would make a difference or change their decision in arranging a marriage for her. Ailia had never wanted to dishonor her family in any way and she wasn’t going to start now. She knew that Unni and Brander had tried their best to find a suitable husband, even though, in her eyes, they had picked a man who she thought she would never love or even like.
“I always knew there was something special about you, Ailia. Maybe this is your fate? To marry a chieftain?  Not many young women accomplish that. You’ll have a great life and many more opportunities than if you had just married a farmer or fisherman,” Unni said.
Ailia nodded. She knew what Unni meant, but she didn’t feel that this path was the path she had been put here in Midgard to take. 


5

Sun Queen

Three months had passed since Lucia’s mother had died and the pain of her loss was just as strong. Unfortunately, Lucia’s father had become even more controlling than ever and his lessons to Lucia had increased in both length and in frequency. She let most of his lectures go in one ear and out the other. Why would she listen to him when she was not planning on ruling like her father in any way?  
Her father wanted her to learn about the Christian faith before her coronation as well. “Tomorrow, when you are crowned queen, you will be able to use the information you obtain about the new religion to your advantage. Know your friends well, but know your enemies intimately,” he had said this morning before she left to attend the weekly church service. Lucia had never been to church before and sitting next to Vilda, amongst commoners, certainly did not make her want to come back again. I wish my father would just burn the Christian church down, she fumed as she sat in the church pew. 
Vilda sat next to her and panted during the whole sermon. Does she ever not pant? Lucia certainly could not remember a time. She brought her hand up to her mouth to hide a yawn and tried to refocus on the sermon.
Bishop Peter, an unusually tall and skinny man with huge eyes and a long pointy nose, spoke from the pulpit.
