“Bergendal has gradually declined into poverty of the mind and the land as grief and fear have stolen the minds and hearts of its people. It is the kind of fear that is always lurking, always present and will never grant peace to anyone who chooses to bear such sorrow. You have all become comfortable with living in fear and pain. Beware, for the longer you live with it, the more likely the pain will become bearable. You will gladly embrace suffering into your hearts and become one with it, barely noticing its toxic, deadly presence. It is everywhere and even some of our leaders have welcomed this dangerous state of mind. We must be watchful, always standing guard at the door of our souls, to not let the Devil enter in with fear and sorrow. Amen.” Bishop Peter outlined the shape of a cross with his right hand and stepped down from the pulpit.
 “Is that Princess Lucia over there?” Lucia heard a middle-aged peasant woman ask a tall burly man. 
“I don’t know. How should I know?” The man replied.
“Yes, I believe it is. I’ve never seen her here. We should go say hello, so she feels welcome.” The slightly plump woman dragged the man over to Lucia. Two guards stood on either side of her.
“Hello and welcome to church, Your Highness. I’m Unni and this is my husband Brander,” Unni said and curtsied deeply. Brander bowed.
Lucia looked at them both and smiled briefly. I do not have time for this, she thought. “Ivar, take me home. I have a meeting with my father and Vilda.” She turned around and headed for the church exit. 
Snow fell from the heavens when they stepped outside. “Away,” Lucia said to the driver, settling into the sleigh. Her two guards were quick to mount their stallions and ride behind the sleigh back to the castle.
“What idiots,” Vilda said. “They think they can approach you like you are a commoner. They have no right to approach you. You are the daughter of a king!”
“Do not worry yourself, Vilda,” Lucia said. She had already forgotten about them. She peered over at Vilda and thought her aunt had become even heavier than she was before. Yesterday, she had overheard the handmaidens say that usually they estimated they needed to use four times as much fabric when they sewed a dress for Vilda than for anyone else, but that this time, even that had not been enough for her ever-expanding body. She muffled a laugh. My poor, fat aunt. Usually, women who were larger were looked up to, but Vilda was just so fat and so ugly that Lucia sometimes could not even stand to look at her. Lately, it seemed as though Vilda was trying to take her mother’s place by comforting Lucia. Lucia was not going to allow Vilda into her heart for even a heartbeat.
“But they did not even acknowledge me!” Vilda’s plump cheeks had turned red from the cold air. “I am the king’s sister. They should at least show me some respect.”
“You always say that, Vilda,” Lucia said. “Do not worry. When I am queen, I will give you all the respect you deserve.”
“Praise the gods! That would certainly be a nice change around here.” Vilda blew her nose in her linen handkerchief. “Your father certainly does not give me the respect I deserve.”
“He does not give anyone respect,” Lucia said. Not even me, she thought.
When they arrived at the castle, Lucia and Vilda dined together in the great hall and then entered the strategy room where King Olav and his Grand Marshal were waiting for them. Shelves filled with books lined two of the walls, and a red and yellow stained-glass window let the afternoon light in, illuminating the opposite, stone wall.
“How was the Christian church service?” King Olav asked as they entered.
Lucia thought she spotted a mischievous smile on her father’s lips. “It was—interesting,” Lucia answered. “Do you know that they believe there is an evil man called the Devil and that he forces humans to do wicked things?” She chuckled. “Who would be so gullible and believe such a thing? Seems like they are looking for someone else to blame for their mistakes.”
“Hmm,” Olav replied, not seeming to pay attention to what Lucia had to say.
“Oh, Olav. I shall never return again,” Vilda said passionately. “Please do not ask me to. You might be wise to get rid of Bishop Peter. He mentioned you in his sermon, saying that you have given into your fears and are leading these people astray with your poverty of mind and poverty of land. He even called you a Devil!” Vilda’s voice sounded unusually dramatic, even for her.
He did not. Were we listening to the same sermon?  Lucia wondered. 
“Well, never mind him, I have more important matters to attend,” Olav said.
Vilda huffed and looked at Lucia as if to say, See?
Olav sat down around the oval table first. “Vilda, my beloved and trusted sister, come here and have a seat by me,” Olav said. Lucia and the Grand Marshall also sat down around the table. “Ever since I took on the crown and responsibilities of the Northlandic Kingdom, you have served me and the country with fervor, granting freedom and fairness to all. I, like many others, am grateful for your continued and faithful service.”
“It has been my honor and pleasure, dear Brother, to serve where I am needed. I am always at your, and the Kingdom’s, humble service,” Vilda said. “I respect you and the Kingdom of Bergendal and I would protect it with my life, if needed. But, as I frequently say, there is always room for improvement.” She rose and walked around the table.
Olav nodded. “Yes, that is what I suspected,” he mumbled. “So, now I will ask something more of you,” he said, his eyes following her where she went.
“I am speechless. More? It is well needed, of course. Anything, Brother, anything at all.” Vilda’s eyes lit up at the mention of an opportunity for promotion. 
What is my father trying to accomplish?  Lucia thought. Lucia did not think her father liked his older sister very much. He always complained about her being too overbearing. 
“Vilda, I ask that you take the position as mistress of the Hammersten Fortress,” Olav said.
Vilda stiffened where she stood. She turned to look at Olav and placed her hands on her gigantic hips with a huff.
Here she goes, Lucia thought.
“Are you trying to get rid of me?” Vilda asked. 
“I beg your pardon?” Olav said. “I am giving you more responsibility, promoting you even and you ask me if I am trying to get rid of you?”
“Hammersten is the northernmost fortress in all of the Northlandic Kingdom and you want to send me there?” Vilda said.
“It is also the most important fortress in the Northlandic Kingdom. If we lose that one, we will lose everything to the Viking invasions,” Olav said.
Vilda folded her arms in front of her chest and huffed. “Who else will be there?” she asked.
“All your handmaidens will go with you and you will have the constant protection of my guards. I will compensate you generously,” Olav said.	
“How long?” Vilda asked. 
“It is only for one year and then Ivar will replace you,” Olav said.
Vilda squinted her eyes. “Why not send Ivar there now?”
“He needs to complete another assignment for me here,” Olav said.
“Which is—?” Vilda prodded.
“Which is none of your concern!” Olav shouted.
Vilda looked upset. “Must I give you my answer now?”
“Not necessarily, but the sooner the better.” Olav said.
Vilda huffed. “Will there be a coronation of some sort? An official event or festivity in my honor for the people of Bergendal, so they might learn of my new assignment?” Vilda asked.
Lucia thought that a celebration sounded fun. The one thing, and probably the only thing, she did like about her aunt was how she always found ways to celebrate life, or specifically, her own life.
“Do as you please,” Olav replied. “So, is that a yes?”
Vilda nodded and snorted a laugh. “Oh, excuse me,” she said.
“Good. Now go, leave me be,” he said, waving his hand. “I will have you leave within the week.”
Normally, Vilda would be bothered with such a short dismissal, Lucia thought, but she seemed to have mellowed over the past few years. She thought her father was being very creative in getting rid of Vilda. But still, something did not seem quite right. Lucia thought Vilda had accepted the assignment too quickly.
Vilda waddled out of the room, panting heavily as always.
Olav stood up and looked at two of the guards who loyally served him. “Lucia,” he said. “Come here. I want to teach you a lesson.”
Lucia approached her father. Not another lesson. The thought of it sounded exhausting.
“You, come here,” Olav said, pointing to the guard closest to him.
The guard paraded over.
“Do you consider yourself a loyal individual?” Olav asked.
“Yes, Your Majesty,” he replied, his face sober, his eyes fixed forward.
“How loyal?” Olav asked.
“Not even through my own death will I relieve myself of serving and guarding King Olav, ruler of the Northlandic Kingdom,” he replied, quoting the oath he had committed to when he had been sworn in to service.
“Is that so?” Olav asked, pacing around him. “What if I killed the guard standing next to you without cause or for entertainment and my own amusement?  Would you still serve me loyally?”
“Yes, Your Highness,” the guard replied with a nod and with no hesitation in his demeanor.
“And what if I killed your friends?”
The guard’s eyes flinched. “Yes, Your Highness, loyally forevermore,” he said and bowed his head.
Olav was right in the guard’s face now, glaring him in the eyes. “And what if I killed your family and burned down your family’s farm?”
The guard stood speechless.
“No? Is that the limit of your loyalty?” Olav asked, pacing around him again, his hands clasped behind his back. “And what if I did not pay you? Would you lose your loyalty to me then?”
“No,” the guard said.
Lucia could see the guard’s forehead starting to bead with sweat. He was obviously lying.
“Your family—” Olav said, staring into the guard’s eyes again. “I see how it is. Everyone, leave me be. Lucia, come here.” He walked her across the hallway in into the throne room. Then he ascended the steps and sat down on his throne.	
“Father?” Lucia asked, still standing at the bottom of the steps, sensing his disgust.
“No one remains loyal under all circumstances,” Olav said to Lucia. “Trust no man or woman completely, for they will live to fail you. Remember that!”
“Yes, Father,” Lucia said. “ I am sure not everyone—”
“No man or woman!” he repeated.
“Not even you?” Lucia pressed. She smiled. Certainly he cannot be speaking of himself, she thought triumphantly. He did not answer. Lucia did not know whether he did not want to answer the question, or if his mind had wandered to other thoughts, but it disturbed her that he remained silent.
“Now, go get fitted for your first ceremonial dress,” Olav finally said.
Does he mean himself as well, she continued to wonder, more seriously, on her way out of the throne room. On her way to the Queen’s Chambers, Nora joined her. 
“So, you must be excited that the time has finally come for you to be crowned queen of the Northlandic Kingdom,” Nora said.
Lucia nodded. She was indeed excited to have reached the age of eighteen. It was the age when all heirs of the Aesira bloodline took the throne. Now she could rule and the days under her controlling father would be over. Nora helped Lucia slip into the unfinished dress. Nora and another handmaiden, Torill, worked on the dress for hours while Lucia stood there wearing it. 
“Almost finished, dear!” Nora said, her old wrinkled hands, sewing on the skirt hem. She was the best seamstress in the entire kingdom, it had been said, and even though she did not sew much anymore, due to the pain in her slow-moving hands, she had insisted on being this dress’s maker. “Do you think you are ready?”
“I do not know. I do not feel any different than before.” Lucia exhaled. “I am still the same me.” 
“Oh, you will never feel very different, trust me. I still feel like I am eighteen years old on the inside, even though it looks like I am one hundred on the outside.” Nora laughed and then pulled another needle out of her mouth and pinned it to the skirt hem. “We are not as sturdy as the walls that make up the castle, neither on the outside or on the inside.” The castle had been built over twelve hundred years ago, when the first royal family had established the Northlandic Kingdom with Iluxia. 
“Mother said that Eiess had tried to seize our castle once before, but that she was not strong enough yet and failed at her attempt. Is that true?”
“True, true—,” Nora said and nodded her small head. 
A few hundred years ago, Eiess had descended from another realm to Midgard and had vowed to take over the Kingdom, destroying what Iluxia had created. 
“Eiess vowed not to return until her strength had grown so powerful that she could vanquish everyone within these walls with one move.”
“How does she get stronger?” Lucia wondered. It seemed strange that someone as old as Eiess kept growing stronger each year. Lucia just thought old people were weak and frail.
“I do not know for certain, but I have heard that she grows more powerful every winter, amassing strength every dark season. She feeds off of darkness,” Nora said, sewing on the dress now. “Just as we feed off of food and water, I suppose.”
“My father once told me that Eiess longs for the day when she will rule completely in a never-ending state of darkness. I never understood why she would want to live in a state of darkness, but it makes sense if darkness is the source of her power,” Lucia agreed.
“I have even heard that she has tried to suspend the seasons on the winter solstice before,” Nora said. She sewed a few more stitches and stopped to look up at Lucia.
“That is impossible though, is it not? Suspending one day is impossible, even for her,” Lucia said. “I think Eiess may be confused. Maybe she thinks she is more powerful than she really is. The gods would never allow anyone to become more powerful than them.”
“I have no answer for you, dear. It seems impossible to me, too. There!” Nora said proudly. “Your dress is finished!”  She took a few small steps back and studied it. “You look so much like your mother did nearly thirty years ago when she was crowned the Sun Queen. Your mother would have been so proud of you if she had been here today.” She became teary-eyed. “Now this is a big day for you, love, I want to make sure you look and feel your absolute best on the day of your coronation. Torill, please come pick up the extra material and clean up here,” Nora said, without taking her eyes off the dress. 
“Yes, my lady.” Torill curtsied. She quickly whisked up all the extra material and threads and left the room.    
“Now turn around and look at yourself in the mirror,” Nora said, gesturing Lucia to look in the mirrors.
Lucia turned around slowly and gracefully and walked to the three full-length gold-plated mirrors in her room. She hardly recognized the woman looking back at her. As a young girl, she had always let her hair hang loosely down around her narrow shoulders. Now, her hair was up in an intricately braided up-do and decorated with golden lace and diamonds. With her coming of age and taking on her responsibility as the Sun Queen, she needed to step into her adult self. That step also included dressing, presenting and speaking as a Queen. I must put aside my child-like self, even if it only shows on the outside right now, she thought as she admired herself.
Lucia continued to watch her image in the mirror as she slowly turned around to examine the back of the dress. Her dress was made of pure yellow silk with embroidered gold stars, suns and sunrays. The drop-waist dress hugged her tiny waistline. It had soft puff sleeves of off-white silk and golden inserts. The sleeves were long and flared all the way to the floor with gold embroidering at the ends. Her neckline was not the typical rounded style, but a V-neck with a regal standing collar. The dress had a cape that was made of pure chiffon and was embellished with gold stitching throughout with a sunray pattern beaming from her shoulders and cascading down the cape. Her skirt was long and had a three-foot train in the back. The back of the dress had crisscross lacing from top to bottom, ending in a three-tiered bustle that looked like sun spokes. 
“Here,” Nora said, placing the solstice crown on Lucia’s head. 
For the first time, as Lucia looked in the mirror, she saw a beautiful lady standing in front of her, instead of a young girl. She smiled contentedly, feeling as though she was leaving the young and innocent girl inside of her behind. I am ready to be the Sun Queen, she marveled. She knew she had no idea everything that being the Sun Queen entailed, but felt confident that she would be able to handle whatever would come her way.
“I see a beautiful lady where I once saw a young girl,” Olav complimented, entering the room. 
“My, how time flies, Your Majesty. I remember your Queen standing in that very spot at what seems but a moment ago. And now, your beautiful daughter is standing there on the eve before she turns eighteen,” Nora said in a humbled voice. 
Olav embraced his daughter and they both looked into the mirror. “What a Queen you will be,” he said tenderly.
Lucia thought he seemed suspiciously kind. What does he want from me?
“Nora, will you please excuse us for a moment?” Olav asked.
“Yes, Your Majesty.” Nora curtsied and left the room. 
Here we go, Lucia thought. More stupid lessons.
“Lucia, I have something very important to share with you before your coronation. I have waited many years to tell you this,” Olav confessed. “But, before I begin, there are a few things that I must say to you.” He paused. “What I share with you today is for the good of my people whom I have sworn to protect. From now on, you will have to give up the life you want, to serve the people you lead.” He looked her squarely in the eyes. 
When I am Queen, I can do as I please, Lucia thought, feeling annoyed that he would want to control her.
“Today you will understand why I have been so hard on you all these years,” Olav said with a sigh. 
Lucia was surprised by his candor. “What do you mean?” she asked. He had never really opened up to her before about anything.
“Just listen,” Olav said, quite impatiently. “There are many things you do not know.”
He always tries to degrade me and make me feel like I am insignificant compared to him, Lucia thought. 
“You know I have told you before that you would be the one to lead the battle to end all battles—the battle of Ragnarok?” Olav asked.
“Yes,” Lucia said.
“Tomorrow, at your coronation, I will reveal to you all the details pertaining to Ragnarok. There are many, many things you do not yet know,” Olav said.
Yes, you have already made that very clear, Lucia thought. 
“Starting tomorrow, Iluxia will guide you and prepare you for the final days before the battle of Ragnarok. Being the leader of the lightelves in Alvheim, he knows many things that have happened and of many things that will come to pass.”
“Do you know these things?” Lucia asked, trying not to sound annoyed. 
“No, I do not.”
Lucia thought it was the first time her father had ever admitted to not knowing something.
Olav clasped his hands behind his back and began pacing around Lucia. “Your mother knew many more things than me, because she was the Sun Queen. Being the Sun Queen means you will be privileged to receive sacred information from the past and quite possibly of the future. Iluxia will share this information with you. He is able to see certain key moments that will have and have had eternal significances on Midgard,” he said. 
“How?” Lucia asked.
“I am not exactly sure how he does it, but he explained to me once that such moments create vibrations and those vibrations travel through time. Only those who are in tune with these vibrations will be able to hear them and actually see them. Iluxia has spent thousands of years perfecting his senses and now he can see that which none other can,” Olav said. “But I doubt a mere mortal—such as yourself—would be able to ever achieve such a thing.” 
Lucia breathed deeply, taking in all the information her father was telling her. She turned around to admire her reflection in the mirror again. I want to achieve that which he thinks I cannot. She looked at her father again. “I must admit, I am a little anxious to meet Iluxia.”
“Do not worry. I know Iluxia seems like such a grand being that one would have reason to believe he would possess an air of superiority. Iluxia is like no other being you will ever meet, for he is not a mere mortal like you and me. However, you will feel completely at ease in his presence,” Olav said.
“Truly?” Lucia asked. I wish I were more than a mere mortal. Maybe I am? She felt a strange connection with Iluxia already.
“Yes, and Iluxia has the unique ability to lift you to a whole other level of existence with his words and his presence. You will leave him a better person than when you came to him, just having been in his presence. I cannot even explain it. It is something that must be experienced,” Olav said. “I have only met him once, but the memory of our meeting is always in my mind.” 
Lucia did not know what to think, but her father’s comments did help put her mind at ease. I wish my father was always this easy to talk to, she thought.
“Having said that, a dark, perilous time is before us and before you, darker than has ever been experienced in the history of Midgard,” Olav said gravely, as his countenance fell before Lucia’s eyes. “On the eve of the winter solstice, you shall receive the Aesira Jewel.”
“What is the Aesira Jewel?” Lucia wondered aloud. She remembered her mother mentioning it on her deathbed.
“It is a sacred season-shifting jewel,” Olav said.
“Season-shifting?” Lucia thought about it for a moment. “Can the Aesira Jewel actually shift seasons?”
“Yes,” Olav said. “It is what controls the seasons. If it is not used, the seasons will suspend at either winter solstice or summer solstice. Only a King or Queen of the Aesira bloodline has the power to engage the Jewel.”
Lucia gasped. “So that is why Eiess wants to take over the Northlandic Kingdom,” she said. The one who possesses the Aesira Jewel, possesses the powers to control the seasons. She felt the hair on her back stand up. “But, Eiess is not of the Aesira bloodline, is she?”
“No, you are correct. She cannot engage the Aesira Jewel. But she could prevent the Aesira Jewel from being engaged,” Olav said. 
“Then the seasons would suspend,” Lucia said. “That would be horrible.”
“This is where your responsibility lies. Your mother was the Aesira Jewel’s keeper until she died. Now you will be its new keeper and ring in the seasons,” Olav said. “Tomorrow, on winter solstice eve, Iluxia will show you how to perform the season-shifting ceremony at Solhenge.”
Lucia’s eyes widened as her mouth fell open. I will change the seasons, she thought, I will meet Iluxia tomorrow. She had started to fall in love with the idea of being the new Sun Queen.
 “The Aesira Jewel rests for the time being behind the mural, over there,” Olav said, pointing to the full-size painting of him. “You must swear to tell no one of these things.”
Lucia nodded.
“There is more thing,” Olav said.
Lucia thought she would burst from all the new information.
“Before your mother gave birth to you, Iluxia came to her and told her that—” Olav paused. “—you are the Great Sentinor pre-destined to defeat Eiess.” He paused and his eyes welled up with tears. 
Lucia was gripped by his sudden emotion. He had told her this before, but it was different now with all the new information he had just shared with her.
“Your mother and I swore to protect the Great Sentinor with all that we had, including our own lives. She has lived twice before, but Eiess has managed to kill her both times. This is her last life in Midgard,” Olav said. 
Lucia was puzzled by his story. I am the Great Sentinor, right? The way he spoke about the Great Sentinor though seemed to vacillate between her and another person.
“She—you were given three lives by Iluxia and the gods to defeat the Empress. Do you understand? This is your last life. If you do not succeed, Eiess will rule Midgard until she finally destroys it with darkness and dearth. All of humanity is at stake.”
“I—I do not know what to think. All of this is very overwhelming,” Lucia said.
“This is why I have been so hard on you. You have no time to waver, even for a moment. Once you are crowned queen, Eiess will know and she will do everything in her power to destroy you.”
Lucia nodded hesitantly, but she still felt confused and overcome. 
“With the gift—” Olav continued, “—of the Aesira Jewel, the Great Sentinor shall also receive the power and force to eliminate Eiess. You will be responsible for making sure this happens.”
“Did I receive this gift—before, in my—previous lives?” Lucia asked, feeling strange talking about her previous lives.
“Yes, the Great Sentinor did,” Olav said slowly and clearly.
“But she, I mean, I failed before,” Lucia blurted out. The reality of the heavy responsibility suddenly felt like too much to bear. 
“Yes, there is always the risk of failure. Nothing is guaranteed,” Olav said.
Lucia breathed heavily. Eiess will come after me. That thought sent her spinning.
“Just remember to take it one day at a time. More will be revealed when you receive the Aesira Jewel. Get ready for bed and get some rest. You will need plenty of rest to prepare for your ceremony.” 
“How can I go to sleep when I know Eiess might be coming for me?” Lucia asked.
“Fear will incapacitate you. Faith will empower you. You always have a choice,” Olav said.
Lucia had a million questions she wanted answered but she dared not ask them now. She did not know if she wanted to know more. Ignorance seemed better.
“What about Soren? Where does he fit in all of this?” Lucia asked.
“He is also a Sentinor and he was sent to Midgard to help the Great Sentinor defeat Eiess. They were also Spiritus Amors, soul mates, before they entered Midgard.
Then why do I not feel a connection with him? Lucia wondered. Maybe I have not tried hard enough to love him.
Olav stepped over to her, cradled her head between his hands and kissed her on the forehead. “Remember, I love you so much.”
It was the first time Lucia had heard those words. She questioned their truthfulness.
“You have been burdened with a heavy responsibility. I wish I could carry it for you and if there were any way that I could, I would give my life so that you would not have to put yours in danger.” Olav looked into her eyes and then hugged her tightly. “Good night.” 
As she watched her father leave the room, Lucia felt petrified about all the new knowledge she had just received. How could she be the Great Sentinor destined to destroy Eiess? She had heard stories about the Empress in which she had decimated a whole house with a flick of her wrist. What powers did Lucia possess? Lucia did not know. She felt like she was meant to be a queen but not the Great Sentinor that her father had told her she was. 
Nora returned to help Lucia get ready for bed. “Why do you look so gloomy?” she asked while undressing Lucia.
“There is so much I do not understand,” Lucia said.
“Time will answer all mysteries,” Nora said, helping her into her night tunic. “You will learn what you need when the time is at hand. You do not need to know everything now, just that you have within you the ability to reach further than you ever imagined. Your father loves you very much and your mother, bless her resting soul, loved you more than her own life. Everything you need to know will reveal itself to you; here a little, there a little.”
“I just feel like too much is expected of me,” Lucia protested. Lucia did not know how much Nora knew about what her father had revealed. Probably nothing. Lucia started to feel angry. Her father had kept valuable information from her and she did not like it.
“Listen to your heart. What it tells you is the right thing,” Nora said.
Lucia thought that sounded like good advice. “At least I know I am supposed to marry Soren,” Lucia said, sitting down on the bed.
“Soren is a very good man and a great match for you,” Nora agreed.
“When you got married, were you excited?” Lucia asked.
“Oh heavens, yes! My husband was not such a noble man, like Soren, of course, but he was simple and sweet and a hard worker. I loved him very much.”
“I am glad my parents chose Soren for me. At least I know I will have him in the future.”
“He will take good care of you,” Nora said.
“I care for him and he has tried very hard to earn my affection, but I do not know that I am in love with him yet. There is not really any passion between us,” Lucia said.
“Well, you are younger than he and he seems like a man who would not take advantage of a young girl. Give it time, dear. The love will come,” Nora said. “Remember, passion has a quick burn. Sometimes, another type of love grows, a love that is steadier and deeper—a love that is lasting. Off to bed you go,” she said, gently nudging Lucia under the covers.
Lucia forced a smile. As she sunk into bed, her mind continued to race. Her thoughts wandered back to her immense responsibility again and she felt overwhelmed about all the new information she had received. Finally, she drifted off to sleep and in her dreams, was taken to a place she had never been before. 
She found herself by a beautiful crystal blue ocean. The sand was sparkling white and she could feel the soft, fine sand between her bare toes. She felt warm and the beach was misty but bright. Over by the grass, there was a partially buried wagon that was missing one wheel. There were three yellow and blue longships on the water with red and white sails. Farther inland, she could see houses and buildings.
In the distance, she saw a person dressed in white walking toward her. As she came closer, she saw that the person looked a lot like her but older. “Mother!  Mother!” she yelled. Maud looked beautiful, radiant and healthy. Lucia ran to her and leapt into her arms. They stood there for some time holding each other. Lucia soaked up her presence and her love. “I will never let you go again,” she said.
“Lucia,” her mother said as she released her from her embrace. “Come with me. I need to show you something important.” 
“Oh, Mother,” Lucia cried. “How I have missed you! I thought I would never see you again. I was so worried I would not.”
“I have missed you, too. But there is little time, Lucia. Now, come.” Maud took Lucia’s hand and started walking down the beach with her.
Lucia walked beside her in silence, holding her hand tightly. It felt so good to have her mother near again and she felt safe and whole again as if a vital lost part of her had been restored. 
Then Maud stopped. She raised her right hand and pointed down at the sand. “There, Lucia, there,” she said. 
Lucia became confused and asked, “What do you mean?” 
“There,” Maud said again, with her finger still pointing down. “The time will soon come when you will need to attain this key. When you do, a whole new understanding of who you are will be revealed to you. You are not just the soon-to-be Queen, my love; remember, you were created to help bring to pass many important things!” 
“I do not understand, Mother,” Lucia said discouraged. “Father told me this too, but I do not feel it in my heart.” She had always felt she could be open with her mother.
“There is another with whom you must be reunited, one who you cannot do without. I cannot tell you who that person is now, for Eiess will surely know if I tell you. Seek this person out and by the grace of the Heavens, together you will conquer Eiess. Behold the key and you will understand and know who you are and what your mission is here in Midgard.”
“Is it my betrothed?” Lucia asked.
 Maud started to slowly fade away before Lucia’s eyes. Her face and hands were vanishing, blending with the misty air, disappearing into nothingness.
“Mother, do not go. Please, stay. I will do anything, anything you want to have you stay. Please, Mother, please!” Lucia said, trying to grasp her mother’s vanishing hand.
“Remember what I have told you and you will understand who you are. You will be tempted to betray your family, Lucia.” Now Maud was barely visible, but her voice was still strong. “There, look, there,” Maud persisted, pointing into the sand. The wind started blowing and Maud’s voice was whisked away with it. “Look there—there—” 
Suddenly, Lucia was alone again. Tears brimmed in her eyes. “Mother!” she gasped, crossing her arms in front of her chest trying to embrace her shattered heart. All the days and nights of hurt, of despair and fear, came down on her all at once. Lucia fell to the sand and wrapped her arms around her knees. She buried her head down into her arms and wept for what seemed an eternity. What did my mother say to me? Who do I need to seek out?  What had her mother meant?  Something must be hidden in the sand. Lucia stood up and looked around. 
Suddenly, flames erupted on the sea’s surface. Blazes licked the blues of the sea, sending smoke to the skies. The flames came closer to the beach where Lucia stood, threatening to spread onto the sand. Then, Lucia saw something small and shiny that looked triangular in the sand. She was about to go over and pick it up, but she froze when she heard her name called again. 
“Lucia,” someone shouted in the distance. “Lucia,” the voice said, summoning Lucia again, this time with more force. 
Lucia looked up and squinted her eyes. In the sea of fire, she saw a woman walking toward her. She did not seem to be affected by the flames that she walked through to reach Lucia. She was dressed in a black linen dress. By the way she held herself and the way she dressed, it seemed the woman was of noble birth. Most of her face was covered with a dark veil, so only her green eyes were visible. When the woman reached the sand, she floated toward Lucia. “Come, follow me,” she said in a deep voice. 
Lucia felt uncomfortable in the woman’s presence but she followed her as there was nowhere else to go. She also decided not to investigate the shining piece of metal, not wanting to draw attention to it in case it might be something important. As they walked, the sky grew darker and darker, growing black from the smoke. The clouds started churning in the heavens and swallowed the light from the day into itself.
“Where are we going?” Lucia asked.
“Just follow me,” the woman answered.
“Who are you?” Lucia dug further.
“Why, I am your destroyer. Do you not remember me?” she sneered sarcastically. 
Lucia stopped dead in her tracks. 
The woman turned around. “This is my reign,” she said. Without warning, she ripped the veil off her face and flung it into the flames that now surrounded them. She was a beautiful woman, young-looking at first glance, but with hateful old eyes. “Tell me what your mother said!” she screamed. 
“My mother?” Lucia asked. “My mother is dead.” 
“Do not play games with me. You do not know who you are dealing with right now. Do not lie. Tell me the truth about what your mother said, or you will never see your father again!” she yelled.
Lucia wanted to run away and wanted nothing to do with this woman.
“You will fail! You will die! This is my rule and reign and I can take your life away in a heartbeat, just as I did in your lifetimes before!” the woman raged.
Lucia took off running as fast as she could but, though her legs were sprinting, she was barely moving away from her enemy. 
The woman started laughing. “You will never escape me, do you not see? I am the destroyer—your destroyer!”
Lucia awoke suddenly by a noise. Her whole body shuddered. As she tried to shake the mare dream, she got out of bed and looked out the window over the country of which she was soon to be the queen. The Northern Star had begun to shine more brightly over the land, as it did each year on winter solstice eve. A sudden feeling of ill-omen in Lucia’s soul caught her attention. She could not describe what was happening, but she could feel Midgard starting to swing toward and prepare for a perilous and dark season. What had my mother tried to show me in my dream? Was it part of what I am feeling now? Lucia knew she had to find the person of which her mother spoke. 
 Nora rushed into Lucia’s chambers, looking troubled and out of breath. “Did you hear the noise?” she asked. Without waiting for an answer, she dashed into Lucia’s wardrobe and started rifling through her clothes. 
Lucia turned around and watched her old, familiar handmaiden. She had become like a second mother to her since her own mother had passed away. Nora was always there, especially when Lucia needed her most. “Yes, it woke me up. Is something wrong?” 
“Quickly, quietly come with me,” Nora whispered. “Your father has asked me to take care of you and safely take you to a sleigh that has been prepared for you outside.” 
Lucia walked toward her and Nora threw Lucia’s white rabbit fur coat around Lucia’s shoulders. As she lifted a torch off the wall, Nora said, “Your father has ordered that you leave the castle immediately.” Her voice sounded urgent.
“Why? What is happening?” Lucia asked. “Where is my father?”
“No time to talk. The castle will soon be under attack,” Nora said and they started down the hallway. The torch lit up the blackened corridor and they could see a few feet in front of them. Finally, they reached the northeastern stairwell and started running down the steps. “Your father told me that Eiess is on her way here to capture you and possibly kill you,” Nora said. She looked more terrified than Lucia had ever seen her. “She believes you are the Great Sentinor and wants to kill you.”
“What?” Lucia said. A shock waved through her body. She felt betrayed by her father. Why had he sent someone else when her life was in danger? He should have come himself.
“Dear, there is no time to discuss or think about these things now. If we do not act immediately and get you out of here, Eiess will be here and we will all be killed.”
Lucia’s blood rushed to her face. She could not breathe. She could not talk. All she could think was that she could be dead by morning.


6

Mare Demons 

Today I will die. 
Ailia was back in her mare dream and there was nothing she could do to stop it.
She carefully peeled back the distressed linen curtain and glanced out the window. A black smoke trail followed the Surtorians, the Empress of Darkness’s agents. “They’re almost here!  You must leave!  Leave now, with Freydis before they come!” Ailia yelled to her mother, Edna. Ailia’s heart hammered like thunder, threatening to burst through her chest. “Oh, may the gods be merciful today!” she said. 
Ailia wanted to wake up, but she couldn’t.
The Empress’s longships had arrived down at the harbor right after dawn and everyone in the Northlandic city knew her intentions: burn the city down until she found the one she was looking for. Yet, no one in Whiteheim seemed to know exactly who that someone was – no one, except for Ailia and her family. 
Why is she after me? Ailia couldn’t remember. 
Dense smoke and flying ashes filled the spring sky and it smelled of burnt wood and flesh. The Empress had come on the one morning Ailia’s husband was gone. Did she plan it that way? Ailia wondered. What’s my husband’s name? Am I losing my mind? No, I’m just dreaming!
“No, I will not leave you, not now, not ever. Do you understand?” Edna grabbed Ailia by the shoulders and shook her. “Freydis needs her mother, your husband needs his wife and I need my daughter.” Her swollen almond eyes searched for Ailia’s gaze. “Come with us. Please, my love, come with us. I beg you!” 
Ailia had never seen her mother so distraught. “It’s me the Empress is after. If she has me, she will put an end to the Surtorians’ slaughter and thousands of lives will be spared.” She twisted out of her mother’s grip. The last time the Empress had been on Ailia’s heels was three months ago and they needed to uproot again and relocate to Whiteheim so they could gain more time, but time to do what? Something did not seem right and the untimely loss of her memory petrified Ailia.
Suddenly, a clamoring crowd rushed past their window, followed by a mob of Surtorians. Death would swallow her victims whole, whether or not those victims were ready for death. Ailia could stop the bloodshed.
Ailia cringed and closed the ashen shutters. “Please, Mother. If the Surtorians find you and Freydis, they won’t hesitate to kill both of you as well as me.” Ailia was tired of running from the Empress of Darkness. Eiess haunted Ailia’s dreams at night and made each day a living mare dream and she would not stop hunting Ailia until she had killed Ailia. If she surrendered now, Ailia would still have one more life to use to outmaneuver and destroy the Empress. If only we had better concealed my identity, she would never have known where to find me, Ailia lamented. “I know how afraid you must be, Mother, but please, for the sake of Freydis, leave now,” she said.
The plea came too late. A Surtorian kicked in the door and entered the dim house, lifting his great sword at Ailia. “I knew I would find you here,” he said, his voice rough and threatening. A grim smile appeared on the monster’s crusty lips. Bright red blood dripped from the greatsword’s massive blade—the blood of who knew how many innocent lives. 
The three-year-old Freydis screamed and ran over to her mother, hiding behind her skirt. 
“Come with me now and I will spare the life of the snot child and the old hag,” the Surtorian barked. He pushed over a table and chair, backing Ailia and Freydis up into a corner of the house by the spinning wheel.
Ailia could see that blood spotted his long, red beard as well. She looked at her mother and back at the Surtorian. His offer to spare Freydis and Edna was more than generous coming from a slayer who was trained to kill humans mercilessly and Ailia knew it. “Do I have your word?” she asked, staring him in the eyes.
“Yes,” the Surtorian said. He lowered his sword and rested both hands on the hilt.
I have no other choice but to believe him. Ailia hunched down and looked her daughter tenderly in the eyes. “You are brave, my love, so very brave. Remember, Mamma loves you. Be good for Bestmother,” she said, hoping the words would bring courage to her daughter and herself. She stroked Freydis’s golden hair away from her face and kissed her on the forehead. There is nothing more beautiful than the innocence of a child, she thought sadly. Ailia glanced at her mother and nodded.
“Freydis, come here, my love. Let’s go into the other room and play,” Edna said, holding her arms open to receive the child. Tears ran down Edna’s cheeks.
Freydis smiled, running over to her bestmother and jumping into her arms. 
The moment is bittersweet, Ailia thought: bitter because she would not live to raise her child, sweet because her child’s life had been spared. She stood up and nodded again to Edna, signaling her to leave. “Please, please, don’t let her forget me,” Ailia said softly as tears flooded her eyes. Oh, the things I would do to have just a few more days with my family, with my husband. 
A cry escaped Edna’s lips and she turned to face her daughter again. “I will always speak of you so she will remember you. I will let her know who you were,” she said. 
“Enough! Before I change my mind!” The Surtorian yelled and rushed toward the women, his creaking black leather armor smelling of sweat and the sea.
Freydis buried her head into her bestmother’s chest. With the child clinging to her body, Edna disappeared into the back room.
Ailia lifted a scroll out of her pocket and placed it next to the hearth before stepping forward to surrender. She had written the scroll earlier that same morning when she had decided to turn herself in to Eiess. She knew for certain she had lost the battle with the Empress of darkness again.
“What do you think you are doing?” the Surtorian roared.
“Leaving a letter for my husband. He’s not here today. Surely you can give me that courtesy?” Ailia held her arms out front so he could tie them. She tried to read the writing on the outside of the scroll. It must be my husband’s name, but I cannot read it.
The Surtorian grabbed the scroll and crumpled it up in his leather-covered fist, throwing it toward the flames. Missing the flames, the scroll thumped heavily to the mud-packed floor instead. He seized Ailia and tied her arms behind her back, before dragging her out into the summer sun. The smell of smoke flooded her nostrils. 
Several Surtorians gathered around Ailia. “Look who I found!” her captor yelled. “The woman our Empress calls ‘Light’!”
Light? Ailia thought.
The Surtorians closed in on her and tugged her from side to side, nearly ripping off her clothes.
“Get back, fools! The Empress has instructed us to bring her back untouched, unharmed,” the Surtorian growled at the others. “Get back!” He lifted his great sword toward them and sliced through the air. They withdrew their filthy hands to keep them from getting chopped off with the sword. 
A lure sounded from the main longship, beckoning all Surtorians to return to the ship. The mission was complete. The lure’s deafening sound competed with the warning bells from the Whiteheim bell tower. Both worked in unison to instill fear into all the people of Whiteheim. 
 “Can you swim, little fish?” The Surtorian grinned, nearly spitting in Ailia’s face. He pulled her along, kicking up the gravel from the road as he strode back toward Eiess’s longships. 
“Yes,” Ailia lied—she could not swim. Ailia twisted around so she could see Freydis and her mother in the window. “I don’t know when I’ll return, but never lose hope that I’ll be back!” she called back toward them. Unable to bear seeing her mother’s forlorn face or her daughter’s longing eyes in the window, she turned back to face the fjord. “Mamma! Mamma!” she heard Freydis cry. At least my child is safe now, Ailia thought. That is all that matters. She could see that the Empress was flying four crescent-moon ensigns on her massive longship that she had appropriated from the late King Harald of Trollsoe.
“I doubt you will be able to swim for very long with weights tied to your feet,” the Surtorian said, jerking her forward.
So they’ll drown me, Ailia thought.
The other Surtorians roared with laughter, clanking and drumming their swords and shields together, as they marched in perfect rhythm to the beat of death—her death. 
They reached the city port quickly. Ailia noticed a slough of seagulls squawking as they flew in circles above the longship. They seemed disturbed—or obsessed—plunging into the sea, appearing to disappear in its depths as if they were drowning themselves. The splashing sound of the waves and the fragrance of the sea rode in on the shifting winds as the breeze excited the longship’s black sails.
The Surtorian pushed Ailia onto the longship and led her below deck. There, in the dimness, a young woman sat on a black and gold throne. Her intense green eyes fixed on Ailia immediately.
“I am so glad I found you again. Welcome to my reign,” Eiess said gently. Her raven hair cascaded in waves down her chest.
Again? Ailia could not remember ever meeting her, but did not respond. Her eyes almost look like they are glowing, she thought. She felt the longship cast off from the pier, and Ailia knew in the back of her mind what was coming. 
The Empress strolled over to Ailia. “Today, Light, you will lose your life again, just like you did before.” She stood at arm’s length from Ailia now, leering at her. “Do you remember the last time I drowned you? It was quite dramatic but fantastically rewarding for me.” She smiled.
Ailia looked away. She didn’t remember, but she had heard about it from—Why can’t I remember my husband’s name?
“You really do not remember, do you?” The Empress tilted her head back and laughed. “How amusing. Has my treacherous friend, your dear husband, visited you and reminded you of our pact?”
Yes, but I don’t remember what he said, Ailia thought.
The Empress caressed Ailia’s auburn hair with her hand. “You must be afraid now that I am going to drown you again. That means you only have one more life to try and destroy me. One—Two—Three, that is all you were given. You have not done very well with the first two lives at all.” She sighed at length and stroked Ailia’s cheek with the back of her pastel hand.
“No, I’m not afraid,” Ailia lied. She did fear drowning and death but not as much as she feared losing her husband or failing her life’s purpose to kill Eiess. 
“Take care of her,” the Empress said to the Surtorian. “The sooner I can get rid of my problem and the sooner we can leave this wretched land of midnight sun, the better.”
The Surtorian promptly pulled Ailia out onto the deck. The bright sun stung Ailia’s eyes and the feisty summer sea breeze gusted in her face. Uncontrollable fear surged through her body like a scream when her eyes finally adjusted and she saw how far they had already sailed from port. 
The Empress came out from below deck. “I loathe the sun, but I will endure the light just so I can have the satisfaction of watching you die,” she said.
 “I will return!” Ailia screamed. “I will come for you again!”
The Empress remained silent, but she needed no words to communicate the gratification that vibrated from her eyes. 
“Any last words?” the Surtorian said. He locked a chain with weights around Ailia’s ankles and dragged her to the longship’s prow.
“When I return, I will return with a vengeance and—” Ailia said.
The Surtorian pushed her overboard.
“—fulfill my life’s purpose in—” Ailia said just as she hit the water.

*    *    *

Ailia woke up drenched in sweat, gasping for air, crying out for help. Another mare dream. She hadn’t had one in a while and thought the horrifying dreams had finally vanished. She sat up and told herself it was just a bad dream. Nothing else, just a bad dream. Dangling her feet over her bed, she debated whether she should try to go back to sleep or get up. No one in Brandersgaar was awake yet; it was as quiet as the deepest ocean. Then it dawned on her what day it was. 
Today was winter solstice and Ailia’s eighteenth birthday. She always felt gloomy around her birthday as thoughts of her real birth parents surfaced to the forefront of her mind. Normally, she would have invited friends over to celebrate, but all her friends had abandoned her, thinking she was a bad omen and even cursed. Rumors of Ailia’s curse still buzzed through Bergendal, even though she hadn’t had any of her mare dreams since the first snowfall. 
She had wanted to escape all the persecution for a while and had finally realized that marrying Geir would be the best thing for her. No one in Solnes, Geir’s hometown, knew about her alleged curse and she was looking forward to a new beginning, even though it took marrying a man she didn’t love. Their wedding was set for spring and Unni had just recently started sewing Ailia’s wedding dress.
When the red cock crowed, Ailia got up and bundled herself in her sheepskin overcoat. She decided to head to Odinseat to clear her mind. She also wanted to get a better view of the Auroras that had been beaming across the heavens over the past few nights. Odinseat was the place in Midgard where the god Odin supposedly visited when he needed to ponder and receive inspiration. And lately, it was one of Ailia’s favorite places to go when she too needed to ponder. 
As she left, she started walking quickly to work up some body heat. It wasn’t too cold outside today, so she was looking forward to her little secret excursion under the star-speckled heavens. If she hurried, she figured she could even be back by sunrise, which was always late morning this time of year. She crossed the nearly frozen-over Bergendal River and continued through the pine-covered Bergendal mountainside and on to Odinseat. 
Ailia climbed upward for some time until she came to the steepest part of the mountain, right before she reached Odinseat. With her back to the icy ridge, she inched her way sideways, so she could squeeze to the other side of the peak. Finally she stepped onto a perfectly flat massive cliff, which jutted out from the mountain, over the Bergendal valley. When she sat down, she was breathless, having trudged through knee-high snow nearly all the way. The sky had been clear at the onset of her trip, but now heavy, dense clouds hung in the heavens instead of stars. Strange, she thought, how all these clouds had suddenly appeared. There was no way she would be able to see any of the Auroras in this overcast weather. All that for nothing? Ailia laughed. Just like my life lately: a huge disappointment. 
She sat still for a long time, thinking of all the things she had now started to realize she would never experience—Love, being one of them. That was the hardest one to give up. She knew she could never love Geir. There was no connection between them whatsoever and marrying him would be like marrying her Uncle Brander: unnatural, awkward and depressing. But at least I will have peace, she thought.
As she sat there for a while longer, cold wind began sneaking its way back into her body as snowflakes gently swayed their way down from above. I best get home, Ailia thought. The last thing she needed was to get stuck in the mountains in the middle of a snowstorm on her birthday!  
She edged her way back around the mountain corner and started down the slope. Suddenly, she lost her foothold and started sliding down the mountainside. She didn’t slide far, however, before a pine tree stopped her rapid descent with a thud. After assuring herself that she wasn’t hurt, Ailia opened her eyes. 
Then, she saw them: a multitude of ruggedly-dressed men standing in the forest, with longswords, spears and Dane axes in hand, looking like they were planning an attack. A few of the closest men spotted Ailia and started pointing at her, yelling and “Look over there! It is a girl!”
Where had they come from? Ailia wondered, frightened immediately. If this was an attack on Bergendal, she needed to get word to Brander as soon as possible. 
“Gunnar, seize that girl straightaway!” a man said. 
Gunnar? she thought. She used to know a man named Gunnar. He’d come over and visit Brandersgaard when Ailia was a child and she thought she remembered that he might somehow be related to Unni. This probably was not the same Gunnar, she figured. 
She couldn’t tell who was giving the orders—there were so many men. Most of them had heavily-bearded faces and they all had thick fur or sheepskin overcoats, with heavy boots, mittens and hats or hoods covering their heads. Some men held torches.
Ailia got up as fast as she could and ran in the opposite direction of the men. The snow on the slanted forest floor was difficult to run in and she kept slipping. All she could think about was her fear and how stupid she had been in not telling anyone where she had gone. Her heart started racing a thousand miles a minute now because she knew she was in grave danger. 
“Stop!” Gunnar yelled after her. “Stop, or I will kill you!” On his way toward Ailia, he grabbed his Dane ax. “I have my ax aimed at your head and I am ready to unleash it!”
Ailia froze up and turned to face her pursuer. Should I run? Shouldn’t I run? They would probably kill her if they caught her. They would probably kill her if she kept running. Fear raced through Ailia’s veins and her heart hammered violently. Her feet instinctively started backing away from her adversary. 
“Who are you?” she heard herself ask. No answer. The distance between her and Gunnar was decreasing rapidly. Get home! Get home! she told herself. She decided to run. Ailia turned around, but another man had snuck up behind her. She ran into him and he grabbed her by the hair. The pain was agonizing. “Ouch!” she yelled, grabbing his thick forearm. Tears flooded her eyes. He smelled disgusting, unfamiliar, almost sweet and yet rancid. Then she noticed a black raven embroidered onto his grungy, red wool overcoat. Don’t the Vik people carry a raven emblem?  
Her hair hurt, but she forced herself not to think about that now. They are going to kill me! “Unni!  Brander!” she screamed. But she knew they couldn’t hear her. Not out here, miles away from Brandersgaard. In a flash, she thought back to a conversation she had overheard between Bishop Peter and Sigrid, their thrall. “If you ever meet a Vik person, make sure that whatever you do—” the Bishop had said, “—don’t ever let them capture you and take them to their settlement. For if you do, you are as good as dead.” 
“Tie her to this tree here,” a man said. He looked like he was their leader.
Gunnar pulled out some rope, bound her arms and feet and then tied her to a hefty pine tree. “What do I do with her now?” he asked.
“I’ll decide that later,” the leader said and rushed off.
Another man came up to Ailia. He pulled off his hooded winter coat and started untying his trousers. Ailia immediately knew what he was going to do. But before he was even able to touch her, a circular object that came flying through the air struck down the man. His head split open and he crashed to the ground, sending blood everywhere. Snowflakes were coming down so heavily now that Ailia had a hard time seeing where the flying object came from. In the distance, she thought she saw a woman in a light-colored dress catch the flying weapon, and then placing it on her head. A crown?
“Keep your men focused on the task at hand,” a woman’s voice yelled angrily to the leader. “If not, I will find myself some other men.”
“Yes, Empress Eiess,” the leader said, bowing.
Eiess is—alive? Ailia had always thought that the evil Empress from her dreams was alive, but now after having seen her and heard her name spoken from someone else’s lips, it made it so much more real. Ailia’s blood curdled in her veins. She tried desperately to see through the white curtain of snow, but it was impossible because it was still dark out. Ailia tried to see if there were any Surtorians from her mare dreams around, too, but she couldn’t see any at all, only the shabbily dressed men that looked like Vik people.
“Get into your formations,” the Vik leader called out. Without missing a beat, the men lined up in five long lines across the mountainside. They stood close to each other, almost shoulder to shoulder. The men started to look more like warriors now than just a random group of men, Ailia thought. Most of the warriors carried a Dane ax, with a brightly colored wooden shield, but some carried greatswords or even spears or bows and arrows.
Eiess didn’t seem to care that Ailia was there, only that she didn’t distract the men from their plans. Ailia was glad Eiess didn’t recognize her, but maybe she had no reason to recognize her. Maybe Ailia’s dreams were of someone else entirely, someone else Eiess was after. Eiess had come a lot closer to Ailia now and was conversing with the Vik leader.
“You will advance your men from the south. The Surtorians will come from the east. Once the outside of the Northlandic Castle has been secured, I will enter and take over the throne—alone,” Eiess said. “Vilda, the king’s sister, has agreed to try and talk King Olav into a peaceable solution. If he does not agree, we will have to use a more creative—method.”
“My men are ready at your command,” the Vik leader said.
“Good, because I would hate to be disappointed by you again,” Eiess said. “Your job is to make sure the people of Bergendal do not interfere in my plans. Keep the inhabitants as unsuspecting as possible. Only kill if you absolutely have to. I need them alive, so they can do work for me later.”
“Yes, Your Excellency,” the man said. “What about the girl over there?”
Ailia’s ears pinned instinctively, but she barely dared listen for the answer. What will my fate be?  
“I do not have time to worry about an insignificant peasant girl. See that she does not cause any more distractions among your men. After the throne is mine, you can do with her as you please,” Eiess said.
Ailia breathed for a moment. Safe for now, but she feared what would come after. Captivity? Rape? Torture? Death? All four?
“Once that is complete, may I take the rest of Bergendal’s spoils with me?” the Vik leader asked.
“Spoils?” Eiess said. “If you do your job like I have commanded, there should be no spoils. I do not want your men plundering or raping in Bergendal. This will be my city now. Anything you plunder, you will be plundering from me. I will reward you with longships and treasures beyond your wildest imagination if you do your job to my satisfaction.”
“Of course, Your Excellency,” the Vik leader said, bowing.
“Now, let us take charge of Bergendal and the entire Northlandic Kingdom,” Eiess said, commanding.
The Vik leader marched over in front of his men who were waiting.
“No plundering! No raping! No killing, unless I command! Only imprison those who rebel. Try to remain invisible to the people of Bergendal. If you keep to these rules, there will be generous amounts of treasures and countless other opportunities for all of you! If you do not keep to these rules, you all know what happens to traitors!” the Vik leader said. The warriors quietly clanked their weapons on their shields. 
The Vik leader nodded to Eiess, who nodded back. 
“Then let us proceed with our descent,” Eiess said. 
The warriors started moving down the mountainside as quietly as cats, ready to help Eiess usurp the Northlandic Throne. 


7

Shift 

Soon, the women reached the bottom of the stairwell. Nora stopped and grabbed Lucia by the arms, looking her frankly in the eyes. “I cannot come with you, but Vilda is waiting for you outside by the king’s sleigh. She will take you to a safe place where Eiess cannot harm you.” 
 “You must come with me!” Lucia objected. “I refuse to go without you!”
“No time to debate,” Nora said. She grabbed Lucia’s hand and started pulling her down the hallway toward the storeroom. They scurried through the door and in to the pantry room. Maneuvering through the heavily packed chamber was tricky since it was stocked to the ceiling with food, drinks and items they had received just days before in preparation for the winter solstice celebration and Lucia’s coronation. 
Now it appears all the preparations have been for naught, Lucia thought. 
Nora pushed the door open and the icy northern wind hit Lucia’s face. She shuddered. Her fur coat kept the subzero air out of her body and she was grateful that Nora had chosen her warmest fur for this night. The wind was fiercely unforgiving, as it blew the millions of fluttering snowflakes in hundreds of different directions. 
Vilda was waiting outside in front of the sleigh. She looked like she was shivering. “Oh good, there you are. I was beginning to get worried something had happened to you.” She sighed briefly before opening her arms to greet Lucia with an embrace. 
Lucia ran over and hugged her. Something is different with Vilda. She could sense it.
Nora curtsied to Vilda and opened the sleigh door. “Do not you worry, Lucia, my dear. I will be meeting you soon enough,” Nora said.
“No, I refuse to leave without you, Nora.” Lucia folded her arms across her chest. She felt desperate for comfort now and she knew she could not get it from Vilda.
Nora turned Lucia toward her and looked her straight in the eyes again. “You will go and you will go now!” she shouted. 
Lucia had never seen Nora this obstinate and was startled by her sternness. 
“Now get in that sleigh, young lady, immediately. Everything will be fine in the end, you will see,” Nora said.
Lucia wrapped her arms around Nora again, squeezing her tight. Nora seemed to be hiding something, something like fear or pain, or the knowledge that everything would not be all right. Nora pried Lucia’s arms open and guided her into the sleigh. 
“But what about my father? Where is he? Why has he abandoned me?” Lucia asked.
“He will meet you after all is settled,” Nora said, her voice competing with the howling wind. “Take good care of her, Your Majesty,” Nora begged Vilda. 
“Why of course,” Vilda replied. “She is safe in my keeping; you can count on that.”
Nora burst into tears when Lucia sat down. “Lucia, I will see you in a few days, my dear.” She hugged Lucia. “Farewell,” Nora said, stepping back and holding the door open for Vilda. 
“Thank you,” Vilda said civilly as she stepped into the white sleigh. “But in reality, you know I do not need your help.”
Nora curtsied and closed the door behind Vilda. She signaled to the coachman who was at the helm of six strong dun fjord horses. The Coachman signaled to his horses and the sleigh started moving, slowly at first and then faster and faster until it faded into the winter storm. 
Lucia watched her old handmaiden fade into the distance. Will I ever see her again? The question stabbed her in the heart.
“Your father is a fool,” Vilda said suddenly. “He thinks he can get rid of me by sending me away to Hammersten.”
Lucia was shocked. She knew her father and Vilda had some disagreements, but she was stunned that Vilda would ever call him a fool, especially in Lucia’s presence. “I think he could really use you there,” Lucia said.
“Yes, that is all I have ever been to him—someone he can use,” Vilda said.
“That is not what I meant,” Lucia said.
“ It is the truth. Driver!” Vilda yelled. “Stop the sleigh. I need to get out.”
“Do we not need to get away from here as quickly as possible?” Lucia asked. “Eiess is after me!”
The sleigh slowed and then finally stopped. Vilda got up next to the coachman and pushed him off with one forceful shove, sending him into the snow. 
“What are you doing?” Lucia asked, ready to jump out of the sleigh. “Have you completely lost your mind?”
“We need to go back to the castle. We need the Aesira Jewel, so you can keep shifting the seasons. Your father is a fool to not have thought about that,” Vilda said.
Lucia felt stupid. Vilda was right. If Eiess got hold of the Aesira Jewel, all would be lost. They rode back as quickly as possible and both got out of the sleigh.
“Do you know where the Aesira Jewel is kept?” Vilda asked.
How does she know about the Jewel anyway? I thought only my father and I knew about it. And now that she started thinking about it, maybe her father had already thought of what to do with the Aesira Jewel.
“There is no time to waste. Eiess will be here any moment,” Vilda said.
“I will go get it. Wait here,” Lucia said.
“No!” Vilda yelled. “I must come with you.”
“No, I will go get it,” Lucia insisted. “My father told me that I should tell no one where it is kept hidden, not even you.”
“Your mother told me everything about the Aesira Jewel and the Scrolls. You do not have to hide anything from me. I am only trying to help you. You can trust me, Lucia,” Vilda said.
Scrolls? Lucia thought. “Let us go see my father first.”
Vilda grabbed Lucia’s arm and shook it. “No! There is no time!”
“I would think, if you do not know where the Aesira Jewel is, you should not have access to it,” Lucia said, starting toward the castle entrance.
Vilda grabbed Lucia’s arm again and pulled her back. She hit her across the face this time and grabbed a chain she had kept underneath a fur in the sleigh. She chained Lucia’s wrists and pulled her after her into the castle and up the stairs to the throne room.
Lucia's mouth and nose was bleeding heavily onto her stark-white fur coat, but her focus was not on that. When my father sees me, he will set me free. “Father!” Lucia yelled, as she saw him with his counselors, their heads down over a table, looking like they were planning their attack.
Vilda tugged the chain she had placed around Lucia’s wrists and Lucia fell hard to the floor, slamming her head against the cold stone surface. Lucia let out a cry. Vilda dragged Lucia back along the floor, pulling on the chain that detained her.
“What in Thor’s name are you doing here?” Olav asked. His face paled immediately. “And what are you doing to Lucia?”
“Brother, dear Brother,” Vilda started. “Hear me out. I have a superior solution to your—to our —problems. It will be better for your people, for your household, for you and especially for Lucia, your only heir to the throne.”
“Vilda, this is no time for discussion; we need to prepare, so we have a chance to save the Northlandic Kingdom. What are you doing? I asked you to take Lucia far away from here, so she would be protected and safe.” Olav started toward Vilda.
“Stop, or I will kill her,” Vilda said, pulling out a dagger.
Olav stopped.
“Brother, do you really think you can stand up to Eiess? You will be dead by morning if you resist her,” Vilda said with a disdainful laugh. “There must be a way we can peaceably resolve matters with her,” she said slyly.
“You know there is no way to peaceably resolve matters with Eiess. She demands utter submission and servitude from everyone. I will not discuss it here. Now, release Lucia instantly!” His voice thundered through the throne room.
All the guards stood at unease.
“And if I do not?” Vilda challenged.
“You will be thrown in the dungeon until we are through fighting this battle and you will be tried for treason,” Olav threatened. 
“Ha!” Vilda yelled. “Ha, ha, ha! I have been living under your pathetic rule for years and this is how you repay me? I have supported you and I have cared for you and Lucia since Maud’s passing. I can help you find a way to live in peace under the Empress and what do you do? You throw it in my face! You king of scum!” she fumed.
Olav gestured to his Grand Marshal and he sent the two closest guards to restrain her. 
Vilda pressed the dagger in front of Lucia’s heart so hard that it pierced her dress. 
Lucia became very afraid now and let out a whimper. The guards halted their attack on Vilda. 
“My Brother, do you think I would come in here empty-handed and without a decent plan? I am not an idiot like you.” She then pulled Lucia in even closer and spoke directly into her ear. “Lucia, your father is not a king to be admired. He hardly even deserves the title. He is a coward and a feeble-minded man. He was never even faithful to your poor mother and spent his time seducing and whoring with another woman named Ava. Did you know your mother was heartbroken over this? She would come to me crying almost every day. She deserved a much better man than him.” 
Lucia looked at her father to see if this was true, not wanting to believe Vilda’s words.
“Enough!” Olav spewed. “Leave her out of this!” 
It is true, Lucia thought. Her heart shattered for her poor mother.
Vilda stood up tall, an air of satisfaction and accomplishment resting on her face. “The seed of doubt has been planted in Lucia’s mind. Now, she is starting to see who you truly are, like I do.” 
“What do you want?” Olav asked hostilely.
“I want to be heard!” Vilda snapped back. “I want to feel that what I have to say is important to you!”
“Do not think of yourself only. Eiess will be here soon and we will surely be defeated unless we act now,” Olav said, meekly now.
“Ah, but she is part of the solution—my solution,” Vilda retorted.
“Very well. If you let Lucia go, you may speak your plan and I will listen.”
“I will not let her go, but hear me now. Eiess will spare your lives and continue the seasons as usual if you give her all power over the Northlandic Kingdom and generously bestow her with the Aesira Jewel,” Vilda said.
“Have you been speaking with her?” Olav asked, his face grimacing in horror.
“She is my ally,” Vilda revealed, looking proud. “An unexpected friend indeed.” She smiled.
“How could you betray your own blood?” Lucia asked. I will never do that.
“At first, I was afraid of the powerful Empress and resisted her advances. I could never betray my brother, my own blood, I believed. But then I thought, what is blood? One cannot choose one’s family; one is just randomly born into one and stuck with them until death. Life in Midgard is too short and difficult to suffer through bad families. So, when I accepted an audience with Eiess, she told me that she is here as the peace giver, the one who brings rest, tranquility and stillness to the weary, worried heart. She is here to—” Vilda said.
Olav interrupted her. “Do you comprehend the destruction she will bring on this land if she gets her hands on the Aesira Jewel? She will usurp everyone’s power, making all men, women and children thralls, with no freedom, no choice and no reason to live. We will all suffer and starve to death sooner or later. Have you given any thought to that?” Olav yelled.
“No, Olav, she has promised to spare the people and to ring in the new season every solstice. She will even allow Lucia to do it. She is no liar, Brother. You know this. She is the Sentinor of rest and peace,” Vilda said.
Olav paused. “She is a liar. She is the ultimate liar! And she will devastate every living soul in Midgard with her finite ideas of rest.” Olav thought for a moment. “Vilda, I concede; I will listen to her when she gets here,” he stated. “Now, release Lucia immediately.”
“As soon as you give me the Aesira Jewel,” Vilda answered slyly.
Unexpectedly to all, the castle walls started shaking violently. Vilda lost her footing and her grasp on Lucia, falling to the floor. Lucia quickly made her way to her father’s side.
“Are you hurt?” Olav asked.
Lucia shook her head. “Only afraid,” she admitted.
Then, as quickly as it had begun, the shaking ceased. All the torches were stripped of their flames, leaving the throne room in a void of light where only a hollow silence remained. Whistling wind sang through the corridors and as the wind neared, it sounded like a woman’s breath exhaling deeply. Sounds of ice crystallizing the walls trickled down in the darkness and with one last breath, the fireplace was also extinguished. 
Everyone in the room held their breaths in the dark as they waited for some evil to beset them. It was alarmingly quiet outside. No birds sang. No men spoke. And not even the wind howled anymore. If Eiess had arrived, there should surely have been a battle, with all the guards that had lined up outside, but there was only the resonating sound of sinister silence. Dawn started to illuminate the cloudy sky and light penetrated the stained-glass windows. The guards’ armor picked up the subtle luminosity, reflecting it onto the walls. 
Lucia could see her breath in front of her in the now-freezing room. She gathered her fur coat closely around her neck and tried to make eye contact with her father. She was afraid to speak, thinking that her voice would attract more danger. 
Then, almost unnoticed, Eiess entered the room.

*    *    *

	Eiess entered the room alone. She moved like a spirit, ethereally flowing as with invisible wings, gliding over the solid, cold, stone floor. She was as stunningly beautiful as she was cold. Her dress was made of ice blue and white silk and was simple, yet timeless. The square neckline on the masterpiece accentuated her square jawline and as subtle light peeked through the windows, Lucia could see that she had piercing light-green eyes and pitch-black hair. Eiess’s age could not be determined, for no one knew when she had actually entered Midgard’s Realm. And she looked like she was in her mid-twenties, still exuding youthful beauty and vivacity. She was also the only living human-like being Lucia knew of who was not warm-blooded and Lucia had heard rumors that she was a soulless creature, who would devour human souls, so she could continue to live in Midgard.
Eiess’s skin was as pale and smooth as porcelain, but strangely enough, she did, Lucia could see, have light, rosy cheeks. Her posture was impeccable and she moved with graceful exquisiteness. The sleeves on her dress were not puffed, but sleek and flared down toward the floor. The drop set waist dress accentuated her elegant thinness. Her floor-length skirt had a train that flowed endlessly, like the breath from a warm-blooded living being on a cold winter night. Sparkling diamond rings adorned most of her fingers and a diamond necklace decorated her swan-like neckline. The Empress had no armor to shield her body, no guard to protect her life; she needed no such things. Her power to kill was infinite and they all knew it and felt her threatening, overpowering presence as she silently entered their midst. 
“Greetings,” Eiess said, her expression completely unapologetic.
Vilda moved to greet her. “My Empress,” she said and bowed down on the floor before Eiess. “I have failed to convince King Olav of your noble and altruistic intentions,” she offered.
“Never mind him,” Eiess responded with the greatest of calmness in her deep, soothing voice. “I will take care of him,” she said, staring at Lucia as if she owned her. 
“My daughter is not for the taking and using in your scheme to control me,” Olav said, pulling Lucia closer.
“Ah, but she is,” Eiess refuted. “You seem to think that you are more powerful than me and that you have a say in the matter. I am Empress here. This is my reign!”
Lucia gasped as a deep chill shot down her spine. “My dream,” she whispered.
 “Ah, now you recognize me, child,” Eiess said. “My question is the same for you. I will persist until I have the answer I seek.” 
“I will never tell you!” Lucia insisted. She was still clinging onto her father’s armor.
“I will give you an opportunity to join me, Olav,” Eiess said, shifting her focus onto him.
“Never!” Olav roared.
“Are you certain? For if you do not, I will seize the castle by force and many more will die today,” Eiess said.
“Never!” he yelled again.
Smiling, Eiess raised her left hand and looked up into the ceiling. She clenched her fist and then pulled her hand down in to her body. She then raised her right hand, with her palm up toward Lucia and started pulling her forward with an imperceptible force. 
Lucia started screaming out of fear as she felt the pull of gravity coming from Eiess. As she was being dragged in the direction of her adversary, she grabbed on to her father’s arm with all her might. She looked into her father’s eyes and yelled, “Father, help me, Father!”
“Lucia!” Olav yelled back, grabbing on tighter to keep her from being pulled away. “Charge!” he yelled to the guards. 
They responded immediately, attacking Eiess with full force.
For a moment, Eiess let go of Lucia, but only to get rid of the distraction Olav had caused. Eiess pulled her hands into her chest, closed her eyes and paused. Then, she opened her glowing, icy green eyes and flung her arms out to the side as quickly as she could. Darkness oozed from her arms, permeating the room. The obscure mist swallowed all the guards and all light in its blackness. The hazy smoke lifted the guards up and up forcing them to the top of the high ceilings. Nervous clamors from the men echoed the room. A fall from there might mean death. Then, Eiess dropped her arms and the darkness disappeared. She slammed the guards to the stone floor with a crash. The colorful stained-glass windows exploded to the outside, opening passages for the cool northern air to enter. 
“Go!” Olav said, pulling Lucia with him toward the northwestern exit door.
The Grand Marshal and four guards followed Olav and Lucia.
Wasting no time, Eiess lifted the diamond and sapphire crown off of her head and flung it toward them, cutting the Grand Marshal and one of the guards on their foreheads. The crown rebounded after its brief, lethal flight back into the hands of the Empress who caught it mid-air. She returned the weapon to her head. It looked innocently beautiful where it sat.
“Thank you Lamnia,” Lucia heard Eiess say. It almost sounded as if she were speaking to her crown.
Lucia shrieked as they exited, stepping over the men, twisting in agony on the floor. 
“You shall not live where you have lived before,” Eiess said, passing by the victims, stepping around their tormented bodies.
