“Vilda, follow me!” Lucia heard Eiess command. Looking back, she clung to her father’s hand for dear life as they ran toward the queen’s chamber. “They are coming!”
A guard opened the door to the room and shut it behind them after everyone had entered.
“Chairs, furniture, everything in front of the door now!” Olav shouted. “Before we leave, we must get the Aesira Jewel. It is the only way to keep the Empress from becoming omnipotent,” Olav ordered. “It rests behind my mural there.” 
Just then, the door blasted open and Eiess entered the chamber with Vilda and numerous Surtorians by her side. 
Lucia wondered if the Surtorians were from Jotunheim, the realm of the giants. They looked monstrously large. 
“I am glad to see you have all managed to meet in my new chamber. I believe I have everything I need here, once my dear Lucia shows me where the Aesira Jewel is. So, my King, I have grown weary of you and I see no need for you to continue living. I am the new queen of this castle and I will reign here forever,” Eiess said, gesturing to the entire room. “Prepare to meet your dead queen.” Eiess stared at Olav with an emotionless gaze. 
He started to shiver uncontrollably, his eyes rolling back into his skull. 
Lucia had heard about how the Empress could kill someone by freezing them to death from the inside, by sucking out their warmth and light, or their very soul. “If you kill him, I will never tell you where the Aesira Jewel is! If you spare his life and let us go, I will give you the Aesira Jewel right now.”
The Empress agreed and released Olav. “I will let you leave the castle with no harm done to you, or your father, or the rest of your useless guards,” she said and waived to the guards with the back of her hand.
Lucia walked over to the mural and tried to lift it off the wall. It was far too heavy for her, so Olav made his way over and helped her. They lost their grip on it, as they lowered it and the frame cracked as it hit the floor. Seeing it was a lost cause, they let the whole mural fall down with a loud thud.
“Oh, how fitting,” Eiess chuckled. Her Surtorians laughed too.
Behind the mural was a small silver door in the wall. Lucia opened it, revealing the Aesira Jewel that was inside. She was surprised at how large it was. She lifted the oval yellow diamond up. It was almost too large to fit in the grasp of both her hands. “Here you are,” Lucia said, holding it up for Eiess to take. 
Eiess looked at the Aesira Jewel and slightly raised her right eyebrow. “After all these years, I thought it would be so much harder to get my hands on the Aesira Jewel,” she chuckled wickedly. “And now I realize that all I needed to do was ask!” She reached for the Jewel and took it gently out of Lucia’s hands. “You may go,” she said, without taking her eyes off of her new prize. She caressed the diamond with her fingertips, studying its intricate workmanship.
“But, Your Excellency, we cannot utilize the Jewel without—” Vilda started to say.
“You think I do not realize that the Jewel needs the Sun Queen and Iluxia to work?  Do you still think I plan on using the Aesira Jewel? You really are stupid.”
Vilda gasped and looked at Olav. 
“Now, there will be no more light or summer from this day forward. No more heat to dread, no more warmth to dull my bitterly cold wind, no more sun to light the shades and shadows!” Eiess said. “Now get out of here before I throw you into the tower!” she warned Lucia and Olav. “And Vilda, get me Johan!” 
Vilda’s eyes searched nervously, but she curtsied and left.
Olav, Lucia, and the three guards headed for the door immediately. They rushed down the western hallway, down the stairs and into the courtyard. The sky was blue and clear now. “Prepare the sleigh!” Olav ordered to his stable boy. “Where are all the guards?” Olav asked the boy.
“What guards?” the stable boy answered.
Olav looked shocked. “All the guards that were—” A loud crashing sound from the castle frightened them all. “Never you mind,” Olav said. “You must leave the castle immediately.” He helped Lucia into the sleigh. “If you do not leave, Eiess will have you for her own.”
Lucia thought the stable boy looked unusual, void of his own personality, his eyes glazed over and drained.
The guard cracked the whip and the horses neighed before taking off. When they were well outside the castle boundaries, Olav leaned over to Lucia. 
“How long until Eiess figures out that what I gave her is not the real Aesira Jewel?” he asked. 
“What?” Lucia looked at him. “That was not the real Aesira Jewel?”
Olav shook his head.
“Why did you tell me the Aesira Jewel rested behind the mural? Did you know Eiess would come today?” Lucia did not like that he had used her to promote his own purposes, even if his purposes had been for the better.
“I suspected she might. My scouts had been telling me all week that they had seen more and more Surtorians in the Northlandic Kingdom. I will admit, I did not know my plan would work so seamlessly.”
Lucia thought they had just succeeded in the biggest deception since the beginning of humanity. “Well, where is the real Aesira Jewel then?” Lucia wondered.
Olav looked at the other three men in the sleigh. 
She nodded. Of course, they needed to keep it a secret.
No one spoke a word, but their eyes all spoke of fear and nervousness as the horses galloped toward safety. Lucia rested her head on her father’s shoulder. Olav had not had time to grab his overcoat, but fortunately, there were fur throws and wool blankets in the sleigh that he could wrap himself in. 
Unexpectedly, one of the guards stood up, drew his sword, stabbed the other two guards in the chest and knocked Olav unconscious with the hilt. 
Before Lucia even had a chance to react, Haakon turned to her and slammed her head into the side of the sleigh. As he held her down to tie her hands behind her back, she could see Eiess’s Surtorians storming in their direction on their black horses. Eiess knows.


8

Wolves

The first thing Ailia noticed when she woke up was the eerie, hollow sound of the wind. Then, the pain set in. Her whole body felt as if it had been trampled on by a multitude of charging reindeer. She opened her eyes slowly and tried to make sense of her surroundings. Her cold fingers met the cold ground as she pushed herself up to a sitting position and she immediately knew she wasn’t at home. 
An opening a few feet away revealed that she was in some sort of a cave. The hood of her overcoat was down, so she pulled it up over her numb ears with her stiff, freezing fingers. Next to her was a piece of material that looked like a handkerchief and a small empty glass flask. She picked them up and put them both in her pocket. 
As she rose, she peered to the outside and saw the Northern Star shining brightly in the sky. She tried to remember how she ended up here, but the last thing she remembered was her hiking to Odinseat, then the Vik men on the mountainside, then—nothing. Ailia stumbled over to the ice cave’s exit in hopes of learning where she was, but the night made it difficult for her to see. An unsettling feeling in her stomach told her that she was far, far away from home. 
The full moon’s sheen glazed the snow-covered mountains and valleys with its subdued light and subtly lit up the black vastness of the heavens. Endless and hazardously beautiful glacier mounds and gorges were all Ailia could see. Where, why, how? Her mind raced. Each gust of wickedly cold wind felt like death calling and it illuminated one dangerous reality; she was wet. 
Both Ailia’s ivory under tunic and dull red top tunic were cold and wet and filthy at the skirt hem. Her sleeves came down tight to the wrists and were also wet and dirty at the edges. The butterfly stitches around her skirt hem and sleeves looked tattered and were barely noticeable. She looked down at her dark leather boots and noticed that they looked unusually worn from what she could remember and Ailia didn’t even recognize the navy woolen hooded cloak she was wearing. 
She pulled the handkerchief out of her pocket and now saw that it carried a black raven embroidering. Don’t the Vik people carry the raven emblem? It sent a chill down her already cold spine. She hated the Vik people for the fear they had instilled in the people of her hometown, but she put the cloth back in her pocket, hoping it would lead to clues about how she had ended up here so dirty, so battered and so alone. The flask was empty and had no scent, revealing nothing of its previous contents.
Biting cold wind tossed her messy hair in every direction as she exited the icy dome. She defensively grabbed the fur hood of her overcoat and pulled it tight onto her face to prevent the bitter blasts from entering her cloak. In the dim light, she could see the silhouette of the countryside and realized she was in an ice cave located on or close to the Blue Glaciers. 
“How did I end up here?” Ailia said out loud as if someone were listening. Her full lips burned in the colder than cold air. She recognized the towering Trollstein Mountains in the distant north, which were not far from her home, but very far from where she now found herself. 
There is no time to waste, she said to herself. She took a few steps and slid on the glassy ice, falling onto her bottom with a thump. “Why?” she said, looking up at the millions of stars above. Can’t I catch a break?  Her stomach made a grumbling sound and she grabbed her hollow belly. It was thinner than she had remembered it being. Finding food out here would be as likely as finding a fish in the sky. If she were extremely lucky, she would run into someone or find a cabin nearby with some hospitable folks. The odds seemed impossibly against her favor.
She stood up and looked for a path to follow as she alternately walked and crawled across the slick, transparent glacier. How long will I last before exhaustion sets in and defeat claims its win? She angrily hurled the thought out of her mind. Any doubt out here, Ailia, will cost you your life.
Continuing over the ice, she heard the distant howling of wolves, wolves that were probably ravenous and hunting for their next meal. Ailia quickly spotted the seven dark animals on the white glacier mountain, their eyes glowing orange in the light of the moon. She hoped they hadn’t seen her or picked up her scent, but it appeared by the direction they were heading that they had not only seen her but were also pursuing her. Ailia’s heart started pounding in her chest. She had never felt this type of fear before, the fear of being hunted and eaten alive by famished, wild creatures. 
She scanned the area for any shelter, but the bare glaciers had no trees, no rocks, nor any other places that she could see to easily hide or escape. Instinctively, Ailia started running in the opposite direction as her mind raced to find a solution. Her legs kept slipping and she fell over and over again as her body slammed onto the unforgiving surface. No! This is not how I am going to die! She couldn’t hold her voice back from letting out a loud, high-pitched scream. She screamed again and again. Her eyes welled up with tears, causing her vision to blur. 
It was already difficult to see in the dark, but reason had left and emotion had taken over. She continued to run as fast as she could, hurrying away from the vicious predators. Now, she could hear their paws hammering the ice behind her. Oh no, this is it! she thought.
Then, she glimpsed a thick, dried, wooden stick on the ground in front of her and picked it up. She hastily turned around and started swinging the stick as quickly and violently as she could in the wolves’ direction as she was screaming at the top of her lungs, trying uselessly to intimidate the wolves. 
The wolves slowed their pace as they methodically closed in on her, growling, snarling, licking their fangs and wagging their tails enthusiastically in excitement over their fresh prey. One wolf looked like he was twice the size of the others and it appeared he was heading the attack on Ailia. 
A smaller wolf got hold of her stick and locked it in with his sharp fangs. Ailia and the wolf were tugging at the stick in opposite directions, fighting for their own victory and a common reward—life. As she pulled the stick with all her might, she succeeded in tearing it from the wolf’s jaw. With the stick loose, she fell down on the ground, but she quickly recovered and jumped back to her feet. Adrenaline had taken over. Swinging at the wolf, she hit him hard on the side of the head causing him to fall to the ground with a whimper. 
Then, the leader came in at Ailia with full force. He was much stronger than the first wolf and deftly pulled the stick out of Ailia’s hands, flinging it to the side as he continued his fixated pursuit without losing a beat. He leapt toward Ailia and sunk his fangs into her foot. Ailia screamed in pain. The pain was sharp and intense and it felt like his incisors had dug all the way into the marrow of her bones. She kicked the large creature in the snout with her other leg and pulled her injured one out of the boot. Thick, red blood dripped onto the white snow. 
All the adrenaline had started to wear Ailia out and she felt like she didn’t have the willpower to fight back any more. Another wolf came at her arm, but it wasn’t able to bite through her thick wool cloak. He bit again and again, until the fabric started giving into his piercing teeth. The animal’s jaws were brutally strong and Ailia could feel the other wolves coming at her, tugging her, pulling her hair, her arms and her legs, wanting to get to her flesh. Just as she thought there was no hope and she was ready to give up, she heard one of the wolves whimper and fall to the ground. The other wolves looked up and another wolf was hit with an arrow, falling to the ground. 
The leader of the pack looked at Ailia and was hit by an arrow on his upper back. He whimpered and looked like he decided to abandon the hunt, pulling with him the rest of the pack. 
Ailia couldn’t believe that the wolves were gone. She knew someone was there heading in her direction, wanting to help her, someone who had saved her life. “Help,” she was able to cry out. “Please, my leg—” she pleaded to her unexpected rescuer.
A young man ran to her side and crouched down beside her. “Everything is going to be fine,” he said, dropping his bow onto the snow beside her as he quickly scanned her body. “Lucia, is that you?” he asked.
Ailia felt her strength leaving her body and then everything went black.

*    *    *

Ailia woke up and was instantly gripped by the pain she felt in her foot. She moaned quietly. A lively fire burned close to her and she enjoyed its presence. White polar bear furs and wool blankets covered Ailia’s body and she felt no coldness—except for on her nose—even though she was still outside and still on the glaciers, as far as she could tell. 
The young man that she recognized as her rescuer approached her. There seemed to be something both in his physical appearance and the way he moved and spoke that was so familiar, so comfortable and soothing.
“I have never seen a young girl like you take on the furious seven wolves before,” he said lightheartedly. Brown, heavy fur covered his blue wool overcoat. His fur hat covered what, Ailia thought, looked like dark-brown hair. 
“The who?” Ailia said.
“The wolves. A little south of here they are known as the furious seven. They have been responsible for many lost sheep lately.”
 “Oh, well, just so you know, I was close to beating them and if you had not interrupted me, I would have had them.” Ailia tried to smile, but noticed that even that hurt.
The stranger smiled. “I believe you!” He laughed. “Your leg will be fine in a few weeks. It looked really bad, several deep puncture wounds and lacerations. I sewed it up for you while you were out. You are lucky the wolves did not break any bones in your legs or arms with their tough jaws,” he said more seriously, stirring a pot on the fire. “I put an herbal compress on your foot and also on some smaller wounds on your face. You also had some sores on your hands from fighting off the wolves and I put some restorative ointment on them, as well.”
“Are you a healer?” Ailia asked, impressed by his apparent knowledge in the area. Ailia looked him in his blue eyes for the first time and was struck by his calming presence. Her soul vibrated at the sound of his deep voice and she felt wholly and unreservedly drawn to him as if by a dynamic, living force—a force that was eternal, yet just beginning to flourish. 
“I am,” he said. “My name is Soren.” He continued to stir the liquid and then he lifted the ladle and smelled the brew. 
Ailia thought the ladle looked like it was hundreds of years old. It was crooked and stained from years of use. “I’m Ailia.” She sat up.
“Pleased to meet you,” Soren said.
“Were you on your way to help someone?” Ailia asked cautiously, not wanting to impose too much on his privacy.
“Yes,” he said without offering any more information about where he was going. 
Ailia felt guilty right away for having stopped him on his journey. “Sorry,” she said. “I hope I didn’t cause anyone else to suffer—or die.”
He smiled. “No, they will be fine,” he said as he poured the boiling liquid into a wooden bowl and offered it to her. “Drink this. It will help take the edge off the pain.”
 Ailia reached for the bowl and smelled it. It smelled like a mixture of coriander, mint and lavender, as well as some other herbs she was not familiar with. She carefully sipped the hot drink and grimaced when the bitterness hit her tongue. 
Soren laughed briefly. “Not very appealing to the palate, I know, but it will help you sleep through the night.”
“Have I been out long?” Ailia asked, expecting a no.
“Oh, about three and a half days,” Soren answered.
“Oh, no! My aunt and uncle will be extremely worried about me,” Ailia said. “Last I remember, I was hiking up to Odinseat, and they didn’t know that I had left.” Then she thought about it. “And it was on my birthday!” She remembered. But after that she couldn’t remember anymore.
“When was your birthday?”
“It’s on winter solstice,” Ailia said.
“That is the last you remember?” Soren asked, looking surprised.
“Yes.”
“Have you been on the glaciers for a long time?” Soren asked.
“I, uh—I don’t know. I don’t remember. I don’t know how I got here, or how long I have been gone,” Ailia said.
“If the last thing you remember was your birthday on winter solstice, you’ve been gone for about four months,” Soren said.
Ailia choked on the tonic she was drinking, coughing it up. “What?” she yelled. Her aunt and uncle must be worried sick. Maybe they even thought she was dead.
“So you have been gone a while,” Soren said.
“Yes.” Months have disappeared, if what Soren was saying was true. “I’m sorry, I—uh, I’m a little shocked,” Ailia said.
“No need to apologize. I am sure I would react the exact same way if I was in your position,” Soren said. He glanced at her a couple of times, looking like he was trying to figure her out.
Maybe he thinks I’m strange, Ailia thought. “I’m just not feeling well. My leg hurts, my head hurts—” she said, trying to explain why she was acting strange.
“The tonic will help you feel better soon,” Soren said.
Ailia kept sipping the bitter brew until it was all gone. It felt good in her stomach and warmed her aching body, though it did nothing to calm her nerves. She needed to find a way to get home—fast. “So, are you from Bergendal or thereabouts?” she asked, hoping maybe she could travel with him home.
“No, I am actually from Trollsoe originally, but I have not lived there for quite some time. It is about a week’s journey by horse, toward the south-west, bordering on the ocean,” Soren said. He paused before he added, “Now, I mainly travel to different cities and stay for a few weeks until my help is needed elsewhere. What about you?”
“I’m from Bergendal. My parents died of the smallpox before I can remember and my Aunt Unni and Uncle Brander raised me as their own.”
Soren looked at her again, as if he thought there was something strange about her.
Why does he keep looking at me like that? Ailia thought. She’d had enough of people treating her unkind. “They’re not actually related to me, but I call them aunt and uncle anyway.”
“Sorry to hear about your parents,” Soren said. “I have lost loved ones in the past. I know how painful it can be.” His face went stoic.
That melted Ailia’s heart. Maybe he was just wanting to help her, not judge her. “I don’t remember my birth parents at all,” Ailia said. “ I wish I would have known them. I wish I could remember my mother’s voice, or that I even had something from them, a letter, an item of clothing, something to prove that they existed.” Ailia wasn’t sure if the drink was starting to work and that is why she felt she could be so open with Soren, or if he was indeed a kindred soul. 
“So you remember the old Bergendal before the eternal winter began?” Soren said.
The eternal winter, isn’t that a prophecy? It sounded like it was. Ailia knew she had heard about it from somewhere, but where? “I suppose. I don’t know any other Bergendal than the normal one I grew up in,” she said. “Is it different?” Now, she started worrying whether her aunt and uncle were all right. They could have been taken by the Vik people, or could be—She didn’t want to think about what bad things could have happened to them.
“Bergendal is not too different, but in worse condition than before.” Soren took off his gloves and used some snow to rinse out the pot he had made the brew in. 
Ailia saw that he wore a gold serpent wedding band on his ring finger. Married. She thought it was strange he hadn’t mentioned his wife or family yet. After a little while the brew was starting to make her feel really drowsy and she couldn’t resist yawning. 
“Well, do not let me keep you up,” Soren said. “We had better get some sleep before dawn. We have a long journey ahead of us and it is easier to travel during daylight. It will take us two to three days to get back to Bergendal.” 
Ailia assumed that was an invitation for him to help her get back home. “Oh, all right,” she said and yawned again.
Soren grabbed a wooden bucket and threw some melted snow on the fire to put it out. The steam rose to the heavens as the fire made a crackling hissing sound before it finally settled into an orangey glow. He plopped down on the opposite side of the fire pit in a makeshift bed that he had prepared for himself. 
Ailia could see that he had given her all the warmer furs as he pulled the thin, overused reindeer fur over his body. Looking at him, she couldn’t help but notice how attractive he looked in the light of the fire. There was something different about Soren, something she could not pinpoint, but something so wonderful.
Their eyes met for a brief moment and Ailia quickly looked away, slightly self-conscious and uneasy about the sudden attraction she felt toward him. Something was familiar about Soren. Where do I know him from? “Good night,” she said, lying down and pulling the furs over her body.
“Good night,” Soren responded. “If you need anything, please do not hesitate to wake me. I am a light sleeper.”
“Thank you,” Ailia said with a nod. “For all you have done. Oh and I was just wondering—” Ailia said. “You mentioned the name Lucia right when you saw me?”
“Yes, my mistake. I thought at first you were someone else.” He nodded, turned around and laid his head on the thin linen blanket. 
“Good night then,” Ailia said and closed her heavy eyelids. 



9

Friend or Foe

Ailia fell fast asleep. In her dream she found herself alone on a high mountaintop dressed in a simple, white, long-sleeved robe she had seen other youth wear at their baptism and confirmation. Heavy snow fell from the sky and the winter wind was so strong that it pushed her body off balance as she walked forward into a white abyss. Looking forward, she saw the silhouette of another taller mountain lingering in the distance. 
Then, just as quickly as it had started, the snow stopped and the burning yellow sun revealed itself in the suddenly blue sky. As she felt the sun’s warm and soothing rays on her face, Ailia closed her eyes to enjoy a rare moment, soaking in the life-giver’s glorious rays. She breathed deeply as if to fill her body with its healing virtue, not understanding how she could have ever forgotten how pleasurable its heat felt on her skin and on her hair. It feels like paradise. Then, in the near distance, she heard a man’s voice calling her name.
“Ailia!” he beckoned.
She opened her eyes and looked around. To her surprise, all the snow on the ground and mountains had vanished. Golden rays from the sun smothered the regal mountains as the bottomless deep blue waters from the old, wise ocean flowed between them. How could she have neglected to remember how breathtakingly sublime summer was and how exquisite nature was all around her?  
The mountainsides were covered with resplendent green fern trees and the water lay resting still, mirroring the breathtakingly beautiful scenery above. Anemone Hepatica flowers covered the aspen forest grounds to her left. These delicate blue flowers only blossomed in Bergendal during late springtime and were Ailia’s favorite flower. She hadn’t seen them in forever, it seemed. Down by the lake, Ailia saw her Uncle Brander and Aunt Unni and also the town’s Bishop, Peter, standing by a Stave Church. They were all looking in her direction, smiling at her. 
Brander summoned her again. “Ailia!” he called. “Here!” He waved his hand to come join them.
Ailia was excited to see them. She waved back and started running down to the fjord. As she felt the cool grass beneath her bare feet, a child-like laugh spontaneously escaped her lips. She smiled and for a moment, she felt like a carefree child again. 
The palisade dark mahogany church was situated to the right of the fjord. It had five tiers of dark-brown, double-sharply sloped roofs, shell-like exterior shingles and intricate carvings of dragons and Christian images and symbols. Ailia remembered that Aunt Unni had become increasingly interested in the new religion. Ailia had wanted to join the Christian faith with Aunt Unni because she liked how merciful and loving their god was.
Aunt Unni had spoken to Ailia, saying that if she wanted to join the Christian faith, she’d have to be baptized and receive her confirmation. 
Ailia slowed down as she neared her family and Bishop Peter. As soon as she reached them, Ailia embraced Aunt Unni and then Uncle Brander, who were both beaming with joy.
“We have missed you so, Ailia,” Uncle Brander said, tears streaming down his normally jovial face.
Ailia didn’t know how long she had been away and had forgotten that she was dreaming—the dream seemed so real.
“Today is your day to be baptized and confirmed,” Uncle Brander said. He lifted his hand toward Bishop Peter. 
“Today, you will be baptized and confirmed a member of the Christian faith. By so doing, you will swear to follow Christ and all His teachings. You will swear to live a life of service and virtue and always remember Him,” Bishop Peter preached.	
    	Ailia looked at Uncle Brander and he nodded. Ailia looked back at Bishop Peter and nodded, yes.
“Then, to prove your commitment to Christ, enter these waters today and be baptized in His holy name,” Bishop Peter said, extending an inviting arm.
Ailia took Bishop Peter’s arm and together, they entered the cool, clear waters. They went out until the water reached Ailia’s waistline. 
Bishop Peter raised his right hand. “Do you freely confess that you are a sinner?  Do you place your trust in Christ as your Savior and repent of all your sins?” he asked Ailia, his voice a supplication. 
“Yes.” Ailia nodded.
 “In accordance to our Lord’s teachings and by His command, I baptize you, my Christian sister, in the name of the Father, the Son and the Holy Spirit, Amen.”
 “Amen.” Ailia inhaled and held her breath before she was submerged. 
Bishop Peter then placed his other hand around her waist and pushed her under the water. 
Ailia’s feet lifted off the bottom of the ocean. She felt the cool, cleansing water enclose her body, leaving her feeling buoyant and unrestricted from the forces of gravity. As she went to place her feet on the bottom of the ocean to come back up again, she could not get a good foothold. Bishop Peter’s hands were no longer around her waist. They had disappeared along with him. 
Then, she started to kick her feet to come to the surface. The water grew colder and colder every second and Ailia opened her eyes to see how far she was from the surface. It was dark now and difficult to see. Being far away from the surface, she started swimming upwards toward the sun, whose rays had dimmed. As she drew close to the surface, she saw that ice had started to form on top of the water. But as she was about to reach the surface, the ice had completely covered the entire fjord, leaving her trapped and not able to break through. Ailia started to panic and hit the thick ice with her fists as hard as she could. Now, she could feel that the air was running out of her lungs and her sense of panic increased. Just then, she saw Eiess standing on the ice above her. What is she doing here? Eiess looked down on Ailia triumphantly, not saying a word. Ailia pounded the ice with her now bleeding fists, but it was useless. She could no longer hold her breath and went to inhale the water.
Just then, Ailia woke up with a scream. She gasped for air and yelled out.
Soren woke up from her scream and was quickly by her side. “What is wrong?” he asked, his eye scanning her face. 
Ailia covered her face with her hands and exhaled loudly. “It was just a dream. I’m sorry I woke you,” she said, dropping her hands into her lap. “It just seemed so real. I don’t want to talk about it,” she finally said. A new mare dream to worry about.
“That is fine. You do not have to tell me if you do not want to,” Soren said. 
Soren looked at Ailia and his gentle gaze had a calming effect on her. She decided to tell him about her dream anyway, even though she feared that he might react negatively to it like her former friends had. “I have these—recurring mare dreams about an evil Empress,” she started. “I don’t even know if she exists, but if I did ever meet her, I’m sure I would feel just as terrified as I do in my dreams. I actually started dreaming about her years ago. Her name is Eiess,” Ailia said.
Soren’s eyes flinched. “Ah, I see,” Soren said.
Ailia braced for a lecture, or for him to tell her she was possessed or cursed, or for him to start ignoring her. That is how all her friends had reacted when she had shared her dream with them. Aunt Unni was really the only one who had listened to her dreams without judgment.
“She does indeed exist and she is as terrifying as she is rumored to be,” Soren said.
Ailia’s heart overlooked a beat. “What?” she said. “She exists and you know of her?” She felt relieved that she was not crazy after all. But then she felt terrified that Eiess actually existed.
Soren nodded. “She is the one who usurped the Northlandic Throne about four months ago and instigated the eternal winter,” Soren said. “She is as real and as evil as they come.”
Ailia couldn’t believe what she was hearing. Now her mare dreams took on a whole new dimension. If she hadn’t been terrified before, she definitely was now. “So, you’ve met her?” Ailia tried not to sound too shocked about this information, but her mouth had become so dry, she was hardly able to get a word out. 
“Yes, on several occasions,” Soren said.
“How did you meet her?” Ailia continued as she sat up a little straighter, her appetite whetted with curiosity. She had finally met someone who could help her make sense of her dreams and excitement grabbed hold of her.	
 	“You should get some sleep. You need rest so your leg can heal,” Soren said, looking concerned for Ailia.
She wasn’t having it. She’d waited years for an explanation to her mare dreams. “I’m wide awake; I couldn’t sleep even if my life depended on it,” Ailia said. Then she realized that her life actually might depend on her getting enough rest. “Well, you know what I mean,” she added flippantly. 
“All right,” Soren said. He got some fresh branches and firewood and started a new fire next to the other watered-down one. “Well, the first time I met Eiess was when I was a young boy, nine, maybe ten years old. I was living in Trollsoe at the time with my parents and three brothers. Eiess was riding in her sleigh with another woman, a young woman who looked absolutely petrified. I remember it so vividly, because I was struck by Eiess’s beauty. I had heard about her from my parents, but I did not really understand who she was until much later. In my young mind, I had envisioned Eiess to look absolutely terrifying. When her sleigh passed through my village, I recall being impressed by how enormous it was. Her light green eyes pierced right through me, as she slowly sleighed by and she smiled at me,” Soren said. 
Ailia could see by his facial expression that he was envisioning the moment clearly and she knew exactly what he meant by her piercing green eyes. 
Then he continued pensively. “It was not a normal, happy smile, but a smile that looked as if she knew what great evil was going to happen to me in the future, like she had planned to destroy my future somehow. I wonder if she knew then exactly who I was and what wicked vices she would curse me with.”
Who is Soren really? Ailia wanted to know.
“She never was one to look after the well-being of another.” Soren stopped and looked at Ailia directly. “We never spoke that day, but her glare left a lasting and chilling impression on my soul, an impression that terrified me even more than any threatening beast or demon could have.”
Ailia felt gripped by his experience and she was strongly drawn to learn more about the Empress. “She sounds so—mystical,” Ailia said for a lack of better words. “You said that that was the first time you saw her?” 
“The second time I met Eiess was years later. My wife, Lucia, and I had just moved into our first home in Trollsoe and we had been married the first time for almost two years.”
Wasn’t that the name he mentioned when he saw me? “The first time?” Ailia asked, thinking that was strange.
“I will explain later,” he said. “Lucia was from Trollsoe and another Sentinor had told Lucia’s mother and father, before she was born, that another baby, a boy, would also be born in Trollsoe and that we were separated Spiritus Amor. That boy was me.”
“Spirit Am—?” Ailia asked confused. “Iluxia?” She didn’t want to let Soren know she had dreamt of Iluxia also, since she didn’t know whether or not to trust Soren completely yet.
“Spiritus Amor,” he corrected gently. “When our souls were formed in the immaterial realm, we were created together, as soul mates, if you may, but two separate individuals to perfectly fulfill each other’s incompleteness.”
“How do you know that?” Ailia asked. She regretted asking the question immediately, afraid he wouldn’t share more if she became too skeptical. “I mean, I cannot fathom how anyone would be able to remember the pre-mortal sphere, if there even actually is one,” she said. “I’m not saying I don’t believe you though.” He seems too sincere to be just making this up.
“Well, I do not exactly remember it, but Iluxia has told me about it.”
 “Who is Iluxia?” Ailia asked. “Isn’t he just a myth?”
“Iluxia is the leader of the lightelves. He lives in Alvheim. He is one of Midgard’s four protecting Sentinors, who covenanted at the beginning of the Midgard’s creation to protect humanity. He also helps change the seasons with the Sun Queen and the Aesira Jewel.”
“I thought the Aesira Jewel was just a myth too,” Ailia said. 
“No. The Aesira Jewel was created by Iluxia and it has been passed down through the generations of Sun Queens since the beginning of Midgard’s existence. Iluxia captured seven thousand years of the sun’s rays and soldered it into an eternal jewel called the Aesira Jewel. The jewel was created for one purpose and that was to keep the balance of Midgard’s seasons, so humanity could survive. To release the Aesira Jewel’s powers, the jewel’s key and the jewel needed to be used by Iluxia and the Sun Queen of the Aesira bloodline.”
“Where is the Aesira Jewel now?” Ailia asked, thinking, of course he’s not going to tell me.
“We are not sure. It has been missing since Eiess took over the throne. Usually, the Jewel has been kept hidden by the Sun Queen, so that no one can misuse its powers.”
“So, why are you telling me about all this? Maybe I’ll try to steal the Jewel—and how do you know about the jewel anyway?” Ailia asked suspiciously, her hands hitting her hips.
“Maybe I will tell you some other time,” Soren said in a teasing tone.
Ailia squinted her eyes.
Soren paused and looked at Ailia as if he had just realized something.
“What?” she asked inquisitively, feeling self-conscious from the way he studied her face.
“I think the reason I feel I can be so open with you, is that you remind me of someone I used to know.” His intense blue eyes studied her face. He shook his head and huffed.
“What?” Ailia endured. There was a long pause. Finally Ailia retorted, “Well, actually, you don’t have to tell me if you don’t want to.” 
He looked at her and they both smiled at their newly created inside joke. “So, the same year we moved into our new home—was the same year that Eiess destroyed the entire town of Trollsoe, letting her Surtorians rage through the streets and scorch the city unrecognizable.” He paused. 
Ailia thought about her dream and how similar it sounded to his story. Should I tell him? Suddenly Ailia could see a noticeable change in Soren’s countenance. His breathing became even shallower and his eyes looked down and it looked like he brewed with despondency. 
“Lucia and I had discovered Eiess living in the Black Glaciers at the time and had started planning our attack. Lucia, being the Great Sentinor, created and destined to destroy Eiess, became a serious threat to the Empress and the Empress quickly came up with a plan to kill Lucia,” Soren said.
Ailia had heard about the Great Sentinor when she was a child, but she didn’t really believe that the stories she had heard about her were actually true. “You mean the Great Sentinor is actually a real person?” she asked. This is a lot to take in.
“Yes.”
“So she really, truly exists?” she asked again to make sure she heard him right. 
Soren laughed. “Yes.”
“Are you a—Sentinor?”
Soren paused and looked away. “Yes,” he finally said. “One of the original four.”
A Sentinor. She felt honored to be in his presence. “What happened to your wife?” Ailia asked concerned. 
“Eiess cold-bloodedly murdered her,” he said. “The Empress was determined to destroy Lucia, so Eiess devised a plan to kill her while I was away on one of my trips. Eiess followed Lucia and her friends one day when they went out for an afternoon walk. Lucia liked to go down by the fjord and read and write poetry with them. Eiess captured them all and drowned them one by one, forcing Lucia to watch each of her friends die. She saved Lucia until the end and took her out on a boat into Trollsoe Fjord. When she had reached far out into the fjord, she threw Lucia in the water with a weight tied around her ankles. When Lucia hit the bottom of the fjord, she was able to undo the knot on the weight and began to swim to the surface. Right before she reached the surface, Eiess froze the fjord over, so Lucia became trapped under the ice. She drowned,” he said and paused. 
Ailia froze and she felt all the blood leave her face. 
“You do not look too well. I hope I have not frightened you with my story,” Soren said.
“No, I, uh—I just—My mare dream last night and my other mare dream was about what you just described,” she said disturbed. “Except—I was the one—who was underneath the ice.”
“You mean your dream last night was about the exact thing I am telling you right now?” Soren’s eyes popped open.
“Well, not exactly. There were other things that happened. I was to be baptized into the Christian church, but at the end, before I arose from baptism, I was trapped underneath the water, underneath all the ice the Empress had created and she was looking down on me,” Ailia said distressed, remembering her dream.
“Oh,” Soren said, looking surprised and without an initial response. He then gazed into the fire for a long while. “Eiess has this bizarre ability to go into people’s dreams and pull out information, truths if you will, from their thoughts, or even to give people information she wants them to know. You said you never met her, right?”
“No, never.” Ailia shook her head. “I didn’t even know she actually existed until just now.”
“Well, she certainly knows about you!” Soren nearly yelled. 
Ailia looked at him in disbelief. 
“She must know something important about you or want something from you. It is vital we find out what that is before she gets hold of you.”
“Gets hold of me? You mean she’ll come after me?” Ailia asked, frightened at the thought of having such a one pursue her.
“Most likely—if what you are telling me about your dream is true,” Soren said.
“Well, I’ll just run away then,” Ailia blurted out, throwing her arms up. Her heart had begun to race and she felt anxiety start to grow stronger. Though Ailia didn’t know much about the Empress, she knew one thing for certain: she was no match for Eiess. 
“I do not want to alarm you, but Eiess will find you no matter how far you run away. She has thousands of Surtorians roaming every dark corner in Midgard. She has had thousands of years to build her legions and if she sets her mind on doing something, she will get it done. There is no winning when one competes with Eiess. The only person who can do that is the Great Sentinor,” Soren said. “Even she has failed and lost her life twice already.”
“I don’t want to have any part of this. I just want to go home and live my life like I have before,” Ailia said perturbed, the knot in her stomach tightening by the second.
“Then she will surely find you,” Soren spoke softly. He looked Ailia sincerely in the eyes and touched her arm.
She felt a wave of energy go through her body at his touch. Had he felt it too?
“If you let me, I would like to help you solve this enigma. It has been my life’s mission to conquer the Empress and I think it would be very interesting to learn why there is a link between you and her.” He waited for her answer.
Ailia didn’t like that there was a link. “Why do I have to deal with this? I’m a nobody. My parents died when I was young and I have no wealth, no influence and no connections. I don’t even have any real friends!  This is just—oh—just wrong!” She stood up and limped back and forth. Finally after fretting for a few minutes, she calmed down and settled into standing on her left, non-injured leg. “Why?” she exhaled. She couldn’t help from crossing her arms. “I’m a nobody,” she said again.
“Well, the Empress must know something about you that you do not know,” Soren reiterated. “That something is what we need to uncover.”
Ailia thought for a moment, but she couldn’t come up with anything intelligent to say about the subject. “Well, what do we do?” she said, feeling defeated.
“I will help you get home to your family in Bergendal and you should let your leg heal before we decide to do anything. Maybe your family has some insight into this puzzle and of your disappearance,” Soren said. 
Ailia nodded her head in agreement, even though she knew she wanted no part of this.
“Just remember: you are not alone. There are many, many others who would like for nothing more than to see the Empress dead and gone.”
“All right,” she said, slightly more calm. “You must promise to help me. I cannot do this alone.” 
“I promise,” Soren said.
The sun had begun to reveal itself from beyond the horizon. Soren rolled up the furs, while he had Ailia sit on the upside down turned pot. Placing his thumb and index finger inside his mouth, he whistled loudly. 
A large black horse came running from the woods. The horse’s silky mane waived in the wind and its hooves hammered the ground in a perfect rhythm, kicking up the snow behind him, as he gracefully trotted in their direction. 
The horse stopped right by them and Soren firmly grabbed him by the reigns, stroking him briskly on the neck. The horse neighed in acceptance, as his warm breath turned to curly smoke circles in the chill air. 
“This is Volomite, my trusted companion,” Soren said.
“Glad to meet you, Volomite,” Ailia said, feeling he needed a proper salutation and curtsied. 
“I will have you ride him, but we will want to move slowly to not agitate your wound.” Soren secured a brown, worn saddle and attached his pots, bags and furs on it. “That should do it,” he said. “May I help you get up?” He gestured with his hands for her to mount Volomite. 
Ailia limped closer to Soren and let him put his hands around her waist. She reached for his shoulders and placed her hands securely on top of them. She could feel his warm breath on her face and the warmth from his body. Standing this close to him, she could see that his two, maybe three-day beard had traces of gray in it. Ailia thought he seemed too young to have any gray. Underneath his over-coat, she could feel his strong shoulder muscles. She blushed, thinking it had been a long time since she had been this close to a man, at least as far as she could remember. A feeling of longing overcame her and she felt herself drawn to Soren and wanted to stay in his arms for a little longer. 
“Ready?” he asked.
“Yes,” Ailia replied and looked up into his bottomless, blue eyes. There was something so familiar about him and it drove her crazy that she couldn’t figure it out. 
Soren hoisted her up like she weighed nothing. “I am going to walk with Volomite the first while, until we get out of these wretched glaciers. He does not do too well traveling on the slick ice,” he said.
Volomite neighed as if in appreciation. 
“How is your leg feeling?” Soren asked, looking up at her.
Ailia’s leg had started hurting again, but she decided to not complain about the pain. “Just fine,” she said.
“Here,” Soren said, handing her some type of dark colored bread. “Eat this. It will help with the hunger and the pain.” 
Ailia had not noticed how hungry she was until now. She gratefully accepted the food and started eating. 
“Let us just hope we do not run into any brigands. That will be the death of us,” Soren said as he began walking.
Ailia clenched the handkerchief in her pocket and considered throwing it out without him noticing. I do not want him thinking that I am associated with brigands or the Vik people.



10

The King’s Sacrifice

Many men have died here. 
Lucia did not keep track of how long she had been imprisoned in the southwestern tower, but she knew it had been more than three months—maybe even four. She was in and out of consciousness and had developed a persistent cough and her body vacillated between freezing and profuse bouts of sweating. 
Every few days a Surtorian would come in and beat her in some way. Today, it had been more painful than usual. Lucia scanned her fingers across her pained forehead, exploring the sore parts. “Ouch,” she said when her fingers reached the large, bruised area close to her hairline. She pulled her hand away and saw that it was covered in blood. As she wiped it onto the floor, she looked around the ghostly room and saw her fur overcoat lying on the floor in a bundle in the corner. She stood up slowly, walked over to it, picked it up and wrapped herself in it. It had not held up well and it was now so dirty that it was hard to believe it had ever been white. There was a small barred opening for a window in the thick stone walls. Attached to it were poorly constructed, grayed, wood shutters that were wide open. She walked over and shut them to keep the cold and unyielding wind out. There was no bed in the room for her to sleep or rest on, only dried golden straw scattered on the floor and in the corners. The room smelled of urine, straw and old fabric.
Her father’s worried face flashed before her eyes. I hope he is still alive. That hope was the only thing that had kept her alive since Eiess had imprisoned her. She listened to see if she could hear him, or anyone, but the only thing she heard was the whistling sound the wind made as it came in through the angular cracks in the shutters. 
“Father,” she yelled like she had every day since she had entered this foul prison. “Father, are you here?” She paused and waited, her ear listening at the dense oak door. No sound. No answer. There never had been an answer and lately, she had started to think that there never would be one. A loneliness she had known many times rumbled in her heart. She buried her face in her hands and shrank to the floor with her back toward the door. “Odin, Thor?” she said. “If you are there, show me a sign!” Only more silence responded. 
Twice per day, a guard would fling a tray with some leftover fish or game and moldy, dry bread. She recognized the guard named Ivar, one of her father’s previous guards, and she could not believe that he was now serving her father’s worst enemy. She had tried to speak with him, but he never responded, only shut the door and ignored Lucia’s pleadings. The door opened forcefully and another tray was pushed in.
“Please help me. Where is my father? Do you know anything? Is he alive? Please, I just need to know,” Lucia cried. The door slammed shut and she could hear the guard locking it with his clattering keys. There was a pause and then she heard the guard unlock the door. 
I hope he does not beat me! She frantically moved away from the door and cowered to the corner.
Ivar opened the door and stood in the doorway, covering the entrance with his muscular body. “Your father is alive. He is in the queen’s chamber.” He stepped back and slammed the door shut again. 
“Thank you, thank you,” Lucia said. He is alive! My father is alive! No words had ever sounded sweeter. She waited to hear the keys lock the door again, but nothing happened. It was silent for a moment. Then, she heard Ivar leave, his heavy boots colliding with the stone floor. Did he leave it unlocked on purpose? The steps grew quieter and quieter, until finally they were gone. Lucia felt extremely tired and ill, not to mention beaten, but she forced herself to stand up and walk over to the door. She stood there for a moment to catch her breath. With her ear placed on the door, she listened for any movement or sound. It was completely silent. She carefully opened the door, peeked through the crack and became discouraged when she saw that it was pitch black in the hallway. She listened again. No sound. She slowly opened the door enough so she could squeeze through it and then stepped into the corridor. 
After taking a few steps, she reached the spiral stairwell. The light from the open door behind her was all that she had to go by, which was not much. She did not recall how many steps there were, but she knew she only had to go down one level to get to the main hallway. Slowly, she curved around the cylinder shaped wall with her back against it, her hands feeling their way against the cold, rough surface as she descended the stairs. It looked like the torches had been lit in the hallway as the dim flickering glow painted the walls. 
When she reached the bottom of the stairs she paused and listened again for voices. She heard Eiess and an unfamiliar man conversing in the throne room. Could this really be happening? If she kept herself hidden, she might escape today. The thought excited her and frightened her. But she knew she needed to check on her father first. Lucia decided to head down the hallway and check the queen’s chamber for her father before she attempted to escape the castle. She ran as quickly as she could toward the room, but she had to stop when a coughing attack overtook her aching body. She covered her mouth with the inside of her elbow to muffle the uncontrollably loud coughs but kept moving to make use of her valuable time.
“Halt!” she heard behind her. She glanced behind her and saw another guard, one she did not recognize, charging toward her. Lucia stepped up her pace as she neared the queen’s chamber. Another guard was standing at the door and as she approached him, she could see that it was Ivar. He did not react to her when he saw her, but instead he let her pass by and even held the door open for her to enter the chamber.
“Ivar, grab that girl!” the other guard yelled. 
Ivar stepped forward into the hallway instead of following Lucia into the chamber as she had expected he would. 
She heard Ivar draw his sword behind her and prepared for the worst. He is going to kill me!  
The unknown guard fell to the floor after a loud grunt as Ivar pulled the bloody sword out of his abdomen. 
“Your Majesty,” Ivar said, bowing to Lucia.
Lucia’s body was shaking violently. She had never felt so sick in her whole life.
“There is little time. We must get Lucia out of here now!” Olav said to Ivar. 
Lucia turned around and saw her father sitting by the desk at the window. She started coughing again, her whole body shuddering this time with the convulsions. “Father,” she said in between coughs. “You are alive! Are you ill? You look so pale. Oh, what did they do to you?” She had never been happier to see him.
“You must leave now, Lucia. The Empress will kill you if she finds you here,” King Olav said.
“What about you?” Lucia asked.
“I will be fine. The Empress will not harm me, but you must go.”
Lucia embraced her father where he sat. She wondered why he was not standing up. Was he injured? She had never seen him this vulnerable before, so ill and beaten up. He had always been so strong, so powerful and commanding. His vulnerability touched her. He is just a man after all. To Lucia, her father had always seemed immortal, a man of strength who could overcome anything or anyone. She was not used to seeing him so helpless and lifeless, at the mercy of another being. “No,” she said stubbornly.
“Listen, Ivar will take you part way through the secret escape tunnel underneath that rug there,” Olav said, pointing to the floor. “Eiess does not know it exists. Ivar and I have prepared a bag for you to take. It contains supplies, gold, food and a Bergendal-crested ring,” Olav said, pointing to a bag on the floor. “You will need it when you get out. There is also a change of clothes for you in the bag. You must change into these before you exit the castle. No one will recognize you in them. Ivar has arranged for Nora to meet you at the other end.” 
Lucia immediately felt better when she heard that Nora was still alive and that she would see her soon. 
“She will wait for you outside the exit tonight. You need to go now. Be safe. I will see you again soon,” Olav said.
Lucia hugged her father and tried unsuccessfully to hold back her tears—weeks, no months of accumulated loneliness coming out at once.
“I love you, Lucia, always remember that. And if you do what is right, everything will turn out for the best in the end,” Olav comforted.
He seemed a much gentler man than Lucia remembered. “Promise I will see you soon?” she said, all her hate and anger for him melting away in his embrace.
“Yes, very soon, I promise,” Olav said.
Ivar walked over to the rug and peeled it back, exposing a trap door in the floor. He lifted the square door by a cord and revealed stairs in the floor. 
Lucia never knew that it was there. She was stunned. She ran over and hugged her father again. “I will be so angry with you if you are lying to me,” she said, pounding his chest. They embraced again and she forced herself to walk over to the trap door. She turned around and looked at her father one last time. A feeling of death came over her. It was the same feeling she had felt at her mother’s burial: a lonely feeling of forced surrender and utter helplessness. Despair could not even begin to explain it. “I love you!” She swallowed back new tears that were just waiting to flow and silenced her desperate cry to appear brave in her father’s eyes. 
Ivar descended the stairs and his torch lit the way in the dim tunnel. Lucia turned around and followed Ivar. The smell of mold and putrid earth rose into the room. There were old spider webs, dust and mud covering the stone walls. The stairs only continued partway down until they came to a dirt pathway. It became completely black when Olav closed the trap door behind them. 
Suddenly, yells came from inside the castle. Lucia feared that her father had lied to her to save her life. Is he safe from Eiess? A heaving cry grew in her and could not be contained inside. 
Probably seeing what was happening, Ivar walked over to Lucia and placed his hand over her mouth dampening the cry’s release. 
“Guards!” They heard Eiess yell from the castle. “Guards!” She seemed to scream in frustration when they did not respond immediately. Eiess had clearly found the dead guard in the hallway. Lucia heard heavy, rushed footsteps make their way to the scene and she heard Eiess frantically yell and order them to search the castle, check on the princess and gather a search troop. 
“Do not worry. All will be well,” Ivar said. “You will hide here until it has quieted down, and then you will sneak out the back exit, just a little ways from here. I will not fit in that tight tunnel before the exit, so you will need to go alone after that. Remember, Nora is waiting for you there.” 
Lucia was grateful she had a familiar, strong man accompanying her. 
“You should go change over there,” Ivar said, pointing down the hallway. “I will wait here.” 
It was a welcome distraction. Lucia walked a few steps into the dark and opened her bag. She could make out a few items in the dim light of Ivar’s torch. It looked like there was a small purse containing gold pieces, some food and some clothing and other material. She pulled the clothes out of the bag, set the bag down on the ground and started to change her attire. Her body shivered from both the cold and nerves. The clothes looked like regular men’s peasant clothing, rough and worn, but she could not make out the color in the darkness. Unexpectedly her cough came back and she had to stop changing in order to calm her sick body. When she was finished, she went back over to Ivar. 
“Your father instructed me to remind you that you have been sanctified with a great responsibility to help rid this country of the evils of Eiess. He wrote you this letter and you must read it after you are in a safe place, far from here.” Ivar handed her a sealed scroll addressed to her and then stopped and listened. It sounded like more guards had been recruited and they were probably searching the castle for Lucia. “Come,” he whispered, waving her to follow him down the passageway. 
Lucia followed him as closely as she could, clutching her father’s precious words in her hands. Maybe they will be his last.
Finally, they came to a small opening at the end of the passageway. Ivar waived his torch across the wall as his eyes searched for something. He started brushing off the dirt and the dust and uncovered an inscription on the wall. He mumbled as he read it. Then, he knelt down on the ground and brushed some dirt off of something that looked like an iron gate, or a door of some sort. It appeared to be welded shut. “Hold this,” Ivar said, handing Lucia the torch. He pulled a large key from his satchel and placed the key in the only hole.
It had no resemblance of a keyhole and Lucia would have never thought to place the key there. 
Suddenly, the door elevated out of the ground on its own. When it stopped, Ivar pushed the square iron box to the side and exposed a framed hole in the ground. 
It was dark, but Lucia could feel the cool breeze from the outside entering in. 
“This is the way out. It is a tight, curved squeeze, but you are small. You must enter head first, as the path will later curve horizontally and then straight up like so,” he said, demonstrating with his hands. “There is no room to change directions in the tunnel.”
Lucia felt her face cringing. It sounded daunting.
“Remember, Nora is waiting on the other side. You will need to knock four times like so,” he said and showed her on the stone wall. “She will remove the bushes and open the gate on the other side. Wait until she opens it. Nora will need to make sure the path is clear of any unwanted persons. Knock only four times, no matter how long it takes for her to open. Do you understand?” His eyebrows furrowed.
“Yes,” Lucia said quietly.
“And you must not return into the castle, for Eiess will surely have your head,” he said. “It has been a pleasure serving you, Your Majesty. May the gods watch over and protect you on your journey and until we meet again.”	
 	“Please watch over my father. I cannot bear to lose another loved one,” she implored, even though she almost felt in her heart that he was already dead.	
“I will protect King Olav, even with my own life,” Ivar said. 
Lucia thanked him, took a deep breath and crawled into the dark dirt hole in the ground. There were a lot of small pebbles in the tight passage and Lucia barely fit through the hole. First she traveled straightway down into the ground; deeper and deeper the path of the cavity took her. After minutes of being upside down, with blood uncomfortably rushing to and settling in her head, the path straightened out and became easier to maneuver. Then she saw a dim light in the distance. Welcome and encouraging moonlight peered through the tunnel. 
Lucia finally reached the end of the tunnel and it curved very sharply upwards. She tried to squeeze through the last part, but got stuck. She retreated back into the tunnel and tried another way but got stuck again. Her cough came back and she tried to muffle it as best she could. Finally, she became lodged so tight that she could not move backwards or forwards. I hope I do not miss Nora. She was so tired of crying, but she could not help the tears from coming, realizing she had become immovably wedged just a few feet away from her freedom. 
Mother, please help me! Lucia pleaded. Just then, a small rock loosened and it gave her just enough room to squeeze free. She twisted through the last space and entered a small, cave-like opening, containing the escape iron-barred gate Ivar had described. She saw dead bushes covered in snow in front of the opening. 
Will Nora really be there waiting for me? She knocked four times as loudly as she could. It was hard to make a loud enough sound on the thick iron gate. Lucia hoped that Nora had heard her. She waited and waited. Nothing. Not a sound. Not a movement. She felt another cough attack coming on and buried her mouth in her sleeve to dampen the noise. It lasted longer than usual this time. She reached through the barred gate and picked up some snow, placing it in her mouth and letting it melt on her tongue. The liquid helped with the cough. She patiently anticipated Nora would open for her. Should I knock again? It was going to be a long, cold night if Nora did not come, but she still decided not to knock again. 
Fortunately, her father had enough foresight and had placed a thin wool blanket in the bag to keep her warm in the cold tunnel. She took it out and wrapped her cold body tightly in it. The blanket felt warm compared to how thin it looked. 
To make time go by faster, Lucia emptied the bag her father had given her. The Bergendal crested ring glowed in the moonlight. She tried again to envision her being the Great Sentinor, but all she felt was loneliness and despair. 





11

Journey the Land 

Ailia’s bottom had become sore from sitting on the saddle for so long, but she felt she couldn’t complain since she was sitting and Soren had been walking on the slick ground for hours. 
“I cannot believe how vast the glaciers were,” Ailia said, adjusting in her seat, trying futilely to find a comfortable position. 
“They are not really that vast, but they take a long time to cross because they are so slick,” Soren said, walking faster than before as he held on to the reins. 
They had reached the end of the Blue Glaciers and entered the Northland Forest, but Soren had told Ailia he would keep walking instead of burden his horse with another rider. 
The forest’s evergreen trees stood lifeless, starved and skeleton-like, stripped of their previous majestic grandeur. Freezing wind breezed by, swaying the bare evergreen trees gently from side to side. About an hour ago, the sun had reached its pathetically low peak and was now leaving the dimming sky streaked with bright pink, flaming clouds.
“My Uncle Brander says that the evergreen trees in this forest are enchanted with trolls and fairies,” Ailia said. “I used to come here with him all the time when I was a child.”
“And—” Soren started.
Ailia interrupted him. “I hope you’re not going to tell me that trolls and fairies exist too. If you do, I’ll never believe you,” Ailia said, smiling.
He laughed. “No, trolls are just the human’s way of glorifying giants and dwarves. I was about to say and now the Northland Forest is a ghost of what it used to be,” Soren said. 
“I heard once that the Vik people lived in this forest. Is that true?” Ailia asked. 
“You mean the Vikings?” Soren asked. 
“I suppose—” Ailia said, not familiar with that name. “Is it the same thing?”
“The plunderers from the Vik district have recently been nicknamed Vikings. No one knows exactly where they’ve settled, or even if they have settled, but I highly doubt that they are here,” Soren said. “Not to change the subject, but do you know that most southerners consider all of us northerners Vikings, whether we plunder or not?”
“Huh?” Ailia said. “I suppose the southerners don’t know that some of us northerners don’t condone that type of barbaric behavior.”
“No,” Soren said. “We are all the same to them.”
 “Well, I suppose we view all southerners as overly religious extremists, so we’re not any better,” Ailia said.
Soren laughed. “True.”
Ailia grew serious. “I—think there could be a connection with the Vikings and my disappearance,” she said.
“What makes you think that?”
“Just a hunch,” Ailia said, grabbing hold of the handkerchief and flask in her pocket. She wasn’t sure if she was ready to reveal everything just yet to this stranger.
“Well, you would probably be dead by now if they were involved,” Soren said.
“Yes, most likely,” Ailia agreed on the surface, but she was now wondering why she wasn’t dead. Maybe they thought they had killed me but didn’t succeed. 
“You know, actually, now that I think of it, I do remember hearing a story about a young family that was captured and killed close to these woods,” Soren said. “I do not know if it was Viking related, but it could have been.”
“Really?” Ailia said. Is it safe to be traveling here then?  Ailia looked behind her to make sure they weren’t being followed. “So, if they come from the Vik District, why would they have settled around here?” Ailia wondered.
 “I do not have all the information, but I think I heard that a few Bergendalers joined the Vikings in supporting Eiess. However, after they helped Eiess seize the Northlandic Throne, she betrayed them and cast them out of the castle without the promised reward. They tried to re-settle in Bergendal, but they left shortly after because they were ousted by the people of Bergendal for their treachery.”
I had already gone missing by then, Ailia noted. 
“As they left the city, they vowed with one another to destroy Eiess and the people of Bergendal,” Soren finished.
“Where did they go after that?” Ailia asked. Each moment she grew more and more fearful that the Vikings might be lurking close by.
“That is what no one knows,” Soren said.
“Oh, so they could be here?” Ailia asked, lowering her voice a bit.
Soren looked up at her sitting on Volomite and smiled. “You do not need to worry, you know. It will not make anything better. I will protect you. I guess some girls just need a little extra protecting,” he said playfully.
Ailia took off her wool mitten and threw it at him.
He ducked as the mitten just missed his head.
She took off her second mitten and tried to hit him again, but missed.
“Be careful, Miss Ailia, or your hands will be very cold by the end of this trip.” Soren laughed. 
Ailia looked for something else to throw at him, but stopped looking when Soren handed her mittens back. 
“I think we should break here before we continue,” Soren said. “I am starving. How about you?”
“Sounds good,” Ailia said relieved. His playfulness had helped her lose her nervousness a bit. Did he intend that?
“Now, should I leave you up there indefinitely as payback for trying to hit me with your mittens?” he said, his face looking dead serious.
Ailia smiled guiltily. Did I take it too far?
Soren lifted the fur off from the horse, opened it and laid it on the ground. He then walked over to the right side of Volomite and raised his hands to help Ailia down. 
“You don’t hold grudges very long, do you?” Ailia said.
He smiled and shook his head. “Too much to carry.”
She reached down and grabbed hold of his shoulders as his hands wrapped around her waist. 
Soren set her down on the snow very gently. “My lady,” he said.
Their eyes connected for a moment and Ailia had to catch her breath when her heart fluttered. His eyes seemed to exude compassion and wisdom beyond a human’s capacity. If what he had been telling her was true, it would make sense that she felt this way around him: a half mortal, half god-like being. She still couldn’t help but think that she knew him from somewhere. He almost seemed to be a part of her in some way. Maybe I’ve met him before, but I just don’t remember?
“Here, let me help you sit down on the fur.” He reached one arm around her waist. 
Ailia placed her arm around his shoulder for additional support and they both walked over to the fur that was laid down on the snow. She sat down and tightened her coat closer to her body to stay warm. 
“Drink this,” he said, giving her a wooden bowl filled with liquid. 
The drink was cold, but it was the same one he had given her earlier. It tasted much bitterer this time. 
Soren started a small fire. “I have some more healing herbs for you, but I need to go find one last ingredient, so I can prepare them. Volomite will stay here to keep you company. I will not be long,” he said. “Maybe I will even get lucky and find something else to eat, too.” He snagged his bow and quiver filled with arrows. 
Ailia tried not to think about the Vikings, but she couldn’t help from glancing around.
 “I will be right back. Do not worry. No one is around,” Soren said, walking toward the deeper part of the forest.
Ailia watched Soren walk off into the blackness. Even the way he moved seemed so recognizable, slightly springy in his gait, with confidence in each step. The further away Soren got, the more Ailia appreciated having Volomite close. “I’m glad you’re here,” she said, looking up at him. 
Volomite neighed happily, flicking his long, black tail. 
Ailia listened for any sounds in the forest, but it was completely still, except for a few raven squawks and the soft rush of the wind. She lay down and gazed up into the dark, starlit sky. Radiant, colorful Auroras lit up the heavens with a beautiful dance. She remembered a conversation she had had with her Uncle Brander several years back when she was just eight.
“The Auroras are known as the dancers of the spirits and they connect past generations to current generations to future generations with their light display,” Uncle Brander had said.
 “Well, why are they different colors then?” Ailia had asked.
“The reddish colored Auroras are spirits that have not yet been born and the green and bluish Auroras are spirits that have passed away. When someone dies, a part of their spirit is caught up into the heavens and is transformed into light. It’s the only way for us to see that our loved ones are still with us. So anyone who hopes for a child, or has lost a beloved, can look up into the skies and the Auroras will remind them that the spirit is eternal.”
 The light spectacle soothed Ailia’s fears and she felt at ease in the night. She thought about her birth parents and she wished she could remember them, their faces, their voices and their loving embraces. If only I had one single item from them, a word, or something I could know them by, she wished. She closed her eyes and imagined what they looked like. Gradually, she fell asleep under the stars.
Ailia woke up and saw that the fire had started to grow dim. Where’s Soren? He had been gone longer than she thought he would. Suddenly, she heard rapid footsteps behind her and turned around. Nothing was there, except for the gaping vacuum of a dark abyss. “Soren?” She kept her voice low, so not to attract any attention if there was someone out there. Her breathing grew shallow as she heard the footsteps behind her again, more prominent this time. She cringed and wanted to stand up and run away. Maybe the wolves have returned to finalize their kill?  
Volomite started whinnying fearfully and reared, floundering his front hooves into the air. Ailia stood up and hopped over to him on one leg. She grabbed his mane and pressed herself close up to him to calm him and her both. Panic grabbed hold of her body, escalating her heart rate to twice its normal pace. Where were the footsteps coming from? Is another mare demon visiting me?
The footsteps slowed and finally stopped. Ailia closed her eyes and squeezed herself closer to Volomite’s large body, realizing he was currently her only safeguard against danger. Volomite calmed down and stepped forward, lowering his head as if to greet someone. 
It must be Soren, Ailia thought. “Soren?” she said, cautiously peeking around Volomite’s chest. 
There stood two little boys around two and three years of age, dressed in winter overcoats and fur hats and mittens, their boots caked in snow. They looked at Ailia and seemed to be lost. Ailia remembered the young family that had been killed by the Vikings. I hope these aren’t their spirits still wandering these forests. She quickly pulled her head back behind Volomite and closed her eyes to make sure she was not dreaming. Slowly opening her eyes, she took a deep breath and peeked around Volomite’s massive body again. 
The boys were crying and whimpering now, rubbing their moist eyes. 
Ailia stepped out from behind the horse. “It’s all right; don’t cry. What’s the matter?”
They stopped crying and looked up at Ailia with doleful, puffy eyes. “Mamma,” the older one sobbed, pointing randomly into the air. The younger boy stepped closer to Ailia and raised his hands toward her, as if he wanted to be picked up. Ailia instinctively reached for him and lifted him up. She had to put her weight on her injured leg and felt an intense shooting pain, immediately putting the boy down again. She wobbled over to the cushy fur blanket and sat down, smiling and waving for the children to join her. 
The two babes rushed over, both jumping onto her lap, competing for affection and space.
“Where’s your Mamma?” Ailia asked. 
“Mamma gone,” the older one said. 
Ailia looked around again, but she didn’t see anyone. “You can stay with me until we find her again,” she said, hugging their fidgety, cold bodies. 
The older boy started to fuss and the younger one followed his example.
“Let me sing you a song,” Ailia said. She chose her favorite lullaby.

Sleep now my child, your mamma is here
To hold you so tight and erase all your fears
The kettle is full of porridge for you
Your father is coming and he loves you too.

Sleep now my child, rest your sweet head
On this silk pillow that is in your bed
Watch all the stars in the heav’n so bright
Sleep till you wake to the morning light.

Sleep now my child, your future is set
You will have all the riches a young prince can get
Peace in your soul, love in your core,
Faith in each step from shore to shore.

Sleep now my child, the sun will soon rise
The Lord has secured all family ties
Forever you will be in my heart and my mind
My love is eternal, His love is Divine.

Sleep now my child, do not worry at all
Your needs will be met, no matter how small,
 one day you will grow and not want to stay
Just follow your dreams and you will find your way.

The boys settled down serenely and looked up at Ailia with endearing eyes. 
“I hungry,” the younger boy said. 
Ailia remembered where Soren kept his bread. She stood up and told the boys to stay put. “I have some food for you,” she said, limping over to Volomite, looking inside the leather bag. She found the bread and turned around. 
Ailia was startled to find Soren standing right in front of her. 
“Heavens, you scared me!” she said, hitting his arm.
“Was that you singing?” he asked. “It sounded lovely. Look what I found!” he said proudly, holding up two lifeless snow-white hares by their hind legs.
“Uh, yes, you must have heard me,” she said embarrassed, as she tried to look around him for the children. “The children were—hungry, so I wanted to—give them some bread,” she said, trying to get Soren out of the way. “These sweet boys just appeared out of nowhere and were crying.” She tried to peek around him again. Finally, she walked around him to get to the boys, but to her surprise, they were not there. “Where did the boys go? They were just here!” She limped over to the other side of Volomite and looked for them there. “We’ve got to find them. They were lost and looking for their mother and—Didn’t you see them?” she asked.
“I heard you singing, but I did not see any boys here when I arrived,” Soren said, looking bewildered.
“They were just here. They sat on my lap and said their Mamma was gone and that they were hungry. You must have scared them off! We need to find them. They cannot be out here all by themselves!” Ailia was becoming anxious and she always started rambling when she was nervous. “It would be inhumane to leave them in the forest alone.” Ailia hobbled into the forest, looking for them and Soren followed. They searched the area for some time, but they could not locate the children.
The pressure on Ailia’s injured leg became too much and she sat down in the snow as her eyes kept scanning the forest for the lost boys.
“Shh—” Soren warned unexpectedly. 
Ailia looked at him and listened. They heard men’s voices in the distance and saw torches flickering through the shadowy forest trees. 
Soren signaled to her to be quiet, placing his index finger over his lips. “Shh,” he said, this time very quietly but more severe. He slowly crouched down to the ground. 
“I think they are Vikings,” he mouthed to her. 
Ailia looked at Soren, her eyes widening, as terror filled her core.
The voices and torches were coming closer, but there was no place to hide. Soren signaled to Ailia to follow him. They crawled in the snow, worming themselves toward a hopefully safe shelter from the barbarians. Soren laid down on his right side behind a not nearly large enough rock and gestured to Ailia to do the same. She wiggled over and lay in front of him facing outward. Then, his arms wrapped around her, as he spooned her.
Ailia felt his body heat burning against hers. His rough chin rubbed against her virgin soft cheek and his warm breath felt like a soft feather against her cold skin. It surprised her that her body responded so favorably to his and that she longed to be even closer to him than she was now. Their heartbeats conformed to each other as they lay wait for the Vikings to either pass by or notice them. No danger in the world could make Ailia feel less safe. Not around Soren.
