“You must have been mistaken. There’s no one out here,” one of the men said, standing only a dozen feet away from Soren and Ailia. 
“Well, what about these footprints?” the other said.
Ailia cringed. They know we’re here. We’re dead.
“Those are our footprints you idiot. I don’t want to waste my time out here. If Gunnar wants to search for people who don’t exist in the middle of Odin’s freezing night, he can go do it himself.”
“Well, if we find out later that there was someone here, it’ll be your fault that they got away,” the Viking said. 
“Do me a favor and shut your mouth.”
Ailia could hear them continue to argue, as their voices faded away into the distance. 
 “They are gone,” Soren finally said, still whispering and slowly releasing his grip on her. 
“That was close!” Ailia said.
Soren smiled. “We are fine. Even if we had been discovered, I would have been able to take both of them, no problem.”
“ But—they are Vikings,” Ailia said in disbelief. “They kill for a living.”
“I do not know for sure that they were Vikings,” Soren said. “I do not know how to say this without sounding like a completely overconfident fool, but I could have killed twenty Vikings with the greatest of ease had they discovered us.”
Ailia smirked and tried not to laugh at his ‘humility’. She just nodded her head and bit her lip. “Um-hmm—” she said. Is he being truthful or just big-headed?
“What?” he asked.
“Oh, nothing.”
“No, what?” He smiled.
“You are just so— humble,” Ailia said with a twinkle in her eye.
“Hopefully, I will never have to prove it to you,” Soren said.
Ailia sat up and rose to her feet. She could feel that her injured leg had been used too much and the sharp pain had returned. 
“Vikings fly a Raven banner. It is their crest. I did not see one, did you?”
“No,” she said.
“I did not see one earlier either.”
“What do you mean?” Ailia asked.
“I saw them when I went hunting,” Soren said.
“Why didn’t you tell me there were Vikings close by?” Ailia asked disturbed.
“I did not feel the need to tell you because there was no immediate threat,” Soren said.	
	Ailia felt a little irritated that Soren had not told her he had seen them. “I don’t need protecting, you know.”
Soren stared at her intently. “I beg to differ.”
“Well, not usually, just lately.” Ailia met his eyes but looked away quickly, the intensity of his gaze mesmerized her.
“Just since you met me then?” Soren teased.
Ailia huffed. “Let’s just hope they didn’t see our fire and Volomite,” Ailia said, swerving the question. 
Soren let it pass. “Volomite is usually smart enough to stay away from strangers, but if they find the fire, they will know that someone is here and they will come looking for us.” He rubbed his forehead, looking a little worried. 
Ailia had almost forgotten about the children she had seen. “The boys!” 
“I do not want to alarm you further, but are you sure the boys were—uh—real?” Soren asked.
“Of course they were real. They sat on my lap and cried and I comforted them,” she said, almost insulted.
“Were they—alive?” he continued.
“What do you mean?” Ailia asked, perplexed and annoyed.
“There have been sightings of ghosts here in the Northland Forest: sightings of two young boys who were killed by the Vikings.”
“These boys were not ghosts,” Ailia rebuffed. “I held them in my arms.” 
“Were they cold or warm?” Soren persisted.
“Well, they were—well, they were cold, but it is cold out here.” Ailia thought about it for a minute and realized that, yes they were cold, but they also didn’t have any body heat that she could feel when she held them, unlike Soren. When she was pulled close to him, she could definitely feel his body heat. “I saw them and held them,” she said disbelieving. She knew that spirits held no body heat, but she thought for sure that they couldn’t be held or touched.
“I had a friend who also had seen the boys a few weeks ago and they had vanished as quickly as tonight. I am not saying that you did not see them or hold them, but I think that they could be the little boy spirits trapped here in Midgard without a way to go back home to their parents in Valhalla,” Soren said.
Ailia grew silent because she couldn’t make sense of anything. Maybe the medicine Soren had given her had some hallucinogenic effect on her. 
“You look like you are in pain,” Soren observed, taking a step closer to her.
Ailia thought about trying to remain brave and push through the pain.
“I would be happy to help you back to our camp site,” he said, now serious and not teasing, but only seeming genuinely concerned.
“I think I do need help,” she said, realizing her own limitations. She decided not to dwell on the children, but she hoped that they would reappear sooner or later, so she could help them.
Soren swooped Ailia up into his strong arms and their eyes locked for a split second. Ailia thought she saw something that looked like longing, almost like he was searching for something in her gaze. He quickly looked away and carried her all the way back to the campfire. It had burned out completely. While Ailia sat down, Soren gathered some more firewood and rekindled the flames. He skinned the hares, seasoned them and put them to roast on the fire. 
They smelled heavenly and Ailia was starving. She felt useless as she just sat and watched him do all the work. She wasn’t used to sitting and having someone wait on her and more often than not, she had been the one serving others at home. “I’m sorry I’m not much help. It feels strange to me to not be doing anything,” she apologized.
“I do not mind,” Soren said. “It is good for you to get rest, so you can heal.”
“So, may I ask what happened after your wife—?” She didn’t like how harsh the word died sounded and decided not to finish her question. 
 “Well, I arrived home and was told the news. Shortly after, our first son, Bjorn, died of a mysterious infection.” He paused as if to compose himself. “I swore that I would never rest until I avenged my wife’s death and until Eiess was destroyed.”
“I’m sorry to hear about your loss,” Ailia said caringly. It would be the most horrifying thing to lose one’s wife and child.
“It was so long ago now, but it is still painful to think about,” Soren said.
“How could Eiess kill you or your wife? Aren’t you as powerful as the Empress? You are a Sentinor just like her, right?” Ailia said.
“Well, she has had a lot more time to increase her strength here in Midgard. The rules are different here than in Alvheim or Vanaheim, the realm Eiess is from. Eiess became involved with black art magic even before she came to Midgard and she has grown very powerful over millennia now.”
 “Can’t you just kill yourselves and you would be free and together again?” Ailia asked. “Not that I would want you to kill yourself, but I just thought you might be happier if you were with Lucia again.”
“Well, it is not that simple. My wife and me together are the only ones who can defeat the Empress. If we both killed ourselves, we would be leaving Midgard in the hands of Eiess and we covenanted in Alfheim to protect humanity. It is a commitment I am willing to endure all things for.”
“Does she look the same every time?” Ailia was spellbound by his story. She rested her chin in her palm and leaned forward. “Your wife, I mean?”
“Yes and no. She always has hazel eyes and reddish-brown hair. She actually looks like you a little bit,” he added as he studied Ailia’s face.
Ailia blushed and looked down. 
“Do you remember the incident when most young women disappeared in the Northlandic Kingdom about twenty-eight, twenty-nine years ago? Eiess took all women under the age of twenty, probably to be killed.” 
“No, well, I actually hadn’t been born yet,” Ailia responded. “But my aunt and uncle told me about the horrible story. Only a few made it out alive they said. My aunt was over thirty at the time, so she wasn’t taken.”
“The whole story was a lie. Eiess, still an unknown figure to most people in the Northlandic Kingdom at the time, led everyone to believe that it was the Vik People who headed the attack and kidnapped all the young women. But I have reason to believe that it was her. I was on an errand for someone when Eiess sent her Surtorians out to round up all the women.” Soren flipped the hares around to roast on the other side.
“I just don’t understand why she would do something like that,” Ailia said, shaking her head with a furrowed brow. 
“Well, it makes sense if you are as greedy and power hungry as Eiess. Think about it: the prophecy states that the Great Sentinor would defeat her. If Eiess does not know where or who the Great Sentinor is, she will do anything to have her exterminated.” 
“What did she do to the women?” Ailia asked.
“No one know, but they disappeared forever.”
 Ailia thought back to Lucia. “So, has Lucia been re-born many times?”
“This past time was her third time,” Soren said.
“Do you know where she is now? It’s not Princess Lucia, is it?”
Soren nodded. “Eiess has Lucia imprisoned somewhere with her father, King Olav. Nobody knows where Eiess keeps them, or even if they are still alive. I have tried everything in my power to try and break into the castle, but to no avail.”
Ailia could see the pain in his eyes.
“Ironic, is it not? I have waited for three-quarters of a century for her to be born again and she disappears the eve before I was going to formally propose.”
“I’m so sorry to hear,” Ailia said. He’s had a rough life.
Soren gave her a ‘thank you’ look. “I believe Eiess has the Aesira Jewel with Lucia and King Olav in the Northlandic Castle. That is where it was last seen. It makes for a dangerous combination.”
“Can’t Iluxia tell you where Lucia is?” Ailia asked.
“No. He only sees her spirit right before she enters this life and merges with her mortal body—like a light, he told me once.” Soren took the hares off the fire and cut them up. He placed a nice sized portion onto two different wood plates. 
Ailia’s mouth watered, as the scent of fresh meat inundated the air.
“Here you are. I hope this will fill you up,” Soren said, handing it to her.
“Thank you.” Ailia accepted the meat and started eating. It was the best meat she had ever tasted. 
Soren poured her a cup of wine from his wine sack into a wooden bowel. “This may be too strong for you,” he warned as he handed it to her.
Ailia was accustomed to drinking strong mead and wine. She gulped deeply from the bowl, but quickly realized that this drink was much stronger than what she had been raised on. She coughed violently. 
Soren cracked a crooked smile. “Are you all right?” 
“Yes.” She coughed again. “I’m fine,” she said embarrassed, wiping the wine off her face.
“After repast we should be getting on our way again, so we can get out of this forest sooner rather than later,” Soren said.
Ailia’s mouth was full again with another bite of hare meat and she nodded her head in agreement.
“We are lucky the Vikings have not found us yet,” Soren continued. 
“Let’s make sure we keep it that way,” Ailia said strongly. She took another sip of the strong drink, feeling it burn her throat as she swallowed.
Soren smiled. “You surprise me Ailia.” 
“What do you mean?” Did I say something strange?
“Maybe I will tell you someday.” Soren took another bite of his meal.
“Would you stop saying that? No time like the present.” She raised her eyebrow and wouldn’t take no for an answer.
“Well, you remind me a little bit of Lucia, my wife. You have a great sense of humor, you are strong, yet surprisingly compassionate and fragile and—”
“What?” Ailia liked hearing what he had to say about her.
“Do not take this the wrong way, but your spirit is so much stronger than I think you realize,” he finished.
Ailia smiled softly. And you are so much more wonderful than any man I have ever met. 
After the meal, Soren packed up the equipment and food and helped Ailia back up on the horse.
“Are you sure you do not want to ride?” Ailia asked, invitingly.
“No,” he said matter of fact. “It is best that I walk.”
Ailia felt slightly rejected.



12

Widow

Soren and Ailia rode in silence for the better part of an hour. 
“It is best to travel quietly and not attract any attention from the Vikings or any other possible lurking danger,” Soren had said before they started their journey again. 
As they neared the Small Mountains, snowflakes started falling from the cloudy, white sky. 
Finally, Soren stopped. His eyes searched the landscape. “It looks like a major snow storm is headed in our direction,” he said. “I think we need to look for shelter and, if weather permits, continue our voyage tomorrow.”
“Well, I’ll welcome a break,” Ailia said.
They rode on for a while and finally came to an old cabin next to a frozen-over lake. Smoke rose from the cabin’s snow-covered roof and Ailia was excited that they could maybe spend a night indoors. She got the distinct feeling that it had been a very long time since she had experienced the comforts of a home.
Soren walked up to the cabin and knocked on the old wood door. Dirty rags had been stuffed into holes in the wood and an ancient, broken rocking chair sat on the halfway-rotted porch. He waited patiently for an answer. 
Finally, a middle-aged woman, dressed in black, cracked the door open and stuck her head through the opening. Her face was etched with deep scars and Ailia immediately wondered what her life’s story was. The woman’s long, troll-like nose sat as the centerpiece of her face and her eyes were energetic and clear. Over her graying hair she wore a black scarf embroidered with gray flowers. She clasped her hands together when she saw Soren. “What do you want?” she asked, her voice raspy and unwelcoming.
“We are travelling to Bergendal and my friend is injured,” Soren said, pointing to Ailia who was still sitting on Volomite’s back. “We need shelter for the night. Do you have room?” 
“You aren’t welcome here,” the woman said and then slammed the door shut. 
Soren knocked again, no answer. He knocked again more fervently and the woman opened the door again. 
“Leave me be; I don’t want visitors,” she snapped impatiently.
“Will this help?” Soren said, holding out a small leather purse. He shook it and it made a clinking sound. He then opened the bag and showed her numerous silver coins. 
The women looked at Soren and then at the leather purse and then back at Soren again. “Why should I let you in?”
“We are in need of shelter from the storm, and are on a very important mission to defeat Eiess.”
The woman’s eyes grew large. Then, she opened the door all the way and stepped aside. “Hurry, come in quickly. Don’t let all the warm air out,” she said, waving them in. “ I don’t want any trouble from you or your girl there,” she said, nodding in Ailia’s direction.
“Thank you, ma’am. We will not be any trouble at all,” Soren said humbly. He walked back over to Ailia, lifted her off Volomite and carried her into the cabin, where he set her on one of the two chairs in the main room. 
“May I put the horse in your—uh—stable?” Soren asked. 
Ailia assumed he meant the rundown shack next to the cabin.
“Yes, please. There is room for only one horse there,” the woman replied. “My horse just recently died,” she said and laughed. 
Ailia couldn’t see how that was funny.
“But, yes, you may put him in there. The stable still has hay and oats inside, so feel free to give him some of that.” 
Soren guided Volomite into the stable and then returned to the cabin. 
The cabin felt so warm compared to the cold outside. It was dark and stuffy and smelled like smoke, old linens and horsemeat stew. In the middle of the floor was the well-lit hearth and, above the hearth, was a hole in the ceiling where the smoke escaped. A pot sat on top of the hearth and Ailia suspected from the odor that the woman had been cooking the stew for a really long time. To decorate the walls, the woman had hung some colorful fabric along with some dried flowers. 
“Your flowers are beautiful,” Ailia said. There was also a small wood table with a burnt orange tablecloth on it and two non-matching wood chairs in the main room. From what Ailia could see, it looked like the second, smaller room was where the woman slept.
“I don’t have very much,” the woman started. “Since my great uncle died, it has been hard. The past few months haven’t been kind to me, or anyone in these parts. Only cold and lonely,” she complained. “I never get any visitors.”
I wonder why, Ailia thought. If anyone else had received the welcome they had, of course no one would be visiting. “We are just so grateful you are willing to let us stay,” she said.
“Yes, thank you for your generosity,” Soren added. “We would be stuck in the snow storm had you not let us in.” 
“Of course,” the woman said dismissingly. “You just have to be careful who you let into your house these days. Vikings, thieves—cannot be careful enough,” she said as she walked over to the table standing next to the window. She closed the shutters, which also had stuffed fabric in between the multitude of cracks. She grabbed three of the four bowls on the table and her ladle. “Horse meat stew?” she asked, holding up her ladle. 
“We would love some,” Soren said. “I have some bread that would taste good with that,” he said, reaching for his bag.
“Lovely,” the woman said. “I’m Hannah.” She stirred the pot vigorously.
“I am Soren and this is Ailia,” Soren said.
Hannah scooped stew into the bowls and smelled each of them. She moved slower than slow as she methodically placed the bowls on the table. The stew looked like a mix of some type of meat and bark. “Come, eat,” she invited. 
They sat down at the table. Ailia and Soren sat on the two chairs and Hannah flipped over a bucket and sat on that. The stew had a distinct earthy taste to it, Ailia thought. 
“So, tell me about yourselves then,” Hannah invited.
Soren and Ailia both pitched in and told about their journey over the last couple of days. They excluded the part where Ailia had seen the boys.
“Well, I’ll say. You’ve both been through a lot!” Hannah said. Her eyes turned dark and she raised her badly groomed eyebrows. “Did you happen to see the spirits of the Isaksen boys?” she wondered.
Ailia glanced at Soren and leaned forward toward Hannah. “What do you know about the boys?”
“So, you have seen them,” Hannah said, nodding her head. She took another bite of her stew and chewed it thoroughly.
“Yes, they appeared to me, but not Soren,” Ailia confirmed. “They seemed so real. I heard them cry. I saw them as clearly as I see you and I even held them both on my lap. Then, they suddenly vanished.” 
Hannah took another bite of her stew and chewed it slowly. When she finally swallowed, she spoke again. “They have been seen many times around these parts lately. I’ve never heard of anyone touching or holding them,” she said. “Now, that’s something special!” She wagged her index finger toward Ailia. “There is some disagreement about what really happened to the family after they left Bergendal, but no one could really know for sure what great ills occurred to them since there were no survivors. I, however, was also one of the Viking’s prisoners at the time of the family’s capture,” Hannah said intriguingly, taking another huge bite. 
“Did you see the family when they were alive?” Soren asked.
Hannah nodded slowly. “Yes,” she said, not bothering to swallow her food before she spoke. “Regrettably, I was originally one of the Vikings supporting the Empress before she came to reign, but when I found out who she really was, I could not and would not join in her grotesque, despicable lifestyle,” she said bitterly. 
Ailia stopped eating her stew. “Why would you support someone like her?” 
“I was naïve then and I thought that Eiess was the answer to all of our troubles. I learned in a short time that I had made a grave mistake in following her.” Hannah sounded depressed. “She is a cunning liar, that Empress, speaking of ideals, truth and glory. Eiess betrayed us and threw us out of her midst shortly after she took over the Northlandic Throne. I wasn’t a Viking myself, but I followed the Vikings to get away from the hatred of the people of Bergendal. But I should have rather endured their cruelty than associate with the barbarians.” 
“The Vikings soon became animals, no—beasts, or trolls—without hearts, without love, compassion, or care for anyone other than themselves. They were vain and self-absorbed. I couldn’t get myself to live that way and they turned on me, imprisoning me in a small wooden cage.” Hannah breathed deeply as her weary eyes wavered. 
“The mother of the young boys was killed first. They wanted her husband and children to watch the slaughter, hear her scream in pain and agony and plead for mercy. I sat in my prison and couldn’t bear to watch or listen to the poor woman’s screams. Her desperate pained voice is etched in my mind forever. Then, the Vikings turned toward the husband. I’ve never seen a man treated with such disrespect. His body was dismembered and taken apart piece by piece—” Hannah stopped speaking and looked into the flames of the hearth.
“I don’t think I want to know what happened to the boys,” Ailia said, feeling rather queasy. She could never imagine hurting anyone, especially those two innocent boys.
“I never knew what happened to the boys,” Hannah said. “One of the smaller Vikings decided to open my cage and take me into the forest to have his way with me again. I had had enough and something in me snapped. I knew that I had to get away from him, kill him or harm him in a way, so that I could escape from these horrible monsters. When we were a ways away from their settlement, I saw and picked up a large rock and as quickly and hard as I could, I took the rock and smashed it against his head.” Hannah held her fist up into the air. It looked like she was reliving the moment. “He fell to the ground and never stood back up. I ran as fast as I could away from there and I never looked back.”
“Did you go back to Bergendal?” Ailia asked.
“Yes, but they would have no part in helping a former supporter of Eiess and drove me away immediately. I tried to go back to my family, but they had disowned me. My old father’s uncle took pity on me and invited me into his house. The day after he had taken me in, we were both driven out of Bergendal by a mob. After we left, we found this cabin to live in,” she said, gesturing to the whole house. “He was a good man, the only man with complete compassion and love for all fellow men, or woman in my case.” Hannah stopped and swiftly wiped the tears away from her eyes. 
“So he must just have died?”
“Yes, a month ago last week.”
“I’m so sorry for your loss,” Ailia said.
“Me too,” Soren said.
“Now eat!” Hannah said, slapping her hands on her thighs. “Food tastes much better when it is warm.”
After repast, Hannah offered that Ailia sleep in her bedroom with her. She had a double bed that would fit them both. Soren would have to sleep on the floor in the main room. They were all very tired after a long day and the peace of slumber overtook them quickly.



13

Nukkern

Ailia wanted to stay in bed forever. The night had been peaceful and dreamless, a luxury she rarely experienced these days. If every night would be like this one, she would be happy. Ailia could hear that Hannah was up clanking the dishes, cooking breakfast and heating water for their morning tea. She got up and pulled her messy hair loose from the leather string it was tied with. Her long hair flowed down her back. She re-tied the string, got dressed and limped out to the main room.
Soren was already awake, tending to Volomite in the stable.
“Good morning to you,” Hannah said. “It’s going to be a beautiful day as soon as the sun gets up. I suspect you will be on your way shortly. I’ve packed you both food for your trip.” 
Just then, Ailia heard Soren galloping off on his horse. 
Hannah walked over to the window, opened the flimsy makeshift shutters and looked out. She turned to Ailia. “Did he tell you where he was going?” She looked puzzled.
“No,” Ailia said and walked over to join Hannah where she stood. Ailia peered through the window into the darkness and saw only Soren dash off into the murky pre-dawn morning on Volomite’s black back.
“I’m sure he’ll return soon,” Hannah comforted. “I never understood men. Just off doing their own thing, no consideration. Maybe gone hunting?” 
“Probably,” Ailia replied, but she felt like something wasn’t right. Soren wouldn’t just take off and leave her like this, would he? Not after all that he had done to help her. Now, she thought maybe she didn’t know him at all and maybe he didn’t care whether she made it back home or not. Maybe all his stories were lies.
Hannah was busy finishing breakfast: sop. Ailia had not had sop in years, since Aunt Unni didn’t like it. 
Hannah prepared three bowls by putting four thick slices of wholegrain bread in them. “Just in case he comes back, which I’m sure he will. Sop is my favorite meal,” she said. “It’s easy to make. It fills the belly up for a long time and it tastes wonderful, especially with a generous portion of honey. Soren gave this honey to me this morning,” Hannah said, holding up a small glass jar. She smiled. “Said he got it from the Southlandic Kingdom. I suppose they have the opposite problem down there now—no cold season.” Hannah scooped hot liquid over the bread. “Now, that might be all fine and dandy for them, you would think, but Soren said that they are suffering from a fresh water shortage since none of the glaciers here are melting and providing them with clean water. It’s only going to get worse. Soren said that thousands upon thousands are dying from lack of water. They are thirsting to death! I would much rather be here and cold, eating my fish, hare and horse, God rest Mimi’s soul,” she said, pointing to her stable. “Now, I cannot get around much any more, but there’s really nowhere to go.” She picked up a bowl and handed it to Ailia. “Be careful now, it’s very hot.”
“You ate your riding horse?” Ailia said. She had eaten horsemeat before and in fact, horsemeat stew was one of Ailia’s favorite meals, but she would never consider eating her riding horse.
“Well, I just didn’t need her to travel anywhere anymore. She was getting old and I needed the food,” Hannah said matter of fact. “Now she serves me each day, just in another way.”
“Makes sense,” Ailia said, but she was still baffled. 
Ailia ate her sop quickly. She decided that sop was now her new favorite breakfast. It satisfied her hunger and filled and warmed her belly, just like Hannah had said. 
Finally, the sun rose above the mountaintops and Ailia excitedly opened the front door and stepped outside to soak up its rare company. Her foot still hurt, but the pain had lessened considerably and it looked a lot less swollen and red than yesterday. 
“Close the door, or you’ll let all the warm air out,” Hannah hollered from inside. 
Ailia closed the creaking door behind her. Last night’s snow had covered the trees and hills in the Small Mountains and Volomite’s tracks were the only disturbance in this winter paradise. Ailia worried that Soren had left for good and she missed his company already. Her mind vacillated between being concerned that he would never return and also thinking that she might be overreacting. Maybe he just went out to hunt food and will be right back. Why hadn’t he told them where he was going and when, or even if, he would return? Will I ever make it home without his help?
Ailia glanced over the white and blue landscape. Branches of aspen and oak trees were covered with crystallized ice and they glittered in the light of the sun. Down by the frozen-over lake, Ailia saw a small deer prance gracefully across the ice. She took a few steps out into the snow to get a closer look at the deer. A chill shot through her legs as her bare feet touched the snow. She took a step back. Her foot was feeling much better and she could apply a little pressure on it without cringing.
Unexpectedly, she thought she heard a fiddle playing somewhere in the direction of the lake. She was curious and went back inside the cabin and put on the overcoat and boots. 
Hannah was still busy cleaning up from breakfast. 
Ailia ran out quickly. “I’ll be right back,” she shouted, as she hobbled toward the lake. She didn’t wait for Hannah to reply and wouldn’t have stopped if she had. The mid-calf-high snow was hard to walk in, so she had to slow her pace and lift her skirt as she trudged through it. She felt the snow enter her boots and it felt as if it was freezing her delicate skin. I should have put my stockings on before I left. 
Music continued to come from the other side of the small lake. She stood still for a moment, scanning the forest on the other side. Nothing, not even a single movement was detectable, only jovial notes could be heard. Ailia saw no reason not to continue to explore where the music was coming from and decided to move around the lake, because she was afraid of water. 
Ailia recognized the playful melody as the tune, Maiden Dance. She had heard it many times during the festivals in Bergendal. Maybe the fiddler is from Bergendal? she thought. 
As she continued, she kept her eyes locked in the direction the music was coming from. Then, she saw movement in the woods. It looked like there was a man on the other side playing the fiddle. Then, the tune stopped. Ailia stood in silence to see if the music would come back. The fiddler started playing another tune, a more melancholy, seductive tune that stirred up emotions in Ailia’s heart. She listened quietly as the melody filled her soul, penetrating her being. It was almost as if it were hypnotizing her, compelling her to go to the creator of the music. Her feet started moving across the snow again and she kept moving toward the sound. Through the woods, Ailia spotted a young, well-dressed, dark-blond man standing and playing the fiddle. 
Suddenly, the man stopped playing and turned around. 
Ailia jumped behind a tree, immediately feeling embarrassed that he might have seen her sneak up on him and then feeling embarrassed that she had hid behind the tree after he had most likely seen her. She wrestled with what to do. 
“Hello?” the man beckoned. “Miss?” 
“Ugh!” Ailia grimaced behind the tree. He had seen her. She apologetically stepped out from behind the tree. 
“Ah, there you are!” the man exclaimed, seeming excited to see her. His nostrils flared as he spoke.
“I’m sorry, I, um—uh was startled,” she said, feeling embarrassed. 
He had traveled very quickly toward Ailia, almost at a quicker than humanly possible pace, she thought. His deep-set, greedy eyes fixed on Ailia. Up close, Ailia noticed his unusually square jaw. It had a deep cleft in it. His full lips were cherry red, looking like he had just been kissing someone. There was an extreme and unexplainable manly sensuousness about him. Pure and raw masculinity seeped from his pores. 
Ailia breathed deeply to try and remain unaffected by his overwhelming presence. She had even started to feel weak in the knees from being so close to him.
“No need to apologize, miss—?”
“Oh, Ailia,” she said and smiled. She wasn’t sure whether she liked him and should stay or whether she should run away.
“What a thrilling name,” the man said, not volunteering his own. “Ailia,” he said again, this time enunciating her name more loudly and clearly as he finished with a glued-on smile. “Bravo, Ailia,” he said.
Ailia muffled a chuckle. She had never thought her name was thrilling, but liked that he thought it was thrilling. She replied with a thank you. 
“You must have heard me playing on my fiddle,” he said, holding it up. His movements seemed contrived, almost choreographed and extremely well-rehearsed.
“Yes, you are a very gifted fiddler. I just had to come over here,” Ailia complimented. “I don’t recognize you, but I recognized the first tune you played from my hometown Bergendal. Have you visited there before?” 
“No, but a man from Bergendal did teach me the melody,” he answered. 
That helped put Ailia more at ease. “Oh really, what was his name? I know all the fiddlers in Bergendal,” Ailia said excited, but still careful and wanting to test his knowledge on the subject.
“His name was Andreas. Do you know him?”
“Yes!” she exclaimed. “Andreas was the best fiddler in town.”
“I agree,” the stranger said and nodded his head fervently. “We met a few years ago at the town festival and I was so intrigued by his playing. I had never touched a fiddle in my life, but I knew that I needed to learn to play like him, and—”
Ailia listened to him tell the whole story and was mesmerized by the way he told it. It sounded like a fairy tale and Ailia loved listening to fairytales. As she studied his face, his deep blue, nearly black eyes drew her in. They were so dark and mysterious and Ailia wanted to know more about him. No, it was more like she needed to know more about him. There was just something absolutely breathtakingly attractive about this man in a very sensual way. All of her apprehension about him had vanished. Ailia had never had a serious crush on anyone, she thought, but she knew for sure that this was indeed a serious crush. His voice danced as he spoke articulately in a slightly southern dialect and his movements seemed so graceful, almost like watching an archangel, if Ailia thought she’d ever meet one in this life. He seemed divine, or on a higher level than any other human Ailia had met before. 
“—but I have not seen him in a few years. I heard he passed away?” he finished his story.
“Yes, he died of old age, I suppose,” Ailia said. She missed Andreas. He had been like an older brother to her, usually being the first one to dance with her at the festivals.
“Here, let me play you another tune you might know. Do you dance?” He started tramping down the snow to make a small danceable area for Ailia.
“Well, I do at our local festivals,” she said modestly. “My foot—” she started.
“Oh, come on, I am sure you are a great dancer. Show me a step or two.” He began playing a spirited tune. He glanced at her as he lifted his eyebrows and smiled ever so charmingly at her. “Well, come on then, dance!”
Ailia hesitantly put her hands on her hips and showed him a few steps, but she quickly stopped dancing because she felt silly. 
He kept playing the melody and nodded his head at her to keep going. “Yes, beautiful!” he shouted over the fiddle tune. “Just keep dancing!” 
Ailia kept going and the more she danced, the more she got into it. Finally, she didn’t feel silly or even uncomfortable anymore and was just happy to have some fun. The tune and dancing brought back so many wonderful memories from home. She couldn’t wait to get home again. She then stepped harshly on her injured foot and fell down into the packed snow. “Aw!” she said under her breath.
“Oh, miss, Ailia, Ailia, are you ok?” He carelessly threw his fiddle into the snow and knelt beside her.
“My foot is just a little injured,” she said. 
He helped her take her boot off and she could see that the wound had re-opened and was bleeding. “Will you help me get back over to the cabin over there?” she said.
“I have a better idea, if you do not mind,” he said. “My home is just right behind that hill there and it is closer. Let me carry you there. I have a very good supply of ointments and dressings.” He lifted her even before Ailia was able to object and started walking toward the hill.
“No, I need to get back,” Ailia said, feeling extremely uncomfortable again. “Hannah will wonder where I am and Soren will be worried too,” she said.
“You mean the young man who took off this morning?” the stranger asked. 
“No,” Ailia lied as she felt a knot tie in her stomach. “That was Leif,” she lied again. His strong arms gripped her tightly. Her mind started to try and figure out ways to escape this man. “I really need to get back now,” she said, more insistently. Maybe this guy was a Viking and he wanted to take her back to his house and kill her, or maybe he wanted to imprison her. Ailia grew more and more afraid.
“I see,” he said and stopped. “I will take you home then.”
Ailia felt relieved for a moment. He turned around and started to walk across the lake. 
“The lake is solid ice and it is much easier to move across it than try to wade through the snow like you did,” he said. 
So he had been watching her come toward him. That didn’t sit well with Ailia.
“I feel a lot better now. I can walk,” she said. By now they were about halfway across the lake. He set her down carefully and offered to support her with his hand. 
“Thank you,” Ailia said, still not trusting him, but she did need his help to cross the rest of the lake. She stepped forward, but without warning, he pulled her back in close to him, clutched her body to his and stomped the ice below so hard that it cracked. He stomped again and yet a third time, so hard it felt like an earthquake. His eyes were staring into Ailia’s and she saw that they had lit up to a bright glowing golden color. His teeth became sharp and his skin turned greyish in front of her eyes, like a dead man’s who had been underneath the water, rotting and decomposing for a long time.
“Help!” Ailia screamed as loudly as her voice would allow. “Hannah!” 
His stomps had opened a small hole in the ice, large enough to fit both of them. He jumped into the water and stayed afloat with Ailia for a while. 
“Shh—” he said stroking her hair. “Do not worry. It will all soon be over.”
The water was numbingly cold and Ailia began shivering immediately. “Help!” she continued to scream, but no one came to her rescue. Just then a conversation she had had with her Uncle Brander flashed through her mind. He had warned her several times about this brute water demon that would drag children and young maidens down to the deep with him, never to be heard from again. The Nukkern, he had called him.
The shape-shifting man tried to kiss her, but Ailia turned her head from side to side, avoiding his purple, wrinkled lips. 
“Help!  Someone please help me!” she yelled again.
“Ailia!” she heard in the distance. 
She glanced quickly to the right and was relieved she saw Soren in the distance, galloping toward her. There was also another white horse, with a brown spotted pattern, carrying a woman dressed in the typical fancy royal blue, red and white Sami clothes.
“Soren!” Ailia yelled. “Help!”
Just as she uttered the last word, the fiddler drew her underneath the icy water with him, wrapping his arms tightly around her body, locking in her arms so she could not move. 



14

Sister

It was the third day Lucia had sat at the end of the freezing tunnel. Her hands had been numb since day one and both her hands and feet had started to turn blue with splotches of black. Her cough had progressively grown worse and her lungs burned as she hacked away, sometimes even coughing up blotches of blood. All the food that was packed in her bag was gone and she could feel the fever raging through her body. 
She had tried once more on the second day to knock on the gate, but Nora had never come. Lucia had even tried to open the gate, but it appeared to be locked from the outside. Returning to the castle was out of the question, because she knew what ill fate awaited her there. Though she was utterly exhausted to the core, sleep had eluded her deprived body for she was too afraid to miss Nora’s arrival, or worse, she feared she might fall asleep—forever.
When the knock finally came, Lucia thought that she was dreaming, but then, as the knock persisted and increased in volume, she realized that it was real. She crept up close to the opening and saw Nora’s familiar old face peaking in. “Nora!” she cried, tears of joy brimming to the edges of her eyes. 
“Lucia, my dear, dear child, I am so glad you are still here. I was so worried about you. I shall explain everything later. I am just so glad to hear your voice, my blessed child,” Nora whispered.
Lucia started coughing again. 
“Oh dear, you sound dreadfully ill. We must hurry and get you to safety. Come with me,” Nora said, opening the creaky gate. She grabbed Lucia’s arm and pulled her up and out of the cavern. They hastily ran across the quiet, blackened, snowy road toward the northern exit. 
“I have my horse outside the walls,” Nora said, breathing heavily. 
Coughing and wheezing all the way, Lucia had a hard time keeping up with the old handmaiden. Nora helped Lucia mount the horse first and when they were both settled onto the mare, she nudged the horse to start moving. The wind was fierce, leaching in through Lucia’s not warm enough blanket, chilling her bare face and her body to their core. 
“It will be just a little way.” Nora spoke directly into Lucia’s ear. “This road leads down toward the main street and eventually to the church.”
I hope she is not talking about the Christian Church. Lucia still hated Jesus, for the more she thought about it, the more she believed that it was his fault that her mother had died, and that she was in this life-threatening predicament. “I am so glad— ouch, uh—you are here, uh, uh,” Lucia kept saying over and over, her body shaking and jerking with every cough. 
“Oh, my dear princess, we will need to call for a medicine man,” Nora said, placing her hand on Lucia’s burning brow. “Just a while longer now and we will be there.”
Heavy clouds hovered low over the city, slowly releasing their millions of snowflakes onto the tired, old town. There were no other people out this time of night, probably for fear of being abducted by the Vikings who had steadily increased their raids, or for fear of Eiess’s spies. 
Nora steered the horse into a small side road next to the town’s most popular tavern. Laughter came from the inside. The snow-white path led to a field, where the modest Bergendal Stave Church stood. Its four-tiered roof was covered in snow. A low rectangular wooden fence, with one pillar at each corner, edged around the church and enclosed hundreds of tightly placed gravestones. There was a clear path stamped out in the snow from another direction and Nora steered the horse onto the path. Lights were lit inside the church, Lucia noticed, but no light was burning outside. Nora directed the horse to enter at the western gate at the church’s rear entrance. 
A tall, lanky man came out the back door and greeted them. Lucia thought she might have met him before, but could not remember where.
“Hello and welcome to the Lord’s house,” he said, reaching both palms up toward his guests. “I have been expecting you.” He lowered his hands, clasping them in front of his hips. 
Lucia was in and out of consciousness now but still coughing often. She finally recognized the man as the Bishop who had spoken when she attended church a while back. He wore a brown tunic alb and had not bothered to put his overcoat on before he came out. He must be regretting it now. Lucia saw a younger, but very stalky man come out behind the Bishop. 
“This is Anders. He will take your horse and feed him in the stable,” the Bishop said. 
Anders stepped forward and grabbed the horse by the reigns. 
“I’m Bishop Peter.” The two men of God helped both ladies off the horse, but they had not anticipated how weak Lucia was. Lucia collapsed the instant her feet hit the ground.
“Oh heavens!” Bishop Peter said, picking her up immediately and lifting her inside. “I apologize in advance. The church is not very well heated, I’m afraid. I’ll have Anders put some more wood on the hearth.”
Lucia’s eyes were shut, but she noticed they had stepped inside now. It was warmer inside the church but not warm enough for comfort.
“The girl is very ill,” Nora said, walking over to Bishop Peter. “Can you send for a healer or medicine man?”
“I am educated in the art of healing. I’ll do my best. Let me place her in my chambers. You may both sleep there tonight and until she is well. I’ll take the back room.” He started walking through a narrow hallway.
Nora followed closely behind them. 
“Ivar said you would be arriving several days ago. Was I mistaken?” he asked.
Lucia could hear Anders come back inside, stomping the snow off his boots in the entrance. 
“Boil water immediately, Anders, we need to bring this girl back to life,” Bishop Peter yelled in Anders’s direction. 
Lucia felt nearly dead in Bishops Peter’s arms, but she could still hear them talking. I am in a Christian church. I do not want to be here. The gods will curse me, as they did my mother.
“I was captured by a Viking,” Nora said. 
That is why she was late. I could have died because she was not careful enough not to get caught. Lucia surprised herself how bitter she felt.
They entered a small room with a humble looking bed at the end of it. Wool throws covered it and at looked like the mattress was made of straw.
Bishop Peter turned around. “You escaped?” 
“Yes, I told him I would return with my mother’s gold amulet if he released me,” Nora said.
“Do you have such an amulet?” Bishop Peter asked.
“No,” Nora answered. “My mother is not alive either.”
“So, how did you get him to believe you and let you go?” Bishop Peter arrived at the bed and laid Lucia down on it. 
Taking Lucia’s shoes off, Nora started massaging heat into her numb feet again. They were frozen cold, swelled up and blackish purple at some of the toes. 
“You poor thing,” Nora said. “Well, I got the Viking very drunk. He could not think straight,” Nora said, slightly embarrassed.
“Thank the Lord for mead,” Bishop Peter said, folding his hands as if he were praying. 
It was not quite the reaction Lucia had anticipated from Bishop Peter. She thought men of this new God did not believe in excessive drinking.
Bishop Peter sat down on the bed and studied Lucia for a while, listening to her heart and lungs. He stood up and signaled for Nora to follow him to the corner of the room. Nora stood up and approached him.
Lucia could hear their conversation even though they were whispering. 
“This girl is very ill,” Bishop Peter said. “How long has she had this fever and cough?”
“I am not sure, but longer than I would like to think,” Nora whispered back. 
“Her lungs sound horrible. I’ll do my best, but unfortunately, there’s not much I can do for her at this point,” Bishop Peter said. 
“Surely, there must be something you can do. She is the Princess!” Nora pleaded in desperation and then she lowered her voice. “And she is the Great Sentinor!”
“You mean—” Bishop Peter started. “The Great Sentinor? Oh, Lord. The only man I know who may be able to help her isn’t here. It would take weeks before anyone would be able to reach him.”
“Soren,” Nora said. “Yes, he would be the only one.”
“How do you know Soren?” the Bishop asked, sounding surprised.
“Well, he is Lucia’s betrothed. I first met him when I contracted ergotism. He was the only one who could heal me. If Soren knew that Lucia was here, he would come for her immediately. Should we send for him?” 
“Nora?” Lucia said. She could feel the warmth starting to breathe life into her body.
“I am here, my dear. You are safe with us now. No need to worry.” Nora hastened toward the bed.
“I do not think I am the Great Sentinor. It is a mistake,” she said. “I have a letter from my father, uh, uh, uh,” she coughed. Her hands searched her bag for the scroll. 
Nora helped her find it and gave it to her. 
“From my father. He said to read it as soon as I was safe.” She felt the anticipation of reading his words energize her. Holding it up so Nora could see, she asked, “Will you read it to me?  I am too tired.”
“Of course, my love,” Nora said, looking over at Bishop Peter.
“I will leave you to your privacy.” He bowed and walked out, closing the door behind him. 
Nora tucked some itchy wool pillows behind Lucia’s back, so she could sit up in a semi-reclining position. When she opened the red Bergendal-sealed scroll, two additional smaller scrolls fell out. One of them read Lucia and one of them, Ailia. 
“Ailia?” Nora said. “Do you remember that Ailia was the name your mother spoke right before she died?” she said to Lucia. She started reading the main letter first, smiling as if she knew something Lucia did not.

My dearest beloved Lucia,
I have sat down to write this letter many times over the years, but have not been able to put these significant words on paper. Now, as I am imprisoned by Eiess in my own castle and am preparing for your escape, I am forced to write it in a hurry. Please forgive me if I come across abrupt, but time is not my ally and I must inform you of many essential things.
You may not understand everything I am about to say to you or understand why your mother and I chose to do things the way we did. It does not mean that we loved you less than your sister.

Nora looked up “Did you know you had a sister?” she said, caressing Lucia’s face. 
Lucia opened her eyes. “Yes, I always knew,” Lucia said calmly. “My mother spoke to me about her from time to time, but she died when she was just a baby. She was my twin.”
Nora nodded and continued reading.

Your mother spoke to you of your twin sister that died at birth. The truth is that she did not die, Lucia. She still lives, even here in Bergendal.

“She is still alive?” Lucia perked up a bit. “I have a sister! We must find her!”
Nora looked up again, lowering the letter to her lap. “I am sorry we have all kept this a secret from you, Lucia.” 
“What?” Lucia said. “You knew that my sister was still alive and did not tell me?” Lucia felt angry. Every direction she turned, there was another lie and she did not know what or who to trust anymore.
Nora nodded her head, took a deep breath and read on.

To protect Ailia’s identity, we gave her away at birth to a loving couple. To protect her identity even further, your mother and I did not know of the couple’s identity. 
Ivar, our faithful guard, brought Ailia to the family and even to this day, the family still does not know who Ailia is. Your mother and I do not know where Ailia lives, only Ivar knows.
My dear Lucia, your sister is the Light—the Light in which you need to bear—the true Great Sentinor. Eiess still believes you are the Great Sentinor and she will be hunting you until the day you die, or until she discovers the truth. Please forgive us in protecting your sister and endangering you by creating an illusion that you were the Great Sentinor. Try to understand that this decision was an attempt to save her life and ultimately, the life of all people on Midgard, including yours, from the evils of Eiess. If Ailia does not succeed this time, we are all doomed.
Your sister needs you. She does not know of your existence or of her royal lineage yet and she is unaware that she is the Great Sentinor. You must find her and tell her. We gave the same Bergendal crested gold ring to her adoptive parents to keep in an unopened chest until we claimed her back. Ask them for it, for this will be proof to you that she is your sister.

Lucia twisted her ring around her finger. She did not like what she was hearing—not one bit. They used me?  
Nora continued reading.

On the morrow, Eiess has planned for my execution. Please forgive me for deceiving you, yet again. But I feared that you would not have left my side if I had told you. My sacrifice is getting you out safe and it is a happy sacrifice indeed. Rest assured, I am joyful, now having been reunited with my wife and your mother Maud and our unborn son in Valhalla. I am looking down on you with love and hope and I will always be with you, watching over you until we meet again one glorious and bright day.

Your loving father,
Olav

~ I have enclosed two letters from your mother. One is for you and the other is for Ailia. Please give it to her when you find her.

Nora looked up with tears streaming down her face. She lowered the scroll and glanced over at Lucia. 
Lucia felt torn. If I seek my sister out and find her, she will know who she is and Eiess will not be after me anymore. If I do not find her, Nora will probably do it herself. 
Who was this man she called father really? She had always thought he was a man of integrity, one who she could trust to do the right thing, no matter what. Yes, he was a bit harsh, but dishonest? Unfaithful?  A—fraud? Lucia unsuccessfully fought back her tears and wept in Nora’s arms, waiting, hoping that the pain would subside, but no release of heartache came to her and her hurt only grew deeper. The loss of life and loss of trust was too devastating to take in and too painful to speak out loud. Now that she knew Nora was part of that deceitful circle, it made her even angrier. 
“Oh, my king,” Nora said finally. “May your soul rest in peace.”
“Now I am all alone,” Lucia said.
“Well, no, you have a sister! And you have me, do not forget. We will need to find Ailia as soon as you recover,” Nora said. 
Lucia looked at Nora soberly. “I will recover and we will find her,” she said firmly, her eyes focused and clear. Better to have Nora think I care. Do I care? She tried not to think about the voice in her head that mourned her parents’ falseness—more than their deaths—and which killed her trust in them and all she had ever held dear. Even my mother lied to me and used me.

*    *    *

A few days later Lucia’s strength had started to come back and she felt physically well enough and emotionally ready to read the letter her mother had written to her. She started reading it with mixed emotions. 

My beloved daughter Lucia,
The night before you were born, Iluxia spoke to me about you and Ailia. It was a joyous and a sad moment in one. Sad, because Iluxia told me that the Great Sentinor must be dead unto me at birth and joyous because I would get to keep you. He declared that I must name you Lucia, which means ‘Bearer of Light’, so everyone would believe you were the Great Sentinor. 
Your mission in life is to be the bearer of your sister, the one light of Midgard and you must do everything in your power to bear her and uphold her. 
I have prepared something called the Aesira Scrolls for Ailia, which she is supposed to receive when she turns eighteen. You must read the scrolls also, for you will learn through these detailed writings all the important details and history of your Aesira lineage.

Lucia remembered that Vilda had mentioned the scrolls. 

On your eighteenth birthday you will receive the Aesira Jewel from either me or your father, if I have passed on to the other side. You will then take over as the Sun Queen.

Ha!  That was a fiasco, Lucia thought.

You will be charged with keeping the Aesira Jewel hidden from the world and concealed from Eiess and also with engaging the jewel each solstice with Iluxia.
You, my beloved daughter have the immense responsibility and power to help Ailia defeat Eiess. She needs you to support her through her life and I am asking you, with all my love, to be there for her no matter how difficult your lives become. Without your support, Ailia will probably not succeed.

With all my Love,
Mother

“Do you know anything about the Aesira Scrolls?” Lucia asked Nora, who was sitting in the corner on an old wooden stool, hemming a garment.
“Yes. Your mother wrote them and I hid them before you were born. They contained the genealogy of the Aesira bloodline and a lot more. I never read them, but I got the impression that it was very important that Ailia receive them.”
“Well, if you hid them, you know where they are, right?” Lucia asked.
“Well—” Nora hesitated. “I think your mother had them moved to a different location than where I hid them. I had hidden them in the Godwalk Caves right around the time of your birth.”	
 	“So, we will never find them then—” Lucia said discouraged. I must find them before Ailia does.
“Well, your mother and father were very smart people. They would have left some clues about where to find them. I would not give up just yet.”
Lucia sighed. 
“Now, your job is to get better and to get enough rest. Stop worrying about the future. Just focus on getting well here and now,” Nora said.
Lucia looked at her. “Yes ma’am.” First I need to find Ailia so I can get Eiess off my back.



15

Day Mares

As the fiddler plunged Ailia into the freezing water, she vividly remembered her mare dream about being drowned underneath the ice. She panicked and started kicking and squirming her body to get out of the clutch of the man who had quickly become her nemesis. 
Is this really happening? Who is this man? Is he the dreaded Nukkern my Uncle Brander had warned me so fervently about? Ailia’s fight was futile, for his grasp was too strong to escape. As she tried to wrestle her way out, he drew her deeper and deeper into the lake, a lake that appeared shallow and small on the surface, but now felt deeper than the deepest ocean. The freezing water numbed her body, making it painful and difficult to move and she started to feel like she needed to take a breath again. 
She looked up toward the top of the lake and saw sunrays peering through the broken ice and, then, a person jumping into the water. It was Soren. He started swimming toward her. If I can only last a while longer, I’ll be safe and back in Soren’s care, she thought.
Soren swam aggressively downward. Right when he reached them, he grabbed the Nukkern by the shoulder, looked him in the eyes and blurted something incomprehensible. 
The Nukkern looked furious, his eyes now fiery red as he strengthened his suffocating grip on Ailia. 
She felt the air being squeezed out of her already burning lungs. I cannot hold on any more and she exhaled the last breath of air she had in her lungs.
Suddenly, the Nukkern let go of her and disappeared abruptly into the deep, dark chasm. Soren grabbed Ailia by the hand and headed upwards to her salvation, swimming toward the surface. 
Ailia was so cold that she could barely move her body, but she still tried to assist Soren in swimming to the top by kicking her wilting feet. Then finally as she broke through the water’s surface she gasped for air. 
The woman that had ridden next to Soren was waiting for Ailia. She looked like she was in her mid-thirties and was stalky with a muscular build. Thick brown leggings covered her legs and her calves and feet were bound in red and white wool fabric. On her feet, she wore white and brown reindeer fur boots decorated with white, red and green trim that came up to a swirly point at the toe. 
“Grab on,” the Sami woman said, offering her bare hand to Ailia.
Ailia slowly lifted her hand up. It was shaking violently. So cold, so cold.
The Sami woman pulled her out of the water, wrapping her into first a wool blanket and then a reindeer fur she had grabbed off of her horse. “You will be just fine, just fine,” she said, rubbing Ailia’s back briskly.
Soren placed his hands on the ice and pushed himself up out of the water. He grabbed a fur off of Volomite and wrapped himself in it. Both Soren and the Sami woman lifted Ailia onto Volomite’s back and made their way across the rest of the ice-covered lake. When they arrived at the cabin, Soren lifted Ailia off Volomite and barged in the door.
“What happened?” Hannah asked startled. She rose to her feet from her chair, dropping the cup of tea she was drinking from.
“The Nukkern,” Soren yelled. “You should have warned Ailia about him!” He carried Ailia over to the hearth. “Surely, if you live here, you’ve seen him around?”
“What do you mean? There is no Nukkern that lives around here,” Hannah replied defensively. “I would have warned her if I knew.”
“Well, he is here now!” Soren said, his voice seething. He laid Ailia on the floor close to the fire. “Get me some hot water. We need to warm Ailia up to prevent her from freezing to death.”
“Right away, sorry, sorry,” Hannah said, moving frantically to get it done.
Ailia started to shiver even more violently. She saw that her fingers had turned blue. 
Soren started ripping off her wet, cold clothes. “I need more blankets, Silya,” he ordered the Sami woman. Silya gathered up all the blankets and furs that she could find in the room and brought them over to Soren. 
“Horses, get the horses,” Soren said as he took the blankets. 
Silya immediately left the room to tend to the horses. 
Soren covered Ailia’s body with a blanket before he took off her last undergarment. He then proceeded to undress himself, leaving only his undergarments on and slipped underneath the blanket. He embraced Ailia’s cold, quivering body and pressed it up against his. 
Ailia could feel warmth flowing from him and she embraced him back. She thought he seemed unusually warm to just have come out of the same frozen lake as her. 
Hannah came over with hot water she had gotten off of the hearth and some herbal tea for Ailia to drink. She soaked a cloth in hot water and laid it on Ailia’s forehead and then handed the tea to Soren to give to Ailia.
“What happened?” Hannah asked concerned, kneeling down next to them.
“The Nukkern got hold of her somehow and he dragged her under the water with him. First, he broke the surface of the ice and then he held her captive in the icy water for some time. Then, he dragged her underneath the water with him.” Soren stopped for a moment and then continued. “Did you not hear her screams?”
“I, I am sorry, but I’m deaf in my left ear and I have limited hearing in the other, so the main way I listen is by reading lips,” Hannah said. “I wish I could have heard her. I never would have imagined that something like this could happen here.” She looked distraught. 
“I am sorry. I did not know,” Soren said.
Ailia started to stir under the blanket. Her shivering had nearly ceased and the warmth had begun to spread through her body. She opened her eyes and fully realized she was laying next to a nearly naked man, a man she had started to grow fond of. “Soren, you’re back!” She smiled peacefully and leaned her head over to rest on his bare chest. She felt so safe there nestled in his care. “Where did you go? I wasn’t sure if you had left for good, or if you were even coming back.”
Soren slowly wriggled away and stood up. His shoulders and chest were well developed and his legs muscular. He seemed unusually tan for winter, but he had been in the Southlandic Kingdom recently, Ailia noted. Watching him move, bare wet skin, a hard body, Ailia had to catch her breath. She had never seen anything as beautiful before. He looked straight at her and she felt self-conscious. Then she felt a rush of blood go to her face. She looked away to give him privacy. 
Ailia wished he had stayed close to her longer. She enjoyed having him near, so much more than she wanted to admit. She wanted to feel his bare skin against hers and for him to pull her close to his strong body.
“Your temperature is stabilizing, so you will be fine now. Something has come up and I need to leave, so I brought Silya here to help you get home,” he said coolly.
“Oh,” Ailia said dispirited, trying to read his face for any clues of his sudden change of plans. 
“Silya knows the land and she is a very skilled hunter and traveller. She will be good company and help you with anything you might need.” He pulled his blue tunic on, secured the belt around his waist, walked over to Ailia and knelt beside her. Gently stroking her forehead, he said, “Make sure you stay warm for a while longer and stay close to Silya.” He looked into her eyes and exhaled. “You need to be more careful. It is really a miracle that you did not die. What happened out there?”
“I heard music and—” Ailia huffed. “I’ll be more careful.” She was embarrassed that she had to be rescued by him yet again. She sat up and tried not to seem too upset that he was leaving her. “Will I ever see you again?” she asked calmly, doing everything she could to restrain the emotion in her voice. 
“Maybe,” Soren said. He sounded unsure. “What is important is that you get home safely and Silya will see to that.” He walked over to her foot and lifted the blankets. “I saw that your wound had re-opened. I will need to stitch it back together before I leave.” He fetched his bag and pulled out some sharp, scary-looking instruments. “This will hurt,” he said to Ailia. “Hannah, do you have a stick Ailia can bite down on for the pain?” He then refocused on Ailia. “Try to think about something other than the pain.” He prepared the instruments he was going to use to sew the open wound shut. “Ready?” he said and looked into Ailia’s eyes.
“Yes,” she said bravely, not really knowing if she would be able to handle it. 
He started sewing and Ailia felt the needle every time it punctured her skin. She breathed erratically and bit down on the stick hard as she strained to not scream in agony.
“Just a few more stitches,” Soren informed sympathetically. “You are very brave, you know, so very brave.” 
Ailia decided to focus on Soren’s face to help keep her calm. She noticed the pain lessen as she focused on him, on his deep, calming, narrow eyes. Her breathing became more stable and the pain more manageable, the more she immersed herself into him. 
“All done,” Soren said, seeming somewhat relieved. He walked over and crouched down by her side. “I am usually not fazed by any surgery or illness, but for some reason it bothers me to see you suffer.” 
“Thank you again, Soren, for all you have done for me. I’m glad our paths crossed, even if only briefly.” Ailia wanted to ask him to stay longer, wanted to get to know him better, wanted to explore this feeling of desire she had for him, but she realized his heart was promised to and held by, another woman—a woman much superior to her. “I’ll miss you,” she decided to add. 
Soren paused and looked at her in silence as he brushed her cheek with his hand. Their eyes connected and they looked at each other for a while. All else seemed to fade away except for the oneness that connected them then and there. “I will miss you, too,” he said, almost whispering. 
Ailia reached for his hand, but he pulled away. He stood up and started cleaning the bloodied instruments, placing them in his bag when he finished.
Hannah walked over to him and handed him some food she had prepared for his trip. “Thank you for your generosity,” she said.
“Thank you for yours.” Soren then opened the door and joined Silya outside. 
Ailia could hear them talking.
“Please make sure she gets home safely. I might come see you after I have been to see the Empress,” Soren said. 
Ailia’s blood froze. The Empress? Why would he visit the Empress? Is he a secret scout after all? Ailia’s heart dropped as she kept listening in on their conversation. And she was now terrified that Soren might travel to the Empress and reveal all that she had shared with him.
“Have a safe trip, Soren,” Silya said, hugging Soren warmly.
“Thank you.” Soren stepped back inside again and looked at Ailia as if he were trying to engrave the image of her face in his mind, as if it were the last time he intended to see her. He was about to say something but it looked like he quieted his voice instead. He then turned around, stepped outside, closed the door behind him and mounted Volomite. He grabbed the reigns and made a clicking noise twice. Volomite neighed and took off.
Ailia closed her eyes as she heard Volomite’s hoofs disappear in the distance. She wanted Soren near and couldn’t bear the thought of him leaving. Is there something wrong with me that he will not bring me home himself?  
Silya entered in the front door with a huff. “It is a cold day today,” she said, her full cheeks now rosy from the cold. 
Ailia slightly resented Silya being there in Soren’s stead. She didn’t want to be escorted home by some stranger, even if Soren had recommended her. “I forgot to ask Soren before he left: what did he say that made the Nukkern leave?”
“Probably his name. If you know the Nukkern’s name and speak it out loud, he has to leave,” Silya said.
“Really?” Ailia asked.
Silya rubbed her hands together quickly above the hearth, warming them in the heat of the flames. Every move Silya made was quick and quiet. “Years ago, the Nukkern was one of Eiess’s followers in Vanaheim and he descended with her to Midgard to gain power over mortals. They were also followed by many other creatures of darkness.”
“Like who?” Ailia asked, slightly frightened to know the answer. She hadn’t meant to open up that big of a can of worms.
 “Have you heard of the huldras or darkelves?” Silya asked, her alert eyes narrowing.
“Yes—?” Ailia said. “They cannot possibly be real?” she added disbelievingly.
“Well, did you believe in the Nukkern before today?” Silya asked.
“Well, no—?” Ailia didn’t want to believe that all the ominous shape-shifters she had always thought were fictitious were actually real. “Why haven’t I ever seen them before?”
“In the past they have not associated too much with mere mortals,” Silya said.
“Oh,” Ailia said, feeling like being a mere mortal was something a little too average. She didn’t appreciate that and it gave her even more of a reason to dislike Silya.
“Recently though and especially since Eiess took over the Northlandic Throne, they have started interacting more with humans and have even started regularly threatening human existence,” Silya said.
“Just like the Nukkern?”
“Well, the Nukkern is a little different. Soren captured the Nukkern long ago and was going to kill him. Instead, Soren made a pact with him that he could keep his life as long as he remained anonymous and would do a certain amount of work for Soren. Part of the agreement was that if anyone found out the Nukkern’s name, the Nukkern would lose his life.”
“So, his real name is not Nukkern, then?” Ailia asked.
“No. No one except Soren knows his real name,” Silya said.
“So, do any of these other shape-shifters have agreements with Soren too?”
“I do not think so, but I will be the first to admit that I do not know everything.” Silya sat down by Ailia. “How are you feeling?” she asked and smiled, revealing pearly white teeth.
“Much better.” Ailia was feeling much better already and felt that she could start travelling back to Bergendal. She didn’t want to stay too long in this sinister forest. It didn’t seem to be the most welcoming place. “When can we leave?” Ailia asked.
“As soon as we have prepared some supplies and your clothes have dried, I think we can go. Are you feeling well enough to travel though? We could wait until tomorrow,” Silya said.
“I feel great. I’m ready to get going,” Ailia assured, a little too eagerly perhaps.
“Great!  Hannah?”
“Yes,” she said, coming out from the back room.
“Will you be so kind and help us pack our things? Let us put Ailia’s clothes by the hearth so they’ll dry quicker,” Silya said.
“Yes, dear,” Hannah replied. 
They started to pack the blankets and supplies for the trip. Hannah prepared a generous portion of dried horse meat, fish and water in leather skins. Once Ailia’s clothes were dry, they were ready to go.
“Soren told me to give you this before we left,” Silya said, handing Hannah another bag of coins.
“No, I couldn’t,” Hannah said and shook her head. “He already gave me enough to last my whole lifetime!”
“He insisted, so here you go,” Silya said and placed the bag of money on Hannah’s table. “We should be going, so we can utilize the few daylight hours we have, as much as possible. Ailia, Soren told me that you should not put any pressure on your foot for at least two days, so if you would hop on one foot out to the horse with my support, I can lift you up onto Miika.”
“Sure,” Ailia replied, wondering how Silya would be able to lift her up onto a horse. She stood up with Silya’s help and they both hopped through the cabin, out into the snow and stood next to Miika. 
“On three,” Silya instructed, grabbing Ailia around her waist. “One, two, three.” She hoisted Ailia up onto the horse, straining a little bit. Miika was not as tall as Volomite but was extremely thick and stalky and probably weighed the same. 
Soren has certainly picked a sturdy woman to escort me back home. “Thank you for your generosity, Hannah,” Ailia said when Hannah walked out the front door. 
“If you ever travel through again, please come visit,” she said, placing her hands on her heart. “I don’t get many friendly visitors out here.” 
“I sure will,” Ailia said.
Silya signaled to Miika to start walking and they were off into the forest again. 
Ailia waved to Hannah until they reached beyond the first hill. Poor woman. She hated leaving her all alone in these wretched woods. 
After they had travelled for a while, Ailia’s mind wandered back to thinking about Soren. He had promised he would help her find out why there was a connection between Eiess and her and Ailia had believed him. I’m a fool to have believed him, Ailia thought. For now, he had just abandoned her without warning to fend for herself against the most powerful and evil being that walked Midgard. Who can I trust anymore?



16

The Four Sentinors

“So, where do you know Soren from?” Ailia broke the silence that had been hovering uncomfortably in the air since they had left Hannah’s cabin.
“Soren and I have known each other since I was a child,” Silya said, looking at Ailia, sitting on the horse. “We met right after I had been sold into slavery by my uncle. Southerners would come to my hometown, Alta and pay handsomely for strong, young thralls—thralls like me.”
“How horrible,” Ailia said, stunned by Silya’s tragic story. Now she felt sorry for her. 
“After I was sold to a travelling company, who claimed they represented the Southlandic Empress Mumtaz, Soren bought me with sixteen pieces of silver and set me free. I was able to return to my family and I have lived as a free woman ever since.”
“It’s amazing how someone can come into one’s life at exactly the right time and perform miracles like that,” Ailia said, thinking about Soren and her situation with the wolves. “So, was that who Soren was going to see, Empress Mumtaz?” Ailia couldn’t wait to find out. She didn’t want to sound too disinterested in Silya’s story but was dying to know where Soren had gone and wanted to see if Silya would give him away.
“Yes,” Silya answered casually. 
Ailia felt relieved that Soren was not going to visit Eiess. Her facial expression must have given her away. 
“Oh, did you think he was going to see Eiess?” Silya asked and laughed sarcastically. “No, not Soren. He has been working with Empress Mumtaz on a plan to overthrow Eiess. The Southlandic Kingdom is also suffering tremendously because the seasons have stopped. Except, in their case, there is almost no night, only day.”
“Yes, Soren told me. It sounds horrible that one person’s actions can have such a drastic effect on so many innocent people,” Ailia said troubled. “One thing I don’t understand is where Eiess gets all her powers from. Why is she so powerful compared to anyone else?”
“Well, there are many reasons, but what most people do not know is that Eiess also used to be a Sentinor just like Iluxia, Lucia and Soren.”
“Truly?” Ailia asked shocked, still not understanding wholly what a Sentinor was, but never expecting that Eiess would be one.
“Eiess was created in the beginning with the three others, thousands and thousands of years ago. What happened then was never told to anyone in Midgard, except for the Tundra people or, as you call them, the Samis.”
“You are a Sami, right?” Ailia wanted to clarify just to be sure, even though Silya’s dress had Sami written all over it. “Silly question,” she said under her breath, knowing what the answer would be.
“Yes, I am a Sami,” Silya answered without a hint of condescension in her tone. “Many generations ago, our Noaida, Geidun, who is our mediator between the Midgard realm and the spirit realm, was granted a vision by Iluxia. The realms opened to him and he was shown in a fantastic revelation what transpired before the creation of the Aesira bloodline. Four beings were created to be Midgard’s protecting Sentinors: Iluxia, the Sentinor of progress and keeper of the key of the Aesira Jewel; Eiess, the Sentinor of darkness, who gives all of life rest, peace and sleep; Soren, the Sentinor of spiritual and physical healing; and finally, Lucia, the Sentinor of physical and spiritual light.”
Ailia was starting to see a much larger picture of Soren’s deep love and devotion for Lucia. Of course he wouldn’t want someone like me, a young peasant girl who cannot even remember how she ended up on the glaciers. She felt foolish to have feelings for him, but she still couldn’t help feeling that she was meant for him. “What about the Sun Queens, the keepers of the Aesira Jewel?” Ailia wondered.
“First, let me tell you about what happened to Eiess,” Silya said. “Eiess and Iluxia were charged to work in perfect harmony, one ruling the sun, the day, growth, energy, light and anything pertaining to progress, increase and development, while the other would rule the night and anything pertaining to rest, slumber, tranquility, harmony, serenity, repose and stillness. As Midgard drew nearer and nearer to the sun, Iluxia’s powers would strengthen; likewise, as Midgard drew further and further away from the sun, Eiess’s powers would increase. Not only that, but as time went on, their powers would increase with each passing solstice. So Iluxia’s powers were strongest at summer solstice eve. Eiess’s—”
“—powers were strongest on winter solstice eve,” Ailia said. “That is why she chose to strike on winter solstice eve, so that her powers would be the strongest, not only on that day, but as long as the dark season prevailed.”
“And how many winter solstice days have we had?” Silya asked sarcastically.
“Way too many,” Ailia replied facetiously. 
Silya looked up at her and laughed heartily. “A person after my own heart!” Silya said. 
Maybe Silya wasn’t so bad after all, Ailia thought.
Silya turned serious again. “Now this part of Midgard stands frozen on the darkest day of the year and will forever, until someone destroys Eiess. Some people believe this is the eternal winter that will ring in Ragnarok. Others believe winter is here to stay and that the gods have cursed us. Either way, Eiess’s powers are growing exponentially every day since the days are in her favor and Iluxia’s powers are remaining constant. Unfortunately, in comparison to Eiess’s powers, Iluxia’s powers are decreasing.” 
 “So, why can’t the other three Sentinors just work together and destroy her?” Something wasn’t making sense, Ailia thought.
“Let me backtrack a bit. First, none of the Sentinors were supposed to come to Midgard’s realm. When Eiess defiantly descended to Midgard, breaking her pact with the other Sentinors, Lucia and Soren were sent to Midgard to bring her back or defeat her if they could not. The difference was that Soren and Lucia had to be born into Midgard by mortal parents and needed to relearn who they were. That is how they became demi-mortals. Eiess found a way to come to Midgard without having to be born and she remembers everything from before mortality.”
“Demi-mortal?” Ailia asked confused.
“A demi-mortal is someone who is half-human, half-Sentinor. When a Sentinor is born into Midgard, he or she becomes more than just mortal. They retain some of their divine powers that mere mortals like you and I do not have. One of their divine powers is that they cannot be easily killed. They also possess special endowments. Take Soren, for example, he is a healer. Lucia has three lives. The difficult part of becoming a demi-mortal is that they have to be born as infants into Midgard and leave behind all knowledge and power until they rediscover them and redevelop them here again,” Silya said.
“So, they don’t remember who they are?”
“No. They need to learn in time, through life’s experiences and they do not remember the pre-mortal spheres, or what they learned there, as the Empress does. Empress was a title she gave herself by the way.” Silya shook her head. “She is really just a self-serving witch. Whatever she is, Eiess does unfortunately remember the pre-mortal sphere and now she is more powerful than anyone.”
