It was barely perceptible, but nevertheless perceptible, to her in the dusk. She sighed in amazement and took off her mittens to better see her skin. The light from her skin dimmed slowly until it was noticeable no more. “Did you see—that?” she asked Ivar. 
“Yes, my lady,” he said. “You were—glowing.”
“I was glowing,” she said and walked over to the rune stone. “I’m the light source and there’s no reason I shouldn’t be able to find what I’m looking for. I believe my father inscribed this rune stone with directions on how to find the Aesira scrolls. My mother said in her letter to me that ‘A wise woman seeks after the words of her parents.’ These and the letter she wrote to me, are the only words that I have from them. I have read her letter a hundred times and found nothing. But maybe there is something to be found here.”
“I know your mother’s favorite symbol was the Valknut symbol, this one here.” Ivar pointed to the symbol engraved on the rune stone. 
“Well, what is significant about it?” Ailia asked herself more than Ivar. “Hmm—It has three interwoven triangles. The symbol isn’t really related to our family in any way, is it?”
“No, not that I know,” Ivar answered. “But, there is a pattern here, though. Everything is in threes.”
“So, maybe every third gravestone has something on it?” Ailia said. 
They both walked over to different gravestones and searched their surfaces for something hidden.
“There is nothing on mine, is there anything on yours?” Ivar asked.
“No,” Ailia answered. 
“Let us look at one more,” Ivar said, now seeming very excited to be searching for a secret encryption. They found nothing of interest, not even a small inscription or indent, on any of the stones. They were all perfectly round.
“Maybe on the rune stone itself?  Ivar suggested. How about every third sentence?” They tried to read it but it did not make any sense. 
“What about every third word,” Ailia said. They looked at each other believing they were onto something. “Inside—the—Queen—concealed—and—buried—the—Holy—” Ailia’s heart jumped. “We need something to write on,” Ailia said. She cursed herself for not bringing her bag.
“I will go ask Bishop Peter.” Ivar ran toward the church even before Ailia had a chance to answer. He came back with Bishop Peter on his heels. 
“What have we here?” Bishop Peter asked politely, still securing his overcoat around his waist with a pen and scroll in hand.
“We believe there is a message on the rune stone,” Ailia replied. “Will you write the words while I read them out loud?”
“Of course,” Bishop Peter said. “I often wondered if this rune stone had any other purposes,” he said, sliding his hand across the words. “Whenever you are ready, Ailia.”
Ailia nodded and started deciphering the message from the beginning again. It was almost dark outside now and difficult to make out the letters. “Inside—the queen concealed and buried the Holy—Northern Jewel and Sacred—Consecrated writings for the one—elect being—Born and pre-mortally destined for—every victorious end. These will conquer the destroyer—All are hidden inside—Bergendal Church. 
“Read it for me Bishop Peter,” Ailia said, so excited she could hardly stand it. “Read it! Does it make any sense?” 
He read it out loud for her and when he finished, they shot excited glances at each other.
“How did you figure it out?” Bishop Peter asked. 
“The Valknut Symbol. Three sides, three triangles, every—third—word—” Ailia said, slowing toward the end. “Is there a Valknut symbol inside the church?” 
Bishop Peter thought for a moment. “Well, the church was built for your mother, by your mother’s builders, so I imagine that it must be where the scrolls are hidden or buried. I don’t remember there being one, but I wasn’t the bishop here when the church came into service. If there’s a Valknut symbol inside, it’s probably hidden in some detailed painting or carving which would go unnoticed by anyone.”
“I think it would be wise to continue the search now. What do you think?” Ivar said.
“Yes, I agree. Let me first make sure my Deacons are praying properly in their rooms before we start rampaging the church. I think they would think I have gone mad and it’s best to keep things like these between us only. Shall we?” Bishop Peter said.
It was wonderful to be indoors again. Ailia had barely noticed how freezing and numb her fingers had become from being outside. Her cheeks burned as they entered into the warm air. 
After Bishop Peter had settled his Deacons, he came back out and pointed them each in different directions to look for the Valknut symbol. 
There were numerous paintings and engravings and Ailia fretted, thinking it would take way too long to find the Valknut symbol. Maybe if she read the writings from her mother again, there may be some clues as to where she had hidden the scrolls and the Aesira Jewel.
“The rune stone mentions 'Great,' which could also mean Jesus or Mary. It also mentioned the destroyer, which could mean Satan or sinners. Maybe we should start there,” Ailia yelled out into the chapel.
“Shh—” Bishop Peter cautioned her. 
“Oh, I’m sorry. In my excitement I forgot to be reverent in the Lord’s house,” Ailia said. 
“No, that’s not what I meant. I just don’t want the Deacons to hear us,” Bishop Peter said.
Ailia smirked. She meandered over in front of the Rotunda and looked up at the hovering Crucifix. She let her eyes wander the room, searching for any triangular symbols in the paintings that covered the walls. Before, when she had studied these paintings, she had searched for the meaning of life and the meaning of the afterlife. Now, as she let her eyes glide across the canvases, she was hunting for a sign that would save not only her life, but also the life of all people in Midgard. Where could the Valknut symbol be? Her mother would not have hidden it too well, for her mother would have had to known that no one would ever find it. “Anything?” she yelled to Ivar.
“No, nothing here,” Ivar said.
Ailia entered the holy rotunda and looked at the painting of the last supper on the vaulted ceiling. Christ was centered in the middle, with his apostles surrounding Him, partaking of the first sacrament. She looked around to see if she could find a small stool to stand on to get a better view and grabbed a small stool that was stationed on the floor next to the pulpit. Standing on it, she could almost reach the ceiling now. She squinted her eyes, trying to make out the details. 
“Here you are. This will help you see better,” Bishop Peter said, handing her a torch. “Just don’t hold it too close to the paintings or you will burn them.” 
Ailia gladly accepted the torch and held it up closer to the ceiling while Bishop Peter walked away to continue his own search.
“Perhaps your mother didn’t embed the scrolls in the ceiling. They could be on statues, on the walls or on the floors,” Ivar said, coming over with his own torch. He then proceeded over to the square soapstone font.
“Maybe,” Ailia said. She hopped down from the stool and looked down on the rotunda floor. To her amazement, a large, lighter-colored pine triangle was imbedded in the slightly darker floor. How had she not noticed it before? It was only one triangle though, not three like on the Valknut symbol. 
“Bishop Peter—over here!” she said, not wanting to be too excited but thinking she was definitely making progress.
“Yes, coming!” Bishop Peter rushed over to Ailia.
“Look,” she said, pointing to the floor with her torch. “A triangle.”
He looked down at the floor and his gigantic eyes looked even larger than normal. “Yes, a triangle.” 
“Do you think my mother could have separated the three triangles and placed each one in separate locations in the church?” Ailia asked.
“Well, the triangle is a symbol in Christianity too, but it could mean something. Let’s keep looking for more,” Bishop Peter said, moving his slender, gangly frame. 
They discovered another triangle embedded in the floor by the front door. They kept searching for a third one, but they couldn’t find it. 
Finally, Ivar found something. “Come look over here,” he said, pointing to the soapstone font. “Around the font, on the floor—” He circled his torch above the floor.
“The third triangle,” Bishop Peter said.
“It has to be it. Three triangles, just like on the Valknut symbol,” Ailia said. “Let’s try and dig up the one in the rotunda first.”
“All right, but we must be careful to not damage the floor,” Bishop Peter cautioned as they ran over to the rotunda. He carefully guided his finger along the edge of the triangle to see if there was any ledge to lift it. After trying for a while, he said, “Let’s try the font instead.”
They made their way over and all stood in silence for a moment, looking at the square soapstone font. Four stout pillars comprised the font’s base and Jesus, Mary and the Apostles were all carved into the pillar walls. 
Bishop Peter knelt down and crawled around the font, his hands searching the surface of it. “There is something here that resembles—a triangle,” the Bishop said as he looked behind one of the four pillars. 
Ailia stepped closer and knelt down beside him to look. It was hard to see, but she could make out a small Valknut symbol hidden in the sophisticated design.
“The Valknut symbol!” Ailia said.
“There it is!” Bishop Peter said. He scooted in closer to get a better look. 
All of a sudden, a torch broke through one of the stained-glass windows. The sharp, crashing sound of glass hitting the floor echoed through the chapel. The torch hit Ailia’s leg, the flames nearly setting fire to her dress. She kicked the torch away and brushed the flames off her skirt. 
“Are you all right?” Ivar asked. “Did it burn you?”
“No, I’m fine. What’s happening?” she asked.
One of the young Deacons burst in through the back door. “We are being attacked by Vikings!” he yelled, his eyes glowing with the fear of death. “They are setting the church on fire!  What do we do?” he asked Bishop Peter frantically. 
They all looked to Bishop Peter for their next move, but he was paying no attention to them. His focus was on the impressively stunning jewel he had just pulled from inside the hollow font.



27

Confession 

The young girl had disappeared as quickly as she had appeared, running into the forest, vanishing into the shadows of the moonlit night. Lucia had tried to run after her, but Soren urged her not to. 
“When we get closer to Solnes, we will need to carefully assess the situation. If what that girl told us is true, then we will not be staying over in Solnes. We will have to find a detour and continue our journey through the night. We can then camp where it is safe. It will be too risky for us to stay there,” Soren said.
“I just hope the girl is not right,” Lucia said, but her gut told her that she was. 
“Chances are she is. Young children are not usually the target of Vikings. They are looking for youths or adults who can be made into thralls or sold to become thralls. I just hope the attack is local and they have not thought to attack Bergendal where your family is. Maybe we should be heading back?” 
“No, I am sure they are fine. Bergendal is so far away from here,” Lucia said.
“I am worried about Ailia and the others. Their lives could be in danger,” Soren said.
“Well, Soren, you are just one man. It is not like you could save them if the Vikings had attacked,” Lucia said. “We must focus on our lives, building our future together and let the others go,” Lucia pushed.
“Something does not feel right,” Soren said, ignoring Lucia’s prodding. He looked unusually pale. “I could help a great deal if they were in trouble,” Soren said. “I know you are still learning, but we Sentinors are capable of a lot more than you might think.”
“They will be fine,” Lucia said, knowing if they went back, her lies would be exposed.
“Do not take this the wrong way, but if it were my family back there, I would turn back in an instant.” Soren sounded stern.
“Yes, I am sure you would, but you do not know Ailia the way I know her.” Which is true, she thought.
“I hope us leaving so abruptly and her affection for me, hasn’t caused a rift between the two of you?” Soren said. 
Lucia sighed impatiently. “Of course not.” Why is he bothering me about this?
“Because family is so important—at least to me,” Soren said.
“Me too,” Lucia said. He does not understand how my family has lied to me, betrayed me and used me. I hate them for that. 
“I get the impression that you are holding something back from me—” Soren said.
“No, not that I can think of,” Lucia replied, short and sweet. “Ailia was just getting too pushy and I felt very uncomfortable when she told me that she had feelings for you.” 
 “So, everything is not fine between you two. Is that what I am hearing? I sensed some tension from you while Ailia was around,” Soren pressed.
“Everything is just fine,” Lucia said, trying to smile naturally. 
“I have to be honest with you. I feel that there is something you are not telling me. I want us to be able to have a completely open relationship and not have to hide anything. Are you sure everything is fine?” Soren said.
Lucia exhaled sharply. I cannot do this anymore. He will find out soon; I am sure of it. Besides, it probably is not worth it anyway. She squeezed her eyes closed and huffed. Should I tell him? Almost being too afraid to consider the thought.
“Lucia?” he asked, waiting for her reply.
“Please just leave me alone! I do not want to talk about it. Can you not understand that?” she snapped.
“Lucia, whatever it is, I am sure we can talk through it. Nothing should come between us, especially not your family. I hope you can trust me enough to open up,” Soren said. 
Lucia looked at him. He must know. He must just be waiting for me to admit my transgression. He was probably just playing with her, tormenting her with his crafty plans and bogus ideals. “There is nothing wrong, do you understand?” she said.
“Well, I would say there is something very wrong, or you would not be acting up like this.”
“Very well. You want me to admit it? I am not who you think I am, Soren,” Lucia finally said. She gasped from what she had said and her hand hit her mouth.
“What do you mean? How could you be anyone other than yourself, anyone other than Lucia—the Bearer of Light?” Soren asked.
She could not hold back her tongue. “Think about it Soren. I am the Bearer of Light.”
“Yes—?” 
“What does Bearer of Light mean?” Lucia asked.
“That you bear the light, I suppose. Why are we talking about this? I just want to see if I can help you in your relationship with Ailia,” Soren said.
“That is just the point. You do not get it.” Lucia had had enough of him. He did not love her. That was obvious. The truth hurt and if she was completely honest with herself, this whole made up story had only been about Lucia getting her way, not about love.
“Well, I would if you told me.” Soren seemed frustrated now.
“Why are you trying to humiliate me?” Lucia asked.
“I am not. I would never try to humiliate you.”
“Well, you are.” Lucia folded her arms in front of her chest.
“If I am, I apologize, but that is not my desire,” Soren said softly, as sincerely as Lucia had ever heard him.
Something snapped inside Lucia. She could not keep up the façade for another minute. This was going to be painful on so many levels. She truly adored Soren, but did she love him? No. And he did not love her either. He needs to love me for me, if this is going to work, not because he thinks I am Lucia, or Ailia, or blah! Even thinking about it made her crazy. Besides, I do not want to turn out to be like my parents. It was the most genuine thought she had had all day. 
“I do not know how to say this, but I have deceived you.” Lucia sunk her face into the palms of her hands. I cannot believe I am telling him. There is still time to run away, to keep up the illusion. My parents’ illusion—my illusion.
Soren remained silent for a moment. 
“Please forgive me. You see, I thought I loved you and I always thought that you would become my husband. Ever since my childhood I dreamt of us being married and raising a family together,” Lucia said.
“And we will,” Soren said softly, still smiling, unaware of the revelation he was about to have. “Wait, you do not love me?”
“No, I do not and no, we will not raise a family.” Lucia cried and laughed at the same time. She stopped the horse and jumped off, not knowing what to do with herself. She paced back and forth on the snow-covered road.
“Lucia, what are you doing?” he asked and jumped off of Volomite, rushing over to her. He slowly turned her around and looked into her eyes. “I love you and we are going to get married. Trust me, no matter what you have done, it is fine,” Soren said. “It is not as if I am perfect either, you know. While I was waiting for you, I was sorely tempted to—”
“Please, just let me speak!” Lucia said, though now she was curious about what he had done.
“I love you. That is what is true and that is all we need to know here and now.” He kissed her on the forehead. “I know it has been confusing with Ailia in the picture, but—we have to do what is right.”
“You do not know what I have done,” she said, twisting her way out of his arms. “You do not know who I am. I have been such a—liar,” Lucia said, finally giving herself the name, which was rightfully hers.
Soren scooped her back up into his embrace and held her. 
Lucia enjoyed having his affection, but she knew it was just a matter of time before he would despise her and see nothing more than a fraudulent liar standing before him. She hated him for that already. “I am not your Spiritus Amor. You are not accepting or even trying to hear what I have to say. You are holding onto your truth so strongly that any other would be a lie!” Lucia could tell from his facial expression that he was trying really hard to wrap his mind around what she was saying, but he was still not quite getting it. “Kiss me,” Lucia said. She just wanted to see if there could ever be any passion between them and wanted to have him want her one more time. 
He pulled her closer and squeezed her tight in his arms and then kissed her gently at first. 
Lucia tried to soak up the love Soren was showering her with, the attention he was giving her so freely. But it felt so wrong. His kiss became more passionate now, a force to quench his soul’s fiery thirst. He was honest and true. She was deceitful. Maybe, she was starting to feel the passion now? She tried. But no, it was not there. It had never been. 
She knew what she had to do and pulled back abruptly. She could take no more of these lies, her lies that corroded away her insides burning them useless and unrecognizable. Ailia would hate her for this. Soren would hate her for this. She knew it, but she could not care about their feelings anymore. She should only care that she did not continue to live the lies of her parents and that she was true to herself. “I will understand if you do not want to see me ever again after I tell you the whole truth,” she said, looking away, hiding the appalling reality, which could easily be read in her eyes had Soren only looked more closely. 
“This is not the Lucia I know,” Soren said. “You are acting so different from what I remember.”
Lucia could see in his eyes that he had begun to question who she was. “Please, shut up and just let me speak! Do not say another word. I need to think about how I am going to say this to you,” Lucia said, her brows furrowing in deep thought.
“Very well,” Soren said without another word.
Lucia wanted to be as direct as possible with him, so there was no room for any more ‘misunderstandings’. “Ailia is the Great Sentinor. I am not.” Lucia looked away and waited for his reaction.
“What?” Soren said, taking a step closer to her. 
“My parents named me Lucia so that everyone, including you, would think that I am the Great Sentinor. They did this to protect Ailia, the true Great Sentinor, so she would have a chance at destroying Eiess. They thought that if they created this—lie, her life would be spared. They used me!” Lucia was yelling now. 
Soren’s face paled. “You are not joking, are you—?” It was more of a statement than a question.
Lucia shook her head as her eyes welled up with regrets of her duplicity. She looked at his face, searched his eyes and saw the moment of change when he fully realized the truth. It was that moment he went from loving her to despising her. “Soren, I am so sorry. I just, I—something came over me and I just wanted to be with you. I—I thought I loved you. I wanted to love you,” she tried to explain. She could see that her apologies meant nothing to him.
Soren gestured to her to be quiet. “At no time in my lengthy life have I ever felt so betrayed,” he said. “How long have you known?”
“Ever since I met Ailia,” Lucia said. “Well, before, actually. I was the one who told her who she was.”
He nodded briefly, not looking at her. “We need to get back now.”
Lucia nodded.
He walked over to Volomite but stopped and turned to face Lucia again. “Do you know that I struggled with whether to go with you or not? I felt something for Ailia, but I chose to do what I thought was right. I chose to go with you because I trusted you and because I thought you were—Never mind what I thought. I was wrong. You have not got a single decent bone in your body. Do you know the damage you have caused us? Do you know that Ailia could be captured by the Vikings right now?” His voice was quiet but brutal.
“I am sorry, Soren,” Lucia said, barely able to speak. “I am so sorry.” She did truly mean it.
He looked at her coldly. “I will hear no more words from your lips, for your words are words of dishonesty and deception.” He jumped on his horse and waited for Lucia to mount hers. They galloped down the snowy path in silence, both carrying their own tender burdens and remorseful regrets.
Lucia knew she could not object and that she was nothing but a problem and hindrance to him now. Her heart mourned the loss of a love that was never rightfully hers. And what would she say to Ailia once she met her face to face? She did not want to confront her sister who she had betrayed. No, Ailia betrayed me!  Perhaps, if she just let Soren ride ahead of her, she could just slip away forever.



28

Captured 

“Bishop!” Ailia yelled the loudest she thought her voice could yell, pulling his attention back to reality. “The Vikings are burning the church down!” 
Bishop Peter looked at Ailia. “What?” he said, looking almost as if he had just woken up.
“We must stop the Vikings, or they’ll burn the church down and the scrolls with it!” Ailia said.
One more torch cracked through another stained-glass window, landing on one of the pews, bouncing on impact. Ivar darted over, picked it up and chucked it back out the same broken window it had entered. 
 Suddenly, the front door opened with full force, the splintering sound of breaking wood reaching all the way to the font where Ailia and the others stood. A heavily-weapon-laden Viking with long blond hair and a full red beard entered in through the door. Four other large Vikings followed behind him. Their thick burgundy wool tunics all had black embroidered ravens on them and on their heads, they wore simple iron helmets that covered their ears and necks. 
“My, oh my, look who we have here.” The leader said and smiled wickedly when he saw Ailia. “Finn, grab that one there!  She’s mine! Remember me, Ailia?” the leader said, pointing at Ailia as soon as he saw her.
“Should I?” Ailia asked. Is that—?
“Gunnar!” Ivar yelled from the pews, stepping into the hallway, becoming a barrier between Ailia and their attackers. 
Ailia recognized him now. Gunnar had been a friend of their family long ago. But there was something more she wasn’t remembering. She could sense it.
“Ah, Ivar,” Gunnar said. “This time, you will not rob me of my woman.”
“She is not your woman. Leave her be, Gunnar. If our former friendship meant anything to you at all, leave her be and we will be on our way.” Ivar stood unwavering in his spot with his feet firmly planted on the floor. His hand was already reaching and preparing to unleash his sword.	
“Friendship?  We had no friendship. You made sure of that when you deprived me of my freedom. Don’t deceive yourself into thinking that I was ever your friend or you mine.” Gunnar spewed and spit on the floor, the frothy discharge landing right in front of Ivar. “Take her Finn,” Gunnar commanded again, signaling his head in Ailia’s direction while holding his hands on his hips as if he owned the city and everyone in it.
Finn quickly took to walking toward Ivar and Ailia, his sword already drawn from its casing. Backing up, Ailia panicked and tripped over a pew. Ivar responded by drawing his sword in return and stepping forward ready for assault. 
“Do not disgrace the Lord’s house!” Bishop Peter demanded, raising both hands up into the air where he still stood by the font. 
Meanwhile, Ailia could hear the hammering sounds of planks being nailed into the exterior walls over the windows. The Vikings were probably planning to pack the church full of Bergendalers and burn it down, but only after they had relieved the church of all its gold, silver and other valuables. 
Finn came down the center of the pews toward Ivar, swinging his sword so fast that it could cut through a man like butter. Their swords met, chiming loudly as they collided in the holy sanctuary.
“You know, Ivar, talk is cheap, but friendship is even cheaper. I’ve learned this valuable truth from you: once a friend, soon an enemy.” Gunnar was standing in the corner, shouting over the loud blows of the crashing swords. “I used to believe in it, like perhaps you still do, yet now, I find myself a much happier man. I’ve put aside those childish impracticalities and have become a real man. I find the money I want, I take the women I want, I demand respect and I live in excitement every day. Now, what man wouldn’t want that?”
The three other Vikings who were watching the duel nodded in agreement.
“Haven’t I given you all you ever wanted, boys?” Gunnar turned his head around and asked the Vikings.
“Yes!” they all praised.
“This is a Viking’s life, Ivar. Why don’t you join us and I’ll give you this little handmaiden as your first reward. Ah, look at her, Ivar! She is youthful, beautiful and she has a flower that is still waiting to be taken. I can taste girls like her a mile away. They are ready and waiting for a man to make a woman out of them! This time, she’ll break—I can feel it.”
His improper proposition sounded despicable to Ailia and she felt disgusted even thinking about his uncivilized suggestion. “Who do you think you are to give my life away?” she snapped from the back.
“Ooh, this will be a real exciting game,” Gunnar laughed. “I can already see the battle concluding in my favor. Your freedom has ended and now you finally belong to me, my sweet blossom!” he said, claiming his prize before he had won it.
Ailia felt violated and disgusted at the same time.
Ivar and Finn kept at each other, both powerfully fighting for their lives, though for two completely different reasons: one to take away freedom, one to keep it. 
The sword duel escalated, both men appearing physically tired, panting, groaning and grunting as they chopped at each other, destroying and hacking at the pews when they missed. Finn gave Ivar a shove, knocking him to the floor, and boastfully walked over to Ailia, who had now run into the rotunda behind the pulpit to distance herself from the fight. He held his sword high and came at her as if he would chop her down. 
She cringed, thinking her life was over.
Then, he lowered his sword, stroking it up against her face, pressing the blade into her skin. 
She felt the edge pierce her skin and she winced at the pain.
“Finn!” Gunnar yelled back at him. “The girl is mine! You lay a finger on her and you die,” he warned, leaving no room for misunderstandings.
Finn laughed mockingly, but he obeyed his chieftain and stepped back.
Ailia never imagined that the battle for her life would start so soon and she regretted having given into her laziness and not planning better for an attack such as this one. Ailia could see that Ivar was out of practice and he looked ashamed for having taken the first beating of the combat. This could very well cost her and Ivar their lives. 
Ivar got up, shook off his defeat and snuck up right behind Finn, raising his sword high up so he could finish him off. But the savage was too quick. Finn dodged Ivar’s sword, which became imbedded in the altar instead. 
The Vikings all laughed as Ivar jerked the sword out of the sanctified table.
“So, what do you say, Ivar? I’d hate to see a strong, trained soldier like you return home to Valhalla years too early. Life’s simply too enjoyable to leave. Join me and you’ll all be spared and live and reign with us as kings—and queen—” he said, looking at Ailia. “—of Midgard!” Gunnar promoted. He was a good advocate for the Viking life. 
“I am not at all enticed by your future promises. A man who would live off of the welfare of others is no man to respect or follow. You think this is the life, Gunnar? Living off of others, murdering innocent lives, plundering churches and instilling fear in the hearts of innocent people? The friend I once knew is dead if this is the man who is standing before me,” Ivar yelled as he fought off Finn, jabbing at him quicker than before, trying to finish him off. “I will never join you, for living a life like yours is worth less than not existing at all. I do not live to serve my passions like a primitive animal that only knows how to eat, sleep and piss, but I live so that others may benefit from my life.” 
Their swords met, metal to metal, ringing with every clash. Finn lunged forward, jabbing at Ivar who aggressively riposted Finn’s advance by swerving around and forcing his sword to the ground. Ivar stepped on Finn’s arm, locking his weapon useless. Pressing his sword up to Finn’s throat, he said, “Now, let us go and we will spare your lives.”
Gunnar started clapping, applauding the duel’s end, but laughed mockingly at his idealistic command. “In case you have not noticed, I have three other willing strong Vikings ready to fight, with hundreds more outside and if I charge all of them on you, you will be dismembered faster than the flash of Thor’s almighty lightning bolt.”
Finn was tugging uselessly to get out of Ivar’s deadlock, trying to free his arm. 
“I am not playing games, Gunnar. I will not bat an eyelash if I have to take this Viking’s life, especially if it means I must do that to get out of here alive. Now, let us go. One more threat from you and I will end this man’s life,” Ivar said.
Gunnar paused. “Well, I’m sure glad I’m not in Finn’s position,” he said. “You’re a skilled swordsman, Ivar, I’ll give you that. Even at your age and with the extra blubber you’ve put on. It appears I’ve underestimated you and, in the process, overestimated Finn.” 
Finn snarled at Gunnar.
“I hope this is not still about King Olav rejecting you in his guard?” Ivar said.
Gunnar’s face froze. “No, this is about Ragnvei,” Gunnar said.
“But, I spared your life and then you left her to die!” Ivar said.
“It was your fault that she died!” Gunnar yelled back. 
Finn looked at Gunnar with pleading eyes, but it was to no avail. 
“Kill the damn fool,” Gunnar said, staring Ivar in the eyes. “He’s been a very disappointing Viking from the start.”
He left Ivar no choice, if he wanted to live and have any chances of saving Ailia. Ivar raised his sword and, with one deft move, decapitated Finn.
“Now, Ivar, why did you have to go that route?” Gunnar said. He looked at Bishop Peter whose jaw was wide open. “Just remember who desecrated the church first, Your Holiness,” he said, raising his eyebrows as a smile glazed his lips.
Bishop Peter ran through the back door and disappeared, with the Aesira Jewel in hand.
“Bishop!” Ailia yelled after him, but he didn’t respond. Is he a coward after all?
 Ivar stood up tall and gave Gunnar a fierce look. “Who’s next?” he said. 
The other three Vikings looked ready to attack at Gunnar’s command.
“Get that bastard and kill him. And bring me the girl alive!” Gunnar commanded.
All three Vikings started moving, slightly hesitantly, toward Ivar. Just then, Bishop Peter came back out without the Aesira Jewel, but with a loaded crossbow. He wasted no time in shooting one of the Vikings in the chest and quickly re-loaded the crossbow again with another bolt. This time, he leveled the crossbow directly at Gunnar. “I’m going to give you one chance to get out, or I’ll shoot again,” he said, his hands shaking like leaves on a tree in the middle of a storm. “I’m going to count to three and then I’ll shoot again,” he warned.
“Now, certainly we can work this out, Your Excellency—” Gunnar said, his voice as humble as an angel’s. 
“One—” Bishop Peter could not be deterred. “Two—”
“All right, all right, I will take my men and leave,” Gunnar said, finally backing down, oozing, still scheming victory from his eyeballs. The remaining Vikings left the chapel and closed the double doors behind them.
“They’ve surrendered too quickly. They’ll be back soon with more men,” Ailia said frantically. 
“Where in Thor’s name did you get that crossbow and learn how to use it?” Ivar asked Bishop Peter.
“Well, I’ve traveled quite a bit. I purchased this piece from a constable from the Southlandic Kingdom when I was there. Most people don’t know this, but before I committed to becoming a Bishop, I was training to become an archer for King Olav,” he said, looking rather proud.
“We will talk later,” Ivar said.
Bishop Peter agreed and said, “I think the best exit strategy is to depart from the back of the church.”
Ailia folded her arms stubbornly across her chest. “We cannot and I will not leave without the scrolls. If the church burns down, I’ll burn down with it.”
“We have no choice. There is no time to look, now,” Ivar barked, sweating from the duel.
“But, they will burn the church down and then we’ll never have a chance to find them,” Ailia said.
“We have to take that chance. Hopefully they will be content with the gold and silver and leave the church be,” Ivar said.
“Not likely,” Bishop Peter said. “It’s been said that their goal is to burn all Christian churches in the Northlandic Kingdom, leaving none for the worship of the heathens we supposedly are.”
“To live life in oppression or die trying for a mightier cause, which will it be? Which one must it be?” Ailia asked, still believing a path would reveal itself for the liberation of the manuscripts tonight.
“A scroll of no substantial importance may have been left here to burn, but the scrolls of deliverance for all beings in Midgard is worth dying for in trying to obtain,” Bishop Peter said. “No time like the immediate, for tomorrow we may be dead, serving life and God’s people no longer. It’s in the now we must take action to become the heroes of the morn’,” he finished. He sounded like he was in his preaching mode again but speaking at twice his usual pace.
“Yes!” Ailia said excited.
“Very well,” Ivar conceded.
A sledgehammer and wedge were quickly located and the triangle floor underneath the rotunda was easily removed. Underneath the floor was a bronze chest placed inside a square wooden cavern. The trio looked at each other in excitement, but their joy was short-lived. 
“It’s a shame I don’t give up so easily,” Gunnar announced as he entered the church again, this time accompanied with a whole gang of ravage-hungry Vikings.
Ailia and Bishop Peter blocked the newfound treasure with their bodies while Ivar rapidly re-laid the floor. They heard the thud behind them as the triangle descended back into its frame.
It appeared Gunnar had planned the next attack in advance, for his Vikings all strategically went after their own prisoners.
“Remember boys, the girl is mine,” Gunnar said, oblivious to their huge discovery. “Anyone who tarnishes her will suffer death by the blood eagle.” 
Ailia had vaguely heard of this method of torture and she knew that threatening this unthinkably gruesome method would immediately make lambs of wolves in any situation. She had heard that it was known as the most entertaining way to kill a man and that its slow assassinating method was the most feared and most morbid of any other torture method.
Two Vikings grabbed Ailia and another three, Ivar. Six others went to the back to round up Bishop Peter who had disappeared through the backdoor again.
“Don’t worry, Ailia. Finally with Ava gone for good, I’ll make a woman out of you this time,” Gunnar said as the two Vikings dragged her past him, out into the night.




29

Bergendal is Burning 

Lucia rode behind Soren, who rode Volomite and Lucia’s horse senseless. She felt guilt-ridden and now it was official: she was a fraud of a woman. They stopped only occasionally for water for the horses and nothing else. Silence burned her ears and not even the pounding hooves of the horses drowned out the annihilating blizzard she felt coming from him. Lucia suspected Soren wanted to hear nothing from her, yet she wanted to confess to him everything that had made her come to the decision to deceive him. Volomite seemed to be trained well, but Lucia’s horse had a hard time keeping up with the fast, furious pace Soren was setting. 
In front of them, in the moonlight, Lucia saw another rider on the side of the road. As they neared, she saw that it was Silya. She did not want to face her—not at all. 
Silya looked as if she was waiting for something or someone. 
Maybe she is waiting for us? Lucia thought.
“What brings you in this direction?” Soren asked as they neared her, their breaths now mingling in the dark, smoking up the chilling air.
“I was following you. There has been a severe violation of trust, an illegitimate theft of identity—” Silya looked at Lucia sternly, her eyes piercing right through her. “Lucia is—”
“Yes, Lucia told me all about her sham,” Soren said plainly and without even a hint of emotion. 
Lucia cringed at his words.
“I am heading back to Ailia. Have the Vikings entered Bergendal?” Soren asked.
 “Not that I know of, but I did see some traces of other Viking activity nearby,” Silya said.
Lucia’s arms folded tightly in front of her chest as if that would protect her somehow. Suddenly, she heard a sound. “What is that sound?” Lucia asked surprised. “It sounds like a baby is crying.” She heard the helpless screams a ways back from the main road.
“It is a Viking child,” Silya said coldly. “His mother died.”
“So, you already checked on him?” Lucia asked. How could she be so calloused? She hopped off her horse.
“Leave him be,” Silya said more coldly this time. “He is a Viking. He does not deserve to live.”
“We cannot just leave him here to die, poor baby. It is not his fault his parents are Vikings,” Lucia said. 
“Never mind that now. He has no right to live, a babe with Viking blood running through his veins,” Silya said angrily, though not quite convincing. “It is hard enough to listen to a helpless babe cry without you complaining.” Silya rolled her dark brown eyes as if she did not care. “Fine, you go get him, but I want nothing to do with him, you hear? He will be all your responsibility and yours alone.”
“I am amazed that you have such a hard heart for this abandoned, vulnerable creature,” Lucia said. 
“You are definitely not one to speak,” Silya said, moving closer to Lucia.
Soren stepped between the two women. “It is all right, Silya. There’s nothing we can do now to change what has happened.”
 Lucia headed as quickly as she could toward the screaming child, stepping in Silya’s tracks in the snow. She heard Soren speak to Silya as she walked off.
“I am going to leave you with Lucia. I will meet you back in Bergendal when you have figured out this situation,” Soren said. “I will go ahead of you because I need to get back to Ailia.”
The closer Lucia got to the baby, the less she could hear Soren’s voice beneath the baby’s cries. Poor baby. She saw the wagon and, then, the moving bundle on the mother’s stiff corpse. Poor Mother. Lucia’s heart ached for the both of them. The gods have brought me this child, she thought. It is a sign from the gods! Her new life would start now, she decided. Without knowing yet what that new life might entail, she picked up the baby and held him close. He fussed for a while longer and then he settled into a low whimper. I could leave now. Run into the woods and never come back. They probably would not care. But she felt the risk was too great. She decided to wait until she got back to Bergendal. When she arrived back by the road, Soren had already disappeared. Lucia felt both relieved and disappointed.
“So, you got hold of the Viking?” Silya said.
“Yes.”
“Well, he is all yours. Do not bother me with him,” Silya said.
Silya and Lucia made their way back to Bergendal with Lucia carrying the small infant. She worried what she would give him to eat and if he was warm enough in her arms.
“What will you name the berserker?” Silya asked.
“I do not know. Do you know of any good names fit for a king?” Lucia asked.
“The mother was a bloodthirsty Viking named Ava and the child’s father is supposedly a king. That is what the mother said right before she died anyway. Probably a king of the war-thirsty Vikings,” Silya said.
“Ava? Lucia thought she had heard that name before. Was not that the name of my father’s mistress? The age of the child did not fit with the timing of her father’s affair, she concluded. Probably just a coincidence. “His name will be Harald. It means ruler,” Lucia said, ignoring Silya’s resentfulness.

*    *    *

As they came up close to Bergendal, Silya slowed down her horse. Heavy clouds of smoke and fire rose to the dark, cloudy heavens. The sweet scent of blood lingered in the air and an intense feeling of danger engulfed them as they entered the normally peaceful town. 
Lucia heard people clamoring, crying and wailing from a distance. It sounded like the cries of war, of mourning mothers and fathers, brothers and friends and it smelled of demise. Bergendal was burning like it had never burned before. 
Silya jumped off her horse and led both horses forward. The creatures neighed nervously and flicked their tails. 
Lucia could feel her horse’s muscles tense up in nervousness. The horse’s ears pinned up to listen. “Whoa, boy,” Lucia said, stroking the beast’s upper back to soothe him. 
Another horse came galloping down the road toward them at full speed. The horse’s eyes shone with terror. He was carrying someone, who looked wounded, on his back. 
Silya raised both hands up into the air to calm the dashing horse. The horse slowed and a young man fell off him. The horse then took off galloping again. 
Blood dripped from the man’s wounded arm into the snow, blood looking as black and thick as boiling tar in the darkness.
Silya knelt beside him. “I heard there have been local Viking attacks. Is that what has happened here?” she asked, lifting the young man’s head out of the snow and placing it on her lap. 
“The church—they are burning down the church,” the young man said, his voice labored and panting. “I am a Deacon of the Lord. Save the church,” he begged.
Lucia did not want to save the church. She thought that Bergendal was probably being attacked because they had let the Christian faith take root in the people’s hearts. She did feel sorry for the misguided Christians, though. She had to admit she thought the Vikings had been a little too coldblooded this time.
“Come with me.” Silya started to lift the man up onto Miika.
“No, no, don’t take me back to that place. Oh please. They will kill me!” He gripped Silya’s arm. “Please, I don’t want to die today.” 
“We will take you back to Brandersgaard with us then,” Silya said. 
“Thank you,” the Deacon said, his voice whimpering. 
After Silya had settled the Deacon on top of Miika, they all headed for Brandersgaard. 
Harald had started crying again. He is probably hungry, Lucia concluded. She needed to find milk for him soon.
Snow descended heavily now from the heavens, mingling with the smoke from the burning homes and barns. As they rode, they passed many fallen Bergendalers: women, children and men—They had all been stabbed to death, it looked like, their dead bodies painting the snow with their blood. Lucia did not see any Vikings, but the trail they left behind could not be missed.
As they neared Brandersgaard, Lucia’s heart started racing. They hate me and they will oust me, I am sure of it. When they arrived, they entered through the missing front door. The longhouse was still standing, regardless of all the fire damage to it. It was unusually murky inside and Lucia coughed as she entered the smoke-filled foyer. There was no sign of anyone, anywhere and that could either be a very good thing or a very tragic thing. 
“Hello?” Silya’s voice sang, sounding muted and hesitant. She helped the Deacon sit down by the hearth, which was burning unusually strong. Its crackling sound permeated throughout the main room and its flames rose twice as high as they usually did. A portion of the roof had been torn or burned off at the front of the room. Snow entered through the hole onto the loom, turning it white where it stood as beams of subdued moonlight streamed through the gaping hole. They walked slowly to the back of the longhouse. Shelves and cupboards were tipped over, but there was still no sign of Ailia or the others.
Sigrid peeked her head in through the front door. “Silya?  Lucia?” she said.
“Sigrid!” Silya shouted. She looked happy to see her. “Are you well?”
Sigrid limped toward them between the rubble and ashes on the floor. She fell into Silya’s arms, sobbing. “Yes, I’m well, but so many are dead,” she cried.
“Unni and Brander?” Silya asked.
“They haven’t returned and Ailia is gone. The Vikings took her,” Sigrid said. “The Vikings took my Ailia.” She buried her face in her soot-covered, bloody hands and cried.
“Many souls will return to Valhalla tonight,” Lucia said, coldly. 
Silya looked at Lucia and then back at Sigrid. “Did Soren come back?” she asked.
“Yes. He took Ivar with him to go after Ailia a while ago,” Sigrid said. “Though, they didn’t really know where to start looking. They thought maybe they’d head to the Viking settlement south of here.”
“Strange, we didn’t see them on our way,” Silya said. 
Harald had started to fuss again. “I need to find milk for my child,” Lucia said. 
“You go do that,” Silya said and gave her a mean look.
Lucia did not want to take this kind of abuse. She stepped outside and started walking, but to where, she did not know. The snow came down heavily now, splotches of white fluff falling and sticking to her clothes and hair. How dare they treat me, a queen, this way? I should have left sooner. But where would she have gone? And where would she go now? She knew no one in Bergendal and if she kept wandering the streets, she would surely freeze to death. 
I do not want to live anymore, Lucia thought. She decided to try and find a dagger or a knife and then, she would kill the baby first and then herself. It was an honorable death—was it not?  Will the gods accept me into Valhalla? She’d be free from this miserable life and Ailia and Soren would regret ever having treated her the way they did. 
She pulled a dagger out of a dead man’s chest and found a forlorn, halfway burned down barn. I will do it in here. She sat down on the floor and took her mittens off. Holding the dagger up high above the infant, she paused and cried. Is this my destiny? To die instead of rule as queen? To be made a fool of by my sister and her lover?  
She lowered the dagger and held it into her chest. “This is not why I was born,” she said out loud. “This is not why I was born!” she screamed as loud as she could. I am queen! I am queen! I am queen! Aesira blood runs through my veins!  
Then, she had a thought. She almost did not dare let the thought take root, but she was desperate. Eiess might want her. Eiess might now see the value in her. Eiess was evil, yes, but so were her parents. They betrayed me! My sister and her lover betrayed me! Together, Eiess and she would be unstoppable. And she was more on Eiess’s side than on Ailia and Soren’s anyway, was she not? She was too exhausted to reason with herself. All she could think was that she was valuable and she knew that Eiess would see her value now. It is either back to Brandersgaard or back to Eiess or death.



30

Caged

Ailia woke up in a small wooden cage-type crate, which was propped up against the wall of a large house, how large she couldn’t tell. The box prison was too small to stand up in, but wide enough that she could almost stretch her legs all the way out in one direction when she lay down. The floor was covered with hay and old frayed fabric. 
It was difficult to see out between the tightly assembled wooden planks, but slits between the construction allowed her to make out five large houses that were built in a circle. There were also many more houses beyond that, it looked like. This settlement was large.
She assumed she was at the Viking settlement that she and Soren had passed by on their way back to Bergendal many months ago. Hannah was probably imprisoned here. 
Right next to her, it looked as if there were a few more cages propped up against the wall and she thought there might be one more person inside the cage furthest away from her. “Hello?” she called.
Movement came from the other cage. “Hello?” a woman’s voice beckoned in return.
“Where are we?” Ailia asked.
“Where are we?” the answer came. The woman’s accent revealed that she was not from here.
 Ailia grimaced. Why is she playing games? “Do you understand me?” Ailia asked.
“Do you understand me?” the answer came.
Ailia huffed. She must not understand me. Before she had lost consciousness, she remembered looking back at the burning church when Gunnar had dragged her off. It looked like Ivar was fighting with the Vikings who had grabbed hold of him and was winning. She truly hoped that was the case, especially since she didn’t see him anywhere here. 
Ailia couldn’t remember the last time she had cried, but now, with her body being cold and tired and being all alone, she had a hard time holding back the tears. 
Will I ever see Soren again, or is this the life that I need to become accustomed to? She couldn’t imagine things getting any worse and she hated being so vulnerable in the grasp of such a savage as Gunnar. Surely, as a demi-mortal, she would possess some supernatural powers that would help her out of this situation?  
Loud, unencumbered laughter from both men and women came from inside the main longhouse. It sounded like the Vikings were in a happy, highly-tipsy mood, probably from drinking all the mead they had just picked up in Bergendal and the surrounding villages. Most likely, they were proud of their conquests, reveling in their victory and congratulating themselves on such a successful night. 
If the Heavens would be merciful, Gunnar would drink himself to sleep tonight, forgetting about Ailia, who he had threatened to spend some one-on-one time with as soon as he had a chance. She cringed at the idea of that type of man touching her in any way. 
Suddenly, the door to the main longhouse burst open and Ailia jumped. Several cheerful Vikings staggered their way out into the central area halfway undressed, their round, pale bellies hanging out to meet the cold. Snow fell onto their clothes and bodies and they bellowed a song while they clung onto each other.


We, the sons of Thor the strong
Mightier than any throng
Fiercely feared by all the land,
From the seas until the sands.

We have lost some brethren dear,
 do not shed a tear
For they are in paradise today
Drinking mead and eating steak.

We will take whate‘er we see
Vikings, we are tough and free
Live today for present cares
Take your prize and spoil your shares.

Freemen we will always be
We live as Kings and royalty
When we are dead, we want to hear
Songs ‘bout us, a thousand years
 
Ailia sat in her cage, listening to their melody. They were awful singers—couldn’t even carry a tune—and their bigheaded lyrics repulsed her. One of the men passed out into the snow and all the others laughed, slapping their knees and nudging each other. Ailia thought that the unconscious man might be Gunnar and she felt relieved that she might be safe for a few more hours. Two men picked Gunnar up and dragged him inside of the longhouse across from her cage. They shut the door behind them and it grew quiet again, only their muffled voices escaping to the outside. 
Ailia shivered and knew that the longest part of the night was still ahead of her. Is this how I’m going to die: alone, freezing and never having known my love in this life?  
A couple came out arm in arm from another one of the longhouses. The man gently pressed the woman up against the outside wall and kissed her passionately. The woman laughed and cooed in approval. They stood there for a while, kissing and talking, before they finally walked back inside.
Ailia could only see one person guarding the entrance of the settlement and he looked as if he had fallen asleep. She thought she might try to escape. The cage didn’t look too well constructed, being built only from planks and nails, so it couldn’t be too difficult to break and exit. 
She started pushing the exterior wall with her feet as she lodged her back onto the opposite side. Nothing happened. Then, she kicked the planks as hard as she could again and again, trying to loose a board, any board, but there was no sign that any of them would loosen.
Then, from between two of the houses, she saw four Vikings approaching. She stopped kicking. As they got closer, Ailia could see that three of the Vikings held one prisoner. The man’s hands were tied behind his back and had a sackcloth pulled over his head. They treated him roughly and pushed and kicked him to move forward, laughing when he tripped over something in his path. The Vikings stopped for a while and secured their prisoner with their grips. They pointed to the empty cage next to Ailia and nodded in agreement. 
“Get in, dead man,” one of the Vikings said. He opened the top-hinged cage door and pushed the prisoner into the crate. The man groaned as he fell to the ground, taking a hard hit. The Vikings locked the crate by enclosing its exterior with a larger iron cage. 
No wonder I couldn’t get out. Its exterior is reinforced with iron, Ailia thought. After the Vikings had left, Ailia scooted closer to her new neighbor. “Are you all right, sir?” she asked. 
The stranger sat up awkwardly, with his hands still tied behind his back and the sackcloth over his head. “Ailia?” the voice replied. “Is that you?”
“Soren?” She was shocked, concerned and excited all at the same time, not knowing whether she should jump for joy or cry in the small confinements of her pen. “How did you get here?” Her eyes burst with tears of joy, mingled with sorrow, bitter tears of fear and hope welled simultaneously from the windows of her soul. “Soren, I—how—?  I don’t know what to say. I’m so glad, but sad that you’re here,” she finally said, her words not even coming remotely close to expressing her feelings. She tried to get a glimpse of him through the slivers of space. “How did you get here? Are you hurt?” 
Soren struggled for a brief moment to get the sackcloth off his head and was able to untie the ropes that bound his arms behind his back. “Ailia, Lucia told me the truth,” he said, reaching his fingers through an opening in his cage. Their eyes met in the darkness.
She slid her fingers through the cage and their fingers met, interlacing with mighty oneness. The cold air blew through their grasp. It was too cold to hold for long, but Ailia endured the pain to enjoy the pleasure and comfort of his touch. His fingers felt warm and rough to the touch. 
“You came for me,” she said.
Soren’s eyes rested in her gaze. “I wish I would have known sooner. I should never have left you.”
“I’m sorry I didn’t tell you before. I thought it was for the best and wanted it to be a special moment between us,” Ailia said, stretching her fingers longer to be closer to him. “Now, I wish I would have told you the moment I saw you again,” she said, hoping he would meet her heart where it burned.
“I knew in my heart that you were—are—you are my One. How long have you known?” he asked.
“I found out the day I arrived in Bergendal. I wanted to tell you then, but you were still travelling,” she said, her eyes looking away at the sadness of the memory. 
“I thought I was doing us both a favor by leaving, so I would not be tempted beyond what I could manage,” he said despondently. He turned quiet as if he struggled with whether to share something or not. 
Ailia looked back into his eyes again, encouraging him without words.
“I had a dream about you the night before I left you in the care of Silya and when I woke up, I knew that you were a threat to Lucia and I, because I felt a deep unexplainable love for you even then,” Soren confessed.
Ailia smiled as he spoke to her, of her and of the love he had for her. Her deepest, brightest longings had finally been realized in one of her coldest, darkest moments, a moment so close to death that she could taste it, yet she could not be happier.
“In my dream, you were so beautiful, Ailia and I could not even remember wanting anyone the way I wanted you. My love for you grows stronger over time, with every life you live,” Soren said.
Ailia’s smile widened. She knew now he loved her and only her, even before he knew who she really was. It was her soul and being and not her name, that was his One. “I thought you left because I had done something wrong, or because you didn’t care,” Ailia admitted, slightly embarrassed. She pulled her fingers away for a moment to warm them underneath her overcoat. The chilly wind had frozen them cold and her fingers stung as they thawed close to her body. “I knew I loved you too, from the moment we met—again,” she said, her heart and soul agreeing in unison as her fingers reached for his again.
“You are cold,” Soren said. “We need to get out of here before they kill us, or do anything else to harm us.”
“I’ve tried to kick my way out of here, but the entire wooden crate is enclosed by an iron cage,” Ailia said. At least now she had someone to share the loneliness with, though she would rather Soren be gone and safe than with her here, in this near hopeless predicament.
 “Ivar knows where we are. He came with me and I told him to wait in the forest while I looked for you,” Soren said.
“So Ivar escaped the Vikings in Bergendal?” Ailia asked.
“Yes.”
“How did the Vikings capture you?” Ailia wondered.
“Well,” he said, “I walked over to their camp and I let them seize me.” He nearly let out a laugh and a smirk lightened his face. 
“So, it was your plan to be captured?” Ailia asked, trying to decipher his strategy.
“Yes. I knew it was the only way to get close to you,” he said, still smiling.
Ailia smiled too. “Sounds like you are pretty set on being with me if this is the length you are willing to go.”
He turned more serious. “I would do anything to be with you, Ailia. I will never leave you again for as long as I live.”
The sound of her name on his lips felt like sunshine in her soul. 
“I would even sit here for the rest of my life, if that meant I could be close to you,” Soren said.
“Well, let’s not stay here that long,” Ailia said. Of course, it would be impossible for Soren to be captured and taken. He was the master of all hunting, tracking and waylaying methods, having been alive for centuries now. No Viking, no matter how skilled, could compare to Soren. I would do the same for him, she realized. Now Ailia didn’t feel as bad that he was here imprisoned with her. If anyone could get them out safely, it was him.
“Before I left Ivar, I told him to ride back to Bergendal as soon as I had been taken and round up as many able men as possible to come and attack the Vikings on their own territory. They should take back all that was stolen and plundered from them. I just hope he makes it back soon and that the chaos in Bergendal is not too maddening. It took me a good day’s trip to get here, so I would imagine we can be expecting them the day after tomorrow.”
“The Vikings all seem drunk, so I think we will be safe through the night. I doubt they will disturb us,” Ailia said. She felt tired and sleepy, but she didn’t want to sleep a minute as long as she was with Soren. She had longed for time with him, to be open with him and for them to get to know each other again. Every moment with him was a moment of ecstasy. Had she truly lived before she met him?



31

Thrall

When Ailia woke up, the first thing she did was to look for Soren over in the next cell. He was still sleeping and Ailia breathed a sigh of relief that he hadn’t suddenly disappeared in the night. She hadn’t been as cold as she thought she would be, maybe because the snow that covered the planks had helped keep some warmth inside. 
The sun started rising in the east and she could see that dunes of snow had covered the surfaces of everything, blanketing the longhouses, the courtyard and every tree around. Not a single cloud that Ailia could see from her limited view graced the blue sky. Ailia hoped the sun would warm her, as its rays would soon shine around the corner onto their prisons. 
As her stomach rumbled, the last meal she remembered eating was at the repast table at Unni and Brander’s house during the Late Summer Festival. She peered over and wondered whether she should wake Soren or not. She decided to let him sleep a while longer. 
Sitting in silence, she scoped out the territory for any weapons or other items Soren and her might appropriate if they managed to escape today. 
A different Viking stood watch over by the main entrance to the small settlement. He looked much more alert than the guard who had been standing there yesterday. A raven flew from the top of one of fern trees to another, cawing as it landed and disturbed its heavy snow-covered branches, causing clumps of snow to fall to and merge with, the snow below. Ailia hadn’t seen a raven in a long time. How fitting, she thought, a stupid raven. Most birds had flown south for the winter, the eternal winter it would seem. Not if I have anything to do with it. 
The door to the main longhouse opened and four stout Vikings immediately headed in Ailia and Soren’s direction. Gunnar probably lived in the largest of the five abodes and more than likely, he had sent his men to do something sinister. 
Ailia recognized one of the brutes from the previous day, but the others were unfamiliar. They waded two by two through the new, ankle-high snow with their hefty boots, plowing a path that would last until the next heavy snowfall.
“Soren,” Ailia said, trying to wake her still slumbering love. “Soren!” Now her voice became considerably louder as she feared they would take her away without him waking up. “They’re coming!  The Vikings are coming!”
Soren stirred for a second and then woke up, rising abruptly. He spotted the Vikings immediately. “Everything is going to be fine, Ailia,” he said, looking into her eyes across the double divide. “There is no escape for us at the moment, but Ivar will return soon with hundreds of angry Bergendalers. Just find a way to stay alive until that time, promise me!” 
Ailia nodded.
The eldest Viking lifted the steel cage off of Ailia’s prison dome and lifted the opening flap of the plank crate. It creaked loudly, its high-pitched scream sending chills through Ailia’s body.
“Come here, girl.” He grabbed her arm and jerked her out of the cage. The Viking let her go just as the force of the pull peaked and Ailia tumbled into the snow. 
 “Having trouble standing up?” one of the Vikings said. They all laughed.
Ailia stood up and brushed the snow off her dress and overcoat. 
“Be careful what you do to her, for your heads will soon be on my chopping block,” Soren said.
“Well, the damage will already have been done by then,” one of the other Vikings retorted. “And from the look of things, you are going to be losing your head here in a little bit,” he said.
Ailia’s heart pounded in her chest as she thought of the things they might do to Soren and her. “What do you want with me?” she asked, not able to silence her tongue. “If you knew who you are dealing with—” 
The short Viking walked over to her and slapped her face, knocking her into the snow again. The others all laughed loudly. 
“You will treat her with respect!” Soren yelled from this cage. “Once I get out of here, I will kill every one of you. If you stop now and let her go, I still might spare your pitiful lives.”
“Oh, is that a threat?” the eldest one said as he walked over to Soren’s cage and kicked it hard several times, causing the rusty metal to shake and rattle. They all looked at each other and laughed again. The eldest Viking lowered his head and looked into Soren’s eyes, which were peeping through a small opening. “I’d like to see that,” he said, sneering as he spoke. He then turned to Ailia who had been detained by two of the Vikings. “Gunnar would like to see you, little lady.” He walked over to her and started smelling her skin, inhaling her scent as he closed his eyes, as if to experience the pleasure more intensely. “Ah, the smell of fresh, womanly youth.” He opened his eyes. “Nothing smells better.”
Ailia turned her face away in disgust. He reeked of sweat, smoke and urine and his breath stunk like rotten reindeer meat. When he smiled, he looked like an ogre as his teeth were decayed and stained from many years of abuse and neglect. 
“Didn’t your mother teach you how to bathe?” Ailia asked. 
The Viking grabbed her hair and pulled her head backward, aiming a dagger to her throat. 
“Ailia!” Soren yelled from his cage, trying to kick it open. “I will kill all of you, do you hear?” 
“Shut your face, dead man!” the eldest yelled, not taking his eyes off Ailia.
Suddenly, Gunnar came storming out of the largest house, clearly upset. “What’s taking so long?” he blasted, his eyes wide open and his beard-covered face steaming red.
The Vikings quickly redirected their attention to their chieftain. 
“She’s being difficult,” the eldest said.
“Difficult?” he said, looking at him in disbelief. “Difficult?  She is our prisoner. There are four of you, one of her!” 
Ailia saw the opportunity to free herself. She leaned into the Vikings’s arms, lifted her feet off the ground and kicked Gunnar as hard as she could in the head. 
Gunnar flew through the air and hit the snow. Everyone stood silently watching for a moment.
 “Are you—uh,” one of the Vikings started as the others secured their grips around Ailia’s arms. 
She gasped at the pain they were causing her from squeezing her arms so tightly.
“Am I what—am I hurt?” Gunnar said, laughing as he rose to his feet. Blood had started running from his nose and lips and he wiped it away with the sleeve of his black wolf-fur overcoat. “I’m a Viking chieftain. Do you think that a sniveling girl like this can disrupt my being—all right? Oh, she’ll be a pleasure to break, this feisty one,” he said, now licking the blood from his lips. “Do you remember us? We had big plans together you and I,” he said, moving in closer.
Ailia wished she could remember. What she didn’t know frightened her.
“I don’t know how you survived,” Gunnar said. “How ever did you manage?”
“You will leave her alone, Gunnar,” Soren said calmly, but so firmly that Gunnar turned his attention to him.
“Why, who have we here?” Gunnar said. He walked over to the cage and hunched down beside it. “A helpless animal trapped in a cage, threatening his executioner? Why, Soren, I almost didn’t recognize you there behind all the wood and metal. Is this your girl?” he gestured to Ailia with a flick of his wrist.
Ailia could see Soren’s eyes growing darker and darker. She had never seen this furious side of him before.
“But, surely, this isn’t Princess Lucia, the Great Sentinor, your soul mate which you are destined to be with? Do we have a love triangle going on here?” he mocked. “How interesting—I would have to agree with you, though, this young lady is much more desirable than Her Royal Highness Lucia, who pales in comparison to this beauty.” He had closed in on Ailia and squeezed her cheeks on either side of her lips hard with his hand.
Ailia was shocked that he knew about them at all, but she didn’t want to feed his knowledge by acknowledging or denying his information.
“I am warning you, Gunnar; you touch her in any way and you will live to regret it every day of your life,” Soren said.
“I doubt it,” Gunnar retorted arrogantly, staring Ailia down. “Eiess told me many things about you and your beloved Princess Lucia, Soren. It’s a shame she is headed to Eiess’s castle to join forces with the Empress,” he said, his hand fondling Ailia’s overcoat.
Ailia glanced at Soren. Don’t say anything, Ailia. Gunnar’s just trying to get inside your mind. His words are calculating and well-placed. Don’t give him that satisfaction, she thought. Gunnar most likely didn’t know about Ailia’s connection to Lucia but was probably counting on Soren reacting to his cunning comments. 
Soren shook his head at Ailia. 
I’m not going to fall for it and divulge more information, she swore to herself. Ailia tried to wriggle out of the tight grip the Vikings held her in, but it was no use.
Gunnar smiled devilishly. Then, a stroke of enlightenment flashed across his bearded face. 
“Put her back in the box,” he ordered the Vikings. “And come with me.”
The Vikings did as they were commanded and threw Ailia back into the cage. She hit her head on the side of the cage and cut herself across the right eyebrow, muting a scream of pain that wanted to surface. She didn’t want to give the Vikings the satisfaction of knowing that they had hurt her and she definitely didn’t want to appear weak in the barbarians’ eyes. Not because she wanted to impress them, but rather, because she wanted them to know that she would not be cowered. 
The Vikings followed Gunnar back into the longhouse.
“It pains me to see them treat you this way,” Soren said. “You are hurt.”
“I’m fine,” Ailia said, her spirit feeling stronger than before. “It’s strange, but I feel proud that I was able to stand up to them. Well, on the inside at least.”
“I know what you mean,” Soren said.
 “We definitely need to find a way to get out of here,” Ailia said.
“After you went to sleep last night, I worked on sawing the bottom edges of my cage with this,” he said, showing her a small knife through the cracks of his cage. “I hid it inside my stocking, so the Vikings would not find it when they ransacked me. I am done detaching one side and as soon as I detach another, I think I might be able to break the bottom of the cage free if I lift up forcefully like so,” Soren said, as he showed her how.
“If only being a Sentinor meant that we could walk through these walls, we’d be fine,” Ailia said, wishing now that she had another power other than being able to envision the past. It seemed like a useless gift, at least in the moment.
Soren continued to gnaw at the wood with his knife, moving closer to their escape with every stroke. “I wish I had brought a larger knife, but such a one would probably not have gone unnoticed,” he said, somewhat unconsciously.
A dirty-blond, shorn-headed woman crossed the courtyard with two buckets filled with snow. The woman glanced over at Ailia and Soren and unexpectedly stopped in the middle of the yard, looking at them as if she wanted to approach them. “Can I fetch you something?” she asked, her voice subdued and meek. She set the heavy buckets down and eyed the guard, who had turned in another direction, before she shambled over to Ailia. 
“Here,” she said, handing Ailia some dried meat, squeezing it through the largest gap between the planks and quickly stepping back away from the cage a good two feet. “I’m Kelda. Im a new thrall here, serving these self-proclaimed kings—they are truly only fools. It has all gone downhill since Ava, Gunnar’s wife, left a little while back. Ava really helped keep things balanced around here. Now, it’s becoming a miserable place.” She had an unusually high voice, even for a woman and her whole upper body twitched uncontrollably as she moved. Though she was young, she was far from beautiful. Perhaps she had been beautiful at some point, but with her hair shaven, a filthy face and yellowing teeth, one missing in the front, she was not much to look at.
“Thank you,” Ailia said. “Do you have any for my friend here, also?” she asked, hoping there would be enough for the both of them to stop the incessant churning feeling in their empty bellies.
“That’s all I have right now, but I can come back with extra after I place some more wood onto the hearth inside. There’s plenty of that stuff inside,” she said, looking nervously around. “I’ll be back soon,” she whispered encouragingly and hurried off, picking up the two heavy buckets on the way.
There wasn’t much meat, but Soren insisted Ailia eat all of it, saying he would eat some as soon as Kelda returned with more. They both chewed on some snow to quench their thirsts and Soren got back to his sawing project.
 “So, tell me about our grandchildren, or great, great grandchildren, right?” Ailia invited, feeling somewhat strange asking the question and strange that she actually had grandchildren. 
“Let us just call them grandchildren; it makes me feel at least a few years younger,” Soren said, cracking a smile. 
He went into great detail about each grandchild. Ailia envisioned their beautiful faces before her as he spoke of them. “I cannot wait to meet them all. I just hope they’ll want to meet me also.”
“They are extremely excited to meet you,” Soren said, tediously working on the next plank. “I spoke to them just recently and they seem to be one hundred percent on our side. They’d probably be willing to follow us to the ends of Midgard to defeat Eiess.”
“In the past, I’ve had this recurring mare dream of Eiess and I wonder if it might be a memory from one of my first two lives. We had a daughter named Freydis,” Ailia said, seeing if that name meant anything to Soren.
Soren stopped working and looked at Ailia. He became teary eyed. “She was our first and only child, in your second life,” he said.
“Oh.” Ailia couldn’t speak.
“She waited and waited for the day you would return. She never gave up hope that she would see you again. Even right before she died, she thought she might get word of your return,” Soren said. Tears were streaming down his hallow cheeks and it sounded like he struggled with saying the words.
Ailia covered her mouth with her hand. “Oh, my Freydis.”
Two of Gunnar’s Vikings stormed back out of the longhouse, their course set for the cages. They aggressively opened Ailia’s cage and tried to reach her. 
Ailia moved to the back, making it difficult for them to grab hold of her. 
They finally got hold of her feet and pulled her out. One pulled her by the hair and dragged her after him toward the longhouse. It was excruciatingly painful. 
Ailia could feel Soren’s eyes on her and she wanted to say ‘Don’t worry about me, I’ll be fine’, even though she had an unsettling feeling in her stomach that warned otherwise. 
Inside the warm, smoggy house, the two Vikings hauled Ailia over to Gunnar, who was sitting on a throne-like wooden chair. The room was warm and welcoming, with two hearths that burned vigorously. The Vikings pushed her to the floor at Gunnar’s feet. Ailia looked up at Gunnar and rose to her feet as calmly as possible.
“I’m going to give you two choices,” Gunnar said cheerfully, with an undercurrent of anger, playing with his beard.
“Really, only two?” Ailia said.
“Shut your mouth and hear the king speak!” one of the Vikings said, slapping her head.
“Choice number one,” Gunnar said, raising his index finger on his right hand. “Stay here and rule with me as queen of the Vikings. Rule by my right hand, as a free woman, as my wife and equal.” He paused.
“And the second choice?” she urged him, letting no emotion show on her face. Never will I be this man’s queen.
“Choice number two.” He added his middle finger to the first one. “Be sold as a thrall in Trollsoe to whoever would buy you,” he said, looking smug and pleased with himself where he sat.
Ailia glared at him for a long time, saying nothing.
“Well?” Gunnar finally asked. 
“There’s a third choice you haven’t given me and that is to let Soren and I go free. We will then promise not to destroy your settlement and exterminate you and your filthy followers.” Ailia had no idea where her strength was coming from. She didn’t fear torture, or dying, or any other manner of ill fate that would come to her if she did not please Gunnar. Though her body was trembling with fear, she felt calm and peaceful inside, standing firm on her ground, ready to demand what she wanted.
“Don’t mock me!” Gunnar scooted forward to the edge of his chair, as he pointed his finger at her. “I can order these men to kill you right here, right now. You need to think more about your life before you throw it away with your arrogance and idealistic stupidity,” he said, still calm, but with a bit more anger welling up in his tone. “And as you remember before, you made the wrong decision and Ava had no other choice but to get rid of you.” He slouched back into his chair. “Though I still don’t understand how you survived Ava poisoning you.”
What has my association been with him before? I cannot remember anything. “Well, that plan failed, seeing as I’m still here.” Wait, I do remember something. Then as if by a revelation, the memory of her disappearance exploded back into her mind. She recalled every minute detail of the events leading up to her awakening on the Blue Glaciers. After the Vikings had captured her and dragged her here to their settlement, Ailia had sat imprisoned in the very cage Soren was in for a month. Then Ava had requested Ailia become her handmaiden, and Ailia had been allowed to move around the settlement, though still bound by a long chain around her ankle. The problems had started when Gunnar had wanted Ailia to become his second wife. Ava had gotten jealous, but Gunnar wouldn’t stop pursuing Ailia. He had tried to rape her once, but fortunately, Ava had walked in on them and she had put an end to it, threatening she’d leave Gunnar if he didn’t stop. After that Ava had kept Ailia with her at all times, saying she was protecting her and her marriage. Then, Ava had become pregnant, and Gunnar had accused her that the child wasn’t his. Lastly, Ailia remembered right before Ava had left the settlement, that she had forced poison down Ailia’s throat and dragged Ailia to the glacier cave with two Vikings. They had beat her senseless until she lost consciousness.
Strange, Ailia thought, the thing that was supposed to kill me, led me to Soren. She turned her attention to Gunnar. “The only stupidity I see is coming from you, sitting there on your self-made throne, pretending to be a king among men, when all you are doing is leading a bunch of fools with wills so weak that they seek nothing but to be ruled by your ignorant, incompetent hand.” She paused and waited for his response, thinking he just might order his Vikings to take her life.
“You have chosen then?” Gunnar said, his answer almost sounding like a plea.
“Yes,” she said steadfastly, clenching her trembling fists, feeling her fingernails digging into the palms of her hands.
