“Such a shame. Such a loss. You could have been ruler of the Vikings, queen of them all,” Gunnar said, sitting back into his chair, placing his elbow on the armrest and leaning his chin on his sturdy fist. “Take her away and prepare her as we discussed.” He looked away.
Before the Vikings were able to restrain her, Gunnar spoke again. “No wait!” He stood up and walked over to Ailia, grabbing both her arms and pulling her close in to his body. He kissed her passionately, forcing his tongue into her mouth. When he was finished, he let Ailia go softly. “Now, do you remember the passion we shared?” he asked, his blue-gray eyes searching hers.
Ailia spit in his face. She had never felt so violated in her entire life. Her eyes burned with wrath and she had never truly hated anyone until this moment. “We had no passion. You wanted me, but I never wanted anything to do with you,” she said, remembering the day he had nearly raped her. 
“She’s all yours, boys,” he said, wiping the spit off his face with a handkerchief—a handkerchief that looked exactly like the one she had found on the glaciers.
The Vikings seized Ailia and took her to the back room. She thought they were going to rape her or kill her, but she would rather that than give her will to Gunnar. It was better to die honorably, than to marry a man she hated and be without the man she loved and was predestined to be with. 
The two men sat her down on a stool in the back room and took out a pair of shears. 
She knew it was useless to fight, so she sat still as they chopped all her beautiful dark locks off. Tears of anger flowed down her cheeks as they transformed her from a free woman to a thrall. At least, my heart is free. 
When they were done, Ailia looked down at all her beautiful hair that had fallen on the floor. I won’t mourn the loss of my hair. She tried to convince herself. 
“Thrall!” the Vikings mocked. 
“You are the ugliest woman I’ve ever seen,” one said.
I don’t care, Ailia thought.
The men took her back outside, one Viking grabbing her by each arm. But instead of taking her back to her cage, they headed toward an enclosed sleigh that was attached to a single brown horse. She looked around, searching for Soren, but he was nowhere to be found. His cage door was wide open and blood trailed in the snow from the cage, down the snow-packed path, between two houses and into the woods.
“Soren! Soren!” she yelled, knowing that they had hurt him or, fearing even worse, ended his life. “Can you hear me?” Her voice was one of severe desperation in which the most terrifying answer came in return: none.
As they opened the door to the sleigh, she kicked and screamed, knowing that if they transported her to Trollsoe to be sold as a thrall now, she might never see her family, or Bergendal, or Soren ever again.
After they had forced her into the sleigh, they locked her in. 
 “You’ll be regretting your decision sooner rather than later,” one of the Vikings said. “And your friend there—” he said, looking over at Soren’s open, bloody cage, his hands resting powerfully on his hips. “—Good luck ever seeing him again. His dead carcass will be fed to the wolves.”
Ailia lunged over to the small cage window and wrestled to get it open. “You will live to regret this day!” she whispered furiously, tears brimming in her hazel eyes. “I’ll remember you and I’ll come for you. I’ve imbedded your face into my memory and the day you see me again will be the day you die.”
The Viking laughed. “I guess I’ll be living to a ripe old age then!” He got up onto the sleigh and whistled to the horse to start moving.
Ailia’s hands clenched onto the bars of her moving prison as she looked back at the disappearing settlement. I’ll die with my light still unshed. For many more hopeless hours, she kept her gaze fixed on the horizon as it vanished before her tear-filled eyes. 



32

Northlandic Castle

“What a surprise! What are you doing here, Lucia?” Eiess asked when she saw Lucia enter the throne room. “Welcome, Great Sentinor.”
Lucia clutched the infant in her arms as if he were her own. Vilda had escorted her to the Empress.
“I see you have brought a child? Is it yours?” Eiess asked, not bothering to rise from her throne.
“No. It is not my child. This is the child of a Viking king,” Lucia said. “I saved his life and I will raise him as my son.”
Eiess’s lips sprouted a devious smile. “Gunnar’s son,” she whispered. “How may I help you, Your Highness? It is very courageous of you to come here after all that has happened.”
“I have come to side with you. I believe I have been on the wrong side all along,” Lucia said. 
“Of course you have been on the wrong side. I could have told you that years ago. Why the sudden change now?” Eiess wondered.
“My eyes have finally been opened to many lies. I have been taken advantage of and used to promote the other side’s purposes,” Lucia said. Thinking about their deceitfulness still stung.
 “So, Lucia, what can I offer you?” Eiess said.
Vilda stepped forward. “I believe the princess should be asking us that question, Your Grace,” she said.
“Very well, I will concede to that, Vilda. So?” Eiess said, glaring at Lucia.
“I can offer you information that will open your eyes to the truth instead of the mirage in which we have all been led to believe,” Lucia said.
Eiess raised her eyebrows. “Are you saying I have been deceived?”
“Yes, we all have,” Lucia said. 
“How very interesting. And how do you know that I do not already know the full truth?” Eiess asked.
Lucia did not, but she was still willing to bet her life on it. “So, are you saying you have no need for my services, then?”
Eiess looked at Vilda and then back at Lucia. “No, that is not at all what I am saying. This is your home, just as much as it is mine.”
“Ha!” Lucia said. “It certainly did not feel like it when you had me locked up in the castle’s tower and sent your Surtorians to beat me,” Lucia said.
“I am so sorry for that,” Eiess said. “Will you please forgive me?”
“Your cheap apology is accepted,” Lucia said. “But for me to join you and if you expect me to reveal anything to you, I need your sworn protection.”
“How would I know that you are telling the truth?” Eiess asked.
Lucia smiled. “I have proof and you will know in time. Test me, if you please and let me prove that I am being truthful.”
Eiess looked at Vilda. “Ready the queen’s chamber for our new guest.”
Vilda smiled and bowed deeply, her dress flaring wide. 
Eiess exhaled. “United we will extinguish the rebellion.”
Vilda escorted Lucia back to her new chamber and left straightaway. 
Have I made the right decision? Lucia thought. She held tightly onto the small infant. My life and this child’s life, may be in danger. She sat down on the four-post walnut bed—the same bed she had been born in and the same bed her mother had died in. Maybe I should leave before it is too late. 
Vilda entered the room with some food and a fresh outfit for both Harald and Lucia. Her orange and gold dress made her look extraordinarily large and freakishly pale. “I am having Lena bring in the crib your father made,” she said. “It was the one you slept in when you were a babe.” Vilda stood still for a moment. “Just to let you know, Eiess named all the handmaiden’s in the castle, Lena. It is so she can keep things simple around here.”
Lucia thought that was very strange, not to mention impersonal. 
Vilda placed her hands on her elephant-size hips. “I must let you know and it is apparent to everyone here in the castle, that my heart is shattered because I lost my beloved brother,” she said dramatically.
Lucia gently placed Harald on the cobalt-blue silk bedding. She loved hearing him coo and watching him gnaw on his clenched fists. “Are you hungry, little one?” Lucia asked, playing with his feet. “Who killed him?” Lucia asked Vilda, not taking her eyes off the infant. The memories of her and her father’s last conversation rose to the forefront of her thoughts. He did give his life for me, I will give him that.
“Several guards were ordered to end his life simultaneously,” Vilda offered.
“You mean kill him, right?” Lucia said. “Say it—kill him.”
“Yes, kill him,” Vilda lowered her eyes. “I never wanted it, you know,” she said, stepping closer to Lucia.
“Perhaps.” Lucia continued to play with Harald’s feet. “But you did nothing to prevent it either, did you?”
Vilda’s face flustered. “There was no other alternative, Lucia.” 
“You could have let him live,” Lucia said, looking over at her aunt. She is a selfish, fat coward.
“No, he threatened the Empress’s life. He even threatened my life. That is treason!” Vilda said. 
“Treason? Ha!” Lucia barked. “Who is to say what treason is anymore. You stole the throne from my father, your own blood and made him—and me, may I remind you—a prisoner in our own home. Was that not treason?” Lucia’s eyes fixed on Vilda.
“He was never a great leader and something desperately needed to be done—someone needed to do it. I could not change the man, nor the way he reigned. I did not possess enough power,” Vilda said, her voice rising.
“Is it much better now?” Lucia asked. “Have all the problems of Midgard suddenly disappeared with this new ruler? She rang in the eternal winter, you know. Did you fail to recognize that?”
A handmaiden entered the room with the crib and silk sheets for the newborn.
“There are fewer problems and most things have improved, yes. Eiess listens to her advisers, you will see. She changes things when needed and she is not deceitful like your late mother and father.” 
“We will see,” Lucia said, undressing Harald. 
He started fussing.
“I will need more new clothes made for Harald,” Lucia said to Lena. “Seven outfits to start with—one for each day of the week. Outfits that are fit for a future king of the Northlandic Kingdom.” She lifted Harald into her arms. “I also need a wet-nurse immediately. Harald seems very hungry. It has been hours since he has eaten. I will need a new wardrobe. Send the tailor in first thing tomorrow morning. I cannot bear to wear these repulsive peasant clothes anymore.”
“One of the Lena’s just had a child. I will summon her, Your Majesty,” Lena said and curtsied. “And we have an excellent tailor who is in charge of all of the Empress’s wardrobes. I will have him here tomorrow morning.”
“Swell, that will be all.” Lucia walked over to the stained-glass window and over looked the fallen snow-white kingdom.
“Eiess would like to meet with you as soon as you are settled in,” Vilda said, stepping next to her.
Lucia glanced briefly over at Vilda. “Ah, Bergendal,” she said, bouncing Harald up and down, patting the crying child on his back. “It has changed much since Eiess has taken over. Not for the better, I am afraid. Tell Eiess I will meet with her in the throne room at sunset.”
“Yes, my lady.” Vilda started to leave.
“It is ‘Your Highness’ to you,” Lucia corrected, not softly. “I am still the heir to the Northlandic Throne. Your mind seems to be slipping, old woman.”
Vilda’s lips puckered. “I must give you some advice, so that your head is not the next thing to roll,” Vilda said perturbed.
“No, you must not. That will be all,” Lucia said, throwing Vilda a threatening glare.
Vilda huffed and left the room.
Lena came quickly and started nursing the ravenous child. The child suckled at the woman’s full breast until his sleepiness overcame him. 
He is my child, Lucia thought. There is a divine connection between he and I—I can feel it. The gods have brought him to me, so that I will have an heir. 
When the sun had gone down, Lucia walked through the corridors. How can I take back the throne? It was her throne, rightfully, as granted by the gods, as was right by her bloodline. She entered the throne room and Eiess was already sitting on her throne in an indigo silk dress. 
“Greetings.” Eiess smiled. “May I offer you some food or wine?” 
“Yes, food and wine,” Lucia said.
“Lena,” Eiess summoned one of the handmaidens in the room.
Lena stepped over immediately. “Yes, Your Highness.” She bowed her head.
“Prepare repast for us both and bring up some red wine immediately. We will dine in here,” Eiess said.
“Yes, right away, Your Excellency,” Lena said and departed faster than a galloping horse.
“I can imagine it must have been hard for you to come back here,” Eiess said. 
Four Lena’s came back and set up a small table with two chairs in the center of the throne room.
“It was,” Lucia said. “I could no longer stay where I was, so the choice was clear.”
Eiess stood up and took Lucia by the arm. “You are probably wondering how to get your throne back?” She walked them both back to the table.
Lucia was shocked, but she tried not to reveal it. I should not show my emotion. 
“I would expect nothing less from you. Of course, you are the rightful heiress of the Northlandic Throne. I would be a fool to think otherwise.” Eiess offered her to sit.
“I would be a fool to accept your first offer,” Lucia said.
“Offer?” Eiess said.
Lucia sat down. “You are going to offer me something, are you not?”
Eiess sat down on her chair, her light green eyes reflecting the torches and candles in the room. “Yes,” she said. “You are a smart one.”
“So, just start with the offer you were going to give as a last resort,” Lucia said. I will not be taken advantage of ever again.
“Very well.” Eiess smiled and folded her hands on top of the table. “I want the Aesira Jewel and in return, you will be crowned queen of the Northlandic Kingdom.”
“And—?” Lucia leaned forward, her elbows on the table now.
Eiess scoffed. “What else is there to give you?”
“I want Vilda dead. She betrayed my father and me. I want Ailia dead. She used me to get what she wanted,” Lucia said.
Eiess leaned back in her chair and smiled cunningly. Lena came with two gold goblets and poured the wine.
“Ailia is your sister. Do you not have any loyalty toward your family?” Eiess sipped from her goblet.
“She was raised a peasant and when she saw the opportunity, she betrayed me like her worst enemy. Do I owe her anything?” Lucia shook her head.
Eiess set her goblet down on the table. “No, but she is your sister, after all. The only family you have left besides Vilda.”
“I do not consider her or Vilda family,” Lucia said. “Should I?”
Eiess studied Lucia’s face for a while before saying anything. “When I left Alvheim and all the other Sentinors tried to bring me back, I refused to listen to them. I did not want to live under their oppressive rules any longer. There was no individuality, no freedom to express, no gratification. I am talking about the gratification that comes from creating something unique. All we were to do was protect Midgard and humanity.” Eiess traced the rim of her goblet with her middle finger. “Soon, I found that my existence was not fulfilling anymore. All I lived for was to please and help humans and of course the humans were never grateful for my many years of selfless sacrifice.”
“Do you regret leaving?” Lucia asked.
“I regret the relationships I lost. I loved the Sentinors. I loved the oneness we shared. I still love them now. But do I regret leaving and claiming my right as a living spirit to choose my own path?” She leaned forward and looked Lucia intensely in the eyes. “Never,” she said.
“I am like you,” Lucia said.
“You still have time to go back. It is not too late. You can still save your relationships, still fight on the other side.” Eiess drank from her goblet again.
“Would you let me go just like that?” Lucia asked.
“Yes,” Eiess said. “ I would be very put out, of course. Together, you and I would be invincible. With your powers as the Sun Queen of the Aesira bloodline and my powers as a Sentinor, we would be as powerful, or even more powerful, than Ailia and Soren combined.”
Lucia’s eyes wandered as she thought about Eiess’s offer. The idea excited her. This means I will have to make a choice, one that is for certain, without any doubt in my mind. 
Three Lena’s entered the throne room and decked the table with mounds of heavenly food.
Lucia had not noticed how hungry she was until the aroma hit her nostrils. She gulped all the wine from her cup and held it up for more.
Lena walked over and filled it to the brim.
“Eat,” Eiess offered, gesturing to the food.
After the meal, they both walked into the queen’s chamber. Lucia was relieved to see that Harald was still sleeping soundly in his new crib. A dress and sleeping tunic were laid out for her on the bed.
“I had them bring these in for you to wear, while we are waiting for more to be made,” Eiess said. “They should fit. They were your mother’s.”
Lucia lifted the gown off the bed, squeezed it tight to her chest and smelled it. Her mother’s rose-perfumed scent still lingered in the garment.
“If it is too much to bear, I can have—” Eiess started.
“No, this will do,” Lucia said, clinging onto the dress.
“Good,” Eiess said, gliding over to the door. “Take as long as you want on your decision. After a decision has been made, I expect it to be a life commitment.”
Lucia nodded. “Of course, as would I.”
“Sweet dreams,” Eiess said as she exited.
Lucia slipped into her mother’s sleeping tunic and hopped into bed. I am the Sun Queen now. She heard Harald stirring and grunting in his crib, but he remained sleeping. “What a day,” she said out loud as she remembered all the events. She never thought she would have ended up here today. Eiess’s offer had been very generous and she was right—They would be more powerful than Ailia and Soren together. Right as she was about to fall asleep, something hit her. How does Eiess know about Ailia? 


33

Frostland

The creaky sleigh arrived in Trollsoe as the sun made its first appearance of the day. Ailia couldn’t stop the tears from streaming. Her journey had been long and cold, travelling through high, snowy mountain paths and between gorges of ice, but that didn’t bother her as much as not knowing what had happened to Soren. If Soren had been killed, she wanted to die also. 
People were busy in the streets, opening their shops in the over-crowded market place. Leather, furs, wool, clothing, jewelry, fowls and beasts were among the merchandise being sold. The industrious city had managed to outgrow Bergendal over the last decade, most likely because of its port, which was beneficial for merchants and tradesmen. It had been over a hundred years since Eiess had burned the city to the ground, and there was no sign of destruction anywhere. 
Towering buildings and houses that were, in Ailia's mind, taller than necessary had popped up recently and the city was expanding by the day. Passing by each road, she could peer down the street all the way to the busy longhship-filled harbor. 
The Viking steered the sleigh onto the main path of the city and headed for the bay. She passed men women and children busy about their days, dressed in heavy overcoats and furs. Trollsoe seemed a happy city and everyone was probably unaware that Ailia’s hometown Bergendal had just been destroyed by the Vikings. Information travelled slowly and it might even be weeks before the news came here.
Ailia remembered vaguely the story Soren had told her about her first life. Is this where I lived with Soren? She wondered.
Shortly after they had crossed the city border, Ailia noticed a young man following her sleigh. He couldn’t be more than in his mid to late teens and he was not what Ailia would call large in stature. In fact, he looked like he could be called the shortest man in Trollsoe. He kept his black curly hair in a ponytail and he wore a brown-bear fur coat with his hood down. Sneaking after the sleigh, he waved to Ailia and smiled. 
Ailia grabbed the bars of her cage and looked out. “Can you help me?” she asked, not knowing whether he would be able to hear her in the distance with all that was going on. Other carts, horses and sleighs rushed by and the bells of the bell tower started chiming throughout the city.
“Quiet!” the Viking yelled back at Ailia. “Or I’ll take a beating to you.”
Suddenly the young man took off down the street. Ailia sat back down. That was strange, she thought. Longships and fishing boats lined the docks. The Viking stopped by the first longship and approached an elderly man who was busy bundling some rope. 
“I’d like to sell a well able, strong, young female thrall. She is a good worker and has served us well in the past,” the Viking said. Seagulls filled the air, squawking for food, diving for fish. The scent of fish infused the air.
“You will need to go see Mercer,” the old man said and pointed to another man, who was dressed like a nobleman.
The Viking directed the sleigh over in front of Mercer’s knarr and repeated his proposition. 
“One silver coin,” the nobleman said in a foreign accent, holding his finger up. 
The Viking scoffed. “That’s it?” he said. “You’ll have to do much better than that. She is worth at least ten silver coins, my girl.”
Only ten? Ailia thought. That is the value of my life? It seemed ridiculously low.
“If you want more for your female thrall, you will want to go see Erik or his son, Leif. They are Vikings and buy women for much more,” the nobleman said. “Six longships that way and you will find them.” He pointed.
The Viking nodded and followed Mercer’s direction. Finally, they arrived at a brand new longship. 
“Are you Erik?” the Viking asked a man who was directing his crew.
“That depends. Who are you?” the man answered. His eyes were a deep blueish gray and never rested too long on any one thing. He was large in stature and had silver strands of hair shading his dark blond hair.
“I am Oddvar. I was told you buy thrall women?” 
The Viking snorted and spit. “Ah! I am and yes, we do,” Erik said, placing his hands on his hips. “But today, we are full. No more passengers will fit on this fine dragonship. It is fine, don’t you think?” Different colored shields were lined up on the longship’s edge and the sides of the ship were painted in mustard yellow, rust red and deep-cobalt blue. 
“Uh, yes. She is young and able and—” Oddvar pulled in closer to Erik and whispered something in his ear.
Erik’s eyes lit up. “I see. In that case, I think we can make an exception for one more girl, ay?” he said. “Leif! We have another passenger!” 
Ailia wanted to know what Oddvar had whispered.
Leif came running and looked into the cage. “Five silver coins,” he said.
“Bah, Ten!” Oddvar insisted.
“Eight, or we leave her behind,” Erik said, handing him the coins.
“Eight it is!” Oddvar said and stuffed the coins in his sheepskin overcoat pocket. Oddvar opened the door of Ailia’s prison and pulled her out. 
“Do you speak our language?” Erik asked her, holding onto her arm tightly.
Ailia squeezed her lips together and looked away.
“She speaks Norse,” Oddvar said and knocked her on the side of the head.
Ailia wanted to shove him back, but she reasoned it would be best not to start a long trip at sea in a bad way.
The young man who had followed her earlier appeared. “May I buy her from you?” he asked. “I can pay you ten gold coins.” He jingled a leather purse.
A much better offer, Ailia thought. I would much rather go with him.
Oddvar looked at Leif and Erik and nodded, his eyebrows eagerly rising up.
“No, she has already been sold to us,” Erik said, pulling Ailia toward him and then pushing her toward the longship.
“Twenty gold coins?” the young man said.
Erik looked as if he were thinking about it. “No,” he said again.
The young man shifted uncomfortably. “I’m willing and prepared to pay you one hundred gold coins for her, but that’s all I have,” he said and huffed. “You’d be a fool to turn it down.”
They would be crazy not to take it, Ailia thought.
“What is she to you?” Oddvar asked.
“That’s my business,” the young man replied.
“Load her up,” Erik said to Leif. 
Leif looked at him as if he were crazy. “But—”
“Load her onto the ship, Son,” Erik said sternly.
Leif shook his head but did as his father commanded.
Who is this young man? Ailia wondered. I don’t know him, do I?
“Then I surrender my freedom and am willing to come with you as your thrall on your longship,” the young man said to Erik.
There was a pause and the Vikings glanced at each other.
“Leave us be and keep your freedom,” Leif said. “Don’t waste your life on a thrall girl.”
“I’m a great worker and have labored on longships before. I know I would be very valuable to you.” The young man moved into Erik’s way, standing between the longship and Ailia. 
He is mighty bold to be so young and small in stature, Ailia thought.
“If this is some sort of foolery, I’ll have your head,” Erik said. “Now, get out of my way!”
The young man shook his head. “No, the only thing I desire is to come and serve you, so I can be with this woman here,” he said, pulling Ailia by the arm.
“Very well, but I will need those one hundred gold coins from you,” Erik said. His eyebrows arched nearly halfway up his forehead.
The young man didn’t flinch or pause but handed Erik the heavy leather pouch. 
Erik looked like he had made the best deal of his life as he looked into the pouch. “All aboard!!” he yelled, grinning from ear to ear. 
 “Who are you?” Ailia whispered as they boarded the longship. She looked up at the massive red sail, which carried a black raven. Just my luck, more bloodthirsty Vikings. She felt the ship move on the water’s surface and she felt a panic rise inside her, thinking about being on a ship on the water. They maneuvered toward the stern of the ship, passing rows of wooden chests lining the deck’s outer sides and crates and barrels carrying cargo.
“I was just going to ask you the same question,” the young man said.
“Why are you doing this? Helping me, I mean?” Ailia asked.
“When I saw your cart—” He paused.
“What?” Ailia asked.
Leaning in, he whispered, “I saw a light coming from it.” They passed some horses, cows and goats on the deck. 
“Light? Hmm—and that made you follow me and give up your freedom?” Ailia asked.
“Yes,” he said. “Because I believe I know who you are, but I’m afraid to say,” he said, his voice even lower now.
“Don’t be afraid,” Ailia said, hoping he would know who she was and that was why he had risked his life for her.
The ship’s stern was carved like a dragon’s tail and painted gold and Ailia could see, from the rear, the ship’s massive dragonhead prow painted in the same gold. 
“Ah, you see the dragon,” Leif said, walking up next to Ailia. “It’s to keep away the sea devils and Jormundgand, the Midgard Sea Serpent that encircles all of Midgard’s waters. You know of him, right?”
Ailia looked at him fearfully. What other ominous creatures exist out there that I used to think were just a fable?  
“You, sit here, you, here,” Leif said, directing them. He grabbed a rope and tied their hands and feet together, which he then tied to a steel ring secured to a beam on the deck. “We treat our thralls like we treat our animals. You’ll have food and water, but you have no rights other than to breathe the fresh air and to think your own thoughts. We own you now, just like those animals over there,” he said, pointing to the beasts on board. He marched off and came back carrying a hefty, grey fur throw. “For you to share, so you don’t die from the cold.” He flung it toward them. “Prepare to cast off!” he shouted to everyone on board.
The young man turned to Ailia again and wrapped the fur around her freezing body. “You are Light, or Lucia,” the young man said.
Ailia remained silent. How does he know?
“I am Erlend Junior,” he said, grinning broadly, deep dimples etching his cheeks.
Ailia thought for a moment. “Erlend—?” she said. Then she remembered and she gasped. “Erlend!” she yelled, trying to embrace him. “My grandson!” She felt guilty immediately. “You shouldn’t be here! You need to get off this ship. What about your parents? They’ll go crazy not knowing where you are.”
“When I saw you, I ran back home to Sorenhall as fast as I could. I searched for my parents, but they weren’t there. So my grandfather gave me money to purchase your freedom. I asked him what to do if they wouldn’t sell you and he said, 'go and bring Light back, no matter the cost.' So, I couldn’t just leave you. I must complete the mission I started. I’m not a person who gives up so easily, if I might say so myself,” Erlend said.
“Thank you for what you did,” Ailia said. “I’m very glad you are here. Now, how are we going to get out of this mess?”
“Well, it looks like we are headed to Frostland and then Floraland, so we’ll have a long time to think about it,” Erlend said.
“I don’t like the water or boats very much,” Ailia said, remembering her drowning dreams and the Nukkern.
“Well, I can understand why,” Erlend said. “I’m a great swimmer, so if anything happens, I’ll help you.”
Ailia worried. “But, we will both freeze to death in the icy water.”
“Not likely,” he said. “I can manipulate the temperature in the water and heat the water around our bodies. And the only way you can die, is either by beheading or drowning, right?”
Ailia didn’t know that, but she hoped it was true. “Well, I hope it never gets to that,” Ailia said, dreading the thought of entering any water ever again.
The sixty-four Vikings on board readied the ship for departure. They each sat down on their sea chests next to their lengthy ores and started eagerly rowing out to sea. Their rhythm was steady and sure, impelling the longship forward with each forceful row.
Ailia’s heart sank. How would she ever find Soren again, if he were even alive? The thought of his death petrified her. Suddenly, she thought she heard her name being called. 
“Ailia! Ailia!” The voice echoed in the distance. She tried to stand up but couldn’t as the ropes held her down. Is that who I think it is? It sounded like Soren and Silya’s voices.
“Ailia!” Soren yelled.
“Ailia,” Silya yelled.
“Soren!” She screamed at the top of her lungs. He’s alive!  “I’m here, on the Viking ship!”
Erlend started chiming in. “She is here! Ailia’s here!”
The longship had reached further out to sea now.
Leif came over and gagged both Ailia and Erlend. “Shut up! You belong to us, did you forget?” He gave them each a potent kick and headed to the ship’s prow and said something to Erik.
Ailia knew she didn’t belong to anyone other than Soren. She hoped Soren and Silya had heard them. But even if they had, how would they be able to rescue them without a ship?
Erik stood at the front of the longship. “Listen up, everyone. On the last journey, we had some troublemakers on board.” He walked up and down the narrow lane. “They didn’t follow instructions perfectly. I would love to see any others try to do the same. Throwing people off my dragonship bound and gagged is one of my favorite things to do.”
Ailia cringed. She suspected his speech was directed at them.
“Bring the ores up!” Erik yelled.
All the Vikings lifted their ores in from the water and set them aside.
“Prepare to hoist the sail!” Erik blasted.
The Vikings all ran to different stations and waited for the next command.
“Sails up!” Erik bellowed.
The red sail caught the strong wind immediately and carried the longship forward. Waves crashed against the bow of the longship, sending splashing saltwater over the edges and onto the deck. The ship swayed up and down as it drove forth.
It’s all lost, Ailia thought. She started to cry. Erlend told her they’d work it out and not to worry, but she wouldn’t be comforted. 
After they had sailed for some time, one of the Vikings came over and undid their gags. “Time to eat,” he said and threw them each a bowl of some type of mush.
Ailia hated every Viking on the ship. And hadn’t it been for the intense hunger churning in her belly, she would have refused their food. “How long until we get to Frostland?” Ailia asked Erlend.
“Much depends on the winds. If the winds are as strong as they are now, we could be there in less than a week.” 
Ailia moaned. “I’m not feeling so well,” she said, suddenly acutely aware of the unsettling feeling in her stomach.
“Do you think you are going to throw up?” Erlend asked.
“Maybe.” She glanced at him and moaned again.
“Sir, sir, could we please have a bucket? My friend here thinks she is going to throw up,” Erlend said, tugging on the nearest Viking’s overcoat. 
“Well, then she’ll just have to do it overboard,” the Viking answered.
Ailia reached for her mouth and moaned again. “Could you untie me so I can relieve myself?” she said.
The Viking hopped over and cut her and Erlend free. “Suppose you cannot escape now,” he said. 
Ailia sprinted over to the side of the ship and hurled. 
“Not used to sailing?” Erlend said, coming up behind her. He stroked her gently on the back.
Ailia looked at him, feeling drained. “I’ve never been on a ship until now,” she said. “Well, not during this life, anyway.”
“You will get used to it after a while,” Erlend said. “I sail all the time.”
Ailia tried to smile, but the vomit was too fresh in her mouth and she quickly found herself hurling again.
By the end of the day, Ailia had vomited more times than she could count. And though the weather was beautiful and sunny, the winds were strong and continually swayed the longship back and forth. Finally, the sun set on the horizon, sending the heavens into a whirlwind of rouges.

*    *    *

“We’ll stay one day only when we arrive in Frostland,” Erik directed his crew the next morning. “Remember, no plundering there. This is our regular overlay and we need to keep the relationships peaceable.” Erik came over to Ailia and Erlend. “Where did you say you were from exactly?” Erik asked Ailia.
“Bergendal, sir,” she said, standing up. Erlend had managed to locate shears to even out Ailia’s hair. It looked much smoother now and actually was a little wavy.
“How did you end up being a thrall? You don’t seem to fit the part,” Erik said.
Ailia looked at Erlend. Should I tell him?
Erlend nodded.
“I was captured by Vikings when they raided Bergendal,” Ailia said.
“So, you are a native then?” Erik asked.
“Yes,” Ailia said.
“Interesting. What was the name of your Viking chieftain?” Erik blinked.
“Gunnar.” Ailia felt like he was interrogating her.
“Haven’t heard of him. I thought I knew all the Viking chieftains. Must be a rogue.” Erik came closer. “You live close to Eiess, don’t you, ay?” 
Ailia hesitated for a moment, but then nodded.
“Eiess,” Erik said slowly, his eyes narrowing. “I work for that Dragon, you know. She has sent me out to hunt for the Great Sentinor.” He grabbed the rails of the longship and looked out to sea. “Eiess is uglier than the Midgard sea serpent, Jormundgand.” He turned back to face Ailia again. “Have you heard about this Great Sentinor named Lucia?”
Ailia swallowed. 
“She is supposed to destroy Eiess and get rid of this wretched winter,” Erik said. “I would like that. But what I’d like even better is to find the Great Sentinor. If I do find her, Eiess has promised me an unlimited supply of longships. Nothing would be more rewarding than that!” He laughed wickedly.
“I’ve heard of the Great Sentinor,” Ailia said. “But, isn’t she just a myth?”
“No, she’s real. Eiess had her captured in the Northlandic Castle some months back, but she escaped,” Erik said.
“I’ve never met her,” Ailia said.
“Me neither,” Erlend chimed in.
“Hmm,” Erik said. “No one has. She may have just vanished into thin air, this mysterious Great Sentinor. She is supposedly guided, you know. Do you believe that all men and women are guided?”
“What do you mean?” Ailia asked. 
“Guided by a higher power, like the gods?” Erik clarified. 
“I, uh, I don’t know anymore. I thought I was, but lately, it seems I’ve had nothing but trouble,” Ailia said. 
Erik laughed heartily. “I like you, Ailia. You are sincere.” He paused and then said, “Let me give you a piece of advice. Just know that the sun still shines just as brightly, even though it is behind the blackest of clouds. And usually, I have found, to get to Valhalla, you have to spend some time in Helheim.” Erik marched off and yelled at some of his Vikings.
“It seems like it’s going to be night forever,” Ailia said, halfway to herself, halfway to Erlend.

*    *    *

Three days after departing Trollsoe, Ailia finally got used to the swaying longship and was able to keep some food in her stomach. Over the course of the week, she filled Erlend in on all the details about how she had come to this point. They arrived in Frostland seven days after departure. 
“Remember men, no plundering here!” Erik yelled as the crew of Vikings stormed off the longship.
They had landed on the southwestern side of the island in Grindale. Before they had reached land, Erik had spoken of a hot spring of water they were going to visit. “Azure Lagoon,” he had called it. Ailia couldn’t wait to have her feet meet the unshifting soil. 
Erik sent one of his men over and had him chain Erlend’s and Ailia’s ankles together. “Erik is obsessed about protecting his investments,” the Viking said. “You run, you die.”
Ailia and Erlend limped off the longship and got in line behind the two rows of Vikings. 
On solid ground again. Ailia felt liberated from the fear of drowning she had been living with the past week. 
“Every man may do as he pleases. I’m personally going to take a bath!” Erik said.
The Vikings chuckled.
“Be back tomorrow. That is when we leave for Floraland. All relieved!” Erik turned around and they started hiking toward the spring. About half the Vikings left the lines, while the other half followed their chieftain.
Ailia could tell just a few steps into the trip that the chain around her ankles was going to rub her skin raw, unless she did something to prevent it. She tore off a piece of her skirt and stuffed it between her stocking and the metal. She did the same for Erlend.
“Thank you,” Erlend said.
“It’s the least I can do for the man who risked his life for me,” Ailia said, taking his arm.
Frostland seemed milder than the Northlandic Kingdom’s mainland. The gentle sloping hills were covered in snow, but Ailia was surprised to see splotches of bare earth between the whites. “Have you been here before?” Ailia asked Erlend as they walked.
“Yes, once,” Erlend said. “With my father many years ago. When we were here, a small volcano erupted.”
“That sounds frightening,” Ailia said.
“It wasn’t much of a volcano, though. No one was hurt,” Erlend said.
The walk over to the milky-blue springs was not far at all. At the springs, Erik approached a hefty blond. The woman came over to Ailia and Erlend right away. 
“Ailia comes with me,” the woman said, unlocking their chains. “If you try to escape, I will kill you. I am Gilda. Good to meet you. Now come.”
Ailia looked over at Erlend. “Will you be all right?”
Erlend nodded.
When Ailia and Gilda arrived at a small wooden bathhouse, Gilda undressed. “Do you not want to bathe?” she asked Ailia.
“Uh, yes, I do,” Ailia said and began undressing. But inside she felt terrified. She hated deep water.
“I will have your clothes cleaned,” Gilda said, taking them from Ailia as she disrobed.
“Thank you.” Ailia felt uncomfortable being in the nude in the presence of strangers. “Is there a towel I can use?”
Gilda looked at her and grimaced. “For what?” she said. She stepped outside and started for the springs, her pale fat jiggling as she trotted toward her destination.
Ailia couldn’t help but laugh. She followed Gilda, trying to act as normal as possible. When the chill air hit her bare flesh, her skin cringed and turned into a blanket of goose bumps. At the springs, Ailia tested the water with her toe. Warm, almost hot. And to her relief, she saw that it wasn’t deep at all. She stepped out of the freezing air and into the bath of heat. It relaxed her entire body, and she even found herself enjoying it. Gilda looked happy, frolicking in the spring’s waters.
After the bath, Gilda handed Ailia an off-white linen under tunic and a light green wool over tunic to dress in and brought Ailia back to her longhouse with her. “Erik is expecting you,” she said. 
At the longhouse, most of the Vikings had made their way back from wherever they had been and sat at the long table, partaking in a grand meal. Gilda brought Ailia over to Erik right away.
“Ailia,” Erik said. “Serve me my meal and then, you may eat all you want in the back room there with Erlend.” He pointed.
Ailia did as she was told and then joined Erlend in the back. His stomach looked as if he had eaten a horse. 
 “Not bad, not bad,” he said moaning, his hands massaging his protruding belly.
Ailia smiled. “I’m glad you got enough to eat.”
“The food is even better than at home.” His heavy eyelids closed as he spoke.
Gilda arrived shortly after and chained the two together again.
After eating all she could, Ailia settled down to rest, her back propped up against a wood pillar.




34

Death

“Ailia—Ailia wake up!” she heard someone call. She hadn’t even noticed that she had fallen asleep. Someone was tenderly caressing her cheek. The voice almost sounded like Soren’s. I must be dreaming. 
“Ailia.” Someone shook her arm now and she opened her eyes. Soren is here. This was the perfect dream. 
“Wake up!” Soren said, stroking her arm. He looked wet and cold.
Ailia heard raindrops beating on the rooftop. “Soren, I’m so sorry for all that has happened.” Maybe he is dead and this is his spirit visiting me. A sinking feeling set in. I would die if Soren were dead.
“Come with me,” Soren said, gently tugging at her arms. 
“I love you,” Ailia said. “Am I dreaming?”
“No, you have got to wake up!” Soren grabbed her hand and pulled her with him.
Just then, she realized that she was not dreaming, but that Soren was actually there with her and he was trying to bring her with him unnoticed by the slumbering Vikings. She opened her eyes wide and gasped.
“Shh.” He turned to her and kissed her lightly on the lips. 
She desperately wanted more, but she knew all too well that they had other things that needed to come first—moving to a safer place being the most important. “Wait,” she said. “Erlend Junior is here.” She shook Erlend and woke him up.
“How did he get here?” Soren asked.
“He found me in Trollsoe. I’ll tell you about it later,” Ailia whispered.
Soren nodded.
“Follow us,” Ailia whispered, helping Erlend to his feet.
Their chains rattled in the dark and Ailia worried that the sound would awaken the Vikings.
Soren knelt down and looked at them. “I do not think we can get you out of these now,” he whispered. “You will have to try to be very quiet as we leave.”
They snuck out the back door that was closest to them. A heavy, low-lying fog had rolled in and a mist of clouds drifted above the glowing springs. The rain came down light, tickling Ailia’s face as it landed on her skin. 
Soren signaled them to follow him. Outside, Ailia could see Ivar and Silya waiting with three horses. Soren signaled to Ailia and Erlend to mount one horse and he jumped onto the horse Silya was riding. Ivar rode alone. 
After they had ridden for some time, Ailia finally dared to speak. “How did you find us?”
“We just followed the Viking’s tracks to Trollsoe and once we got there, it was easy,” Silya said. “We heard you yelling for us from the longship. After we realized we could not get you off the ship, we just asked around the port, where the ship was headed. A few pieces of silver was all it took.”
“Soren, how did you manage to get out of the Viking camp? When they brought me back outside, you were gone. I saw lots of blood and I thought they might have killed you,” Ailia said.
“I owe my life to Silya,” Soren said.
“Yes, you do.” Silya laughed.
“The Vikings stabbed me in the abdomen and left me in the woods for hours, probably thinking that I would die. But when they came to check up on me again, I was still alive. They then decided to decapitate me, but Silya came riding up just in time and shot them down with her bow and arrow. All three at once! You should have seen it,” Soren said. 
Silya looked like she reveled in his compliments.
“So, you’re hurt?” Ailia asked.
“I am fine. I am the Sentinor of healing and this wound was not too deep. I am just a little sore, that is all. Are you both all right? Any injuries I need to know about?” Soren asked.
“No,” Ailia said.
“No,” Erlend said.
“That was a very brave thing you did, Erlend. Thank you for watching after my Ailia,” Soren said.
Ailia liked how he called her his. 
“It was the only thing I could do. And to me, there was no other option than to make sure she was safe,” Erlend said.
Ailia gave him a squeeze. “Thank you.”
“We have a small karve ready at port and we will need to leave immediately. If the Vikings find out that you are missing, they might come after us. It all depends on how vindictive their chieftain is,” Soren said.
“He seemed fairly reasonable—at least for a Viking. But my guess is he will probably come after us,” Ailia said. 
They rode on for a while in silence and arrived at port. 
“This small karve is no match for Erik’s longship, if he decides to pursue us,” Erlend said.
Ailia knew he was right: the longship was really small. And she dreaded having to get onto yet another ship. She reasoned with herself that she’d be safe with Soren on the ship, but her heart still pounded nervously and her insides felt like fluid. After Soren paid the portman for the horses, they were on their way. 
Out in the bay, the fickle wind tossed the karve’s sail in different directions, moving it nowhere, so they decided to row until they picked up a steady breeze. Clouds were still rumbling in the heavens and lightning flashed in the distance. 
Soren came over to Ailia and Erlend and picked the lock of their chains. The lock clicked open.
I am free! Ailia jumped into Soren’s arms. They stood in each other’s embrace and squeezed each other tightly. But her moment of joy was short-lived. Suddenly, she noticed small, moving specks of light off the shore. Are they torches? Her stomach sank. “Torches.” She pointed to them. Oh no!
The others glanced back toward land. 
“It appears they have discovered that you are missing. Quickly, kill the lights. Hopefully, they have not seen us,” Soren said.
Ailia could see Erik approaching the portman and the portman pointed out to sea in their direction. 
“Oh, Mjoelnir!” Ivar said. “They are coming for us!”
It looked like the Vikings had seen them and were all headed straight toward their dragonship.
Ailia estimated there were about twenty or so of them total. It didn’t look like all of Erik’s Vikings had come down to the shore, thankfully. But now it was inescapable—there would be a battle at sea.
“Row!” Soren said, grabbing his ore.
Everyone aboard the karve grabbed an ore and rowed for their lives, knowing full well that the odds were sorely not in their favor.
“Stop rowing,” Soren said, suddenly.
“What?” Ailia said. “They’re going to kill us!” She knew she was going to die today.
“I need to call upon someone for help if we want to make it out of this alive.” Soren took off his overcoat and two tunics and dove into the freezing ocean.
Ailia ran over to the edge of the karve and looked for him in the deep. What is he doing?
Soren surfaced again to the top a ways away and swam toward them. They helped hoist him back in the boat and got him dressed. 
“Can I try something?” Erlend asked.
“Of course,” Soren said. “Anything at this point.” His dark hair dripped of saltwater.
“I’ve been practicing on developing my endowment and though I haven’t yet perfected it, I might be able to speed the ship up with some winds.” Erlend fidgeted where he stood.
“How dangerous is it?” Soren asked.
“Well, last time I tried it, my boat capsized,” Erlend said and grimaced.
Ailia felt all the blood leave her face, and she felt suddenly very weak.
“Oh.” Soren nodded and his eyes flared.
“But that was over a month ago and my boat was just a small fishing boat!” Erlend confessed. “I’m much better at it now.”
“Let us try it,” Soren said.
Ailia’s chest started revolting as she thought about the water and her most recent near-drowning experience. If anything went wrong here, even Soren probably could not keep her safe. But there was no other option.
Erlend nodded. He breathed out quickly twice and cracked his knuckles. Closing his eyes, he raised his arms into the air and stood still.
They waited. Nothing happened. The Vikings were getting closer—too close.
“Erlend?” Soren said. “They are closing in on us.”
“Wait, just a little while longer,” Erlend said, keeping his eyes closed, now squinting them shut. “Now, hold on tight!” he yelled.
Suddenly, a gust of wind caught the sail and thrust the karve forward. 
Ailia nearly fell out into the water, but Soren grabbed her arm, pulling her back in. Holding on tightly to him, she kept repeating in her mind, Soren will keep me safe, Soren will keep me safe, Soren will keep me safe.
“Whoa!” Erlend said. “This is amazing! I’m simply amazing!”
Ailia heard Soren laugh. She looked into his eyes in the darkness and they both smiled. Their boat seemed to skim across the surface of the water—they were moving that fast. We’re going to make it! Then, out of nowhere, the wind ceased and the karve stalled. 
“I don’t understand,” Erlend said. He stood up again and refocused. Nothing happened. He breathed deeply, fretted and cursed the winds that didn’t bow to his command.
The distance between them and the dragonship was great, but the Vikings were gaining on them quickly.
“To the ores!” Soren yelled. 
They all took their places and started rowing again. Ailia knew it was of no use. They needed a miracle. 
Within minutes, the dragonship had overtaken them. The first Viking jumped into the karve. Soren pulled out his sword, stabbed him through and through and pushed him into the sea. Two other Vikings jumped in. Ivar pulled his dagger and slashed at one of them. Soren struggled a little this time, but finally slashed off the arm of the other and kicked the Viking into the ocean. 
Ailia stood behind Erlend, who had picked up a sword from the deck and moved in front of her. 
“Don’t worry, I’ll protect you,” Erlend said.
Then, she felt a rope lasso her body. It came from the dragonship. She screamed. She was drawn toward the edge of the karve and even though she resisted, she couldn’t help but be pulled out into the water. Dangling from the rope with her torso above water and her legs below, she screamed. She looked up and saw that Leif was at the other end of the rope, pulling her upwards. “Soren!” she yelled. “Help!”
Soren came over immediately. Then, Soren did something Ailia wasn’t expecting. He slashed the rope with his sword. Her body sunk into the ocean, the painfully cold ocean. I cannot swim! I’m going to die! She opened her eyes to see if there was anything she could grab to pull herself up, but right in front of her, she saw the Nukkern’s ugly face before her. Petrified, she started hitting him with her fists.
He grabbed her arms. They were so strong. He then pulled her further under with him and placed his lips over hers. 
Ailia knew her life was over. I have failed, yet again. Now Eiess would rule forever and humanity would slowly disappear from Midgard. She’d never fulfill her purpose, she’d only die and her and Soren’s souls would vanish forever.
 But then, Ailia felt air fill her lungs from the Nukkern’s breath. She inhaled and pulled back.
He smiled. It was a grotesque smile, but a smile nonetheless. 
Ailia exhaled and he pressed his lips to hers again, exhaling air into her lungs. Is he—helping me? Did Soren beckon for him?  Ailia exhaled.
Yet a third time, the Nukkern came toward her, exhaling air into her lungs. Then, he exploded upwards out of the water and came back down with three Vikings clutched in his grip. 
The Vikings kicked and squirmed, but the Nukkern was too strong for even the three of them. He dragged them down to the deep, so deep that Ailia couldn’t even see them anymore.
Then, the Nukkern came back to her and granted her air before bursting to the surface again, only to bring three more Vikings back down with him. He disappeared into the depths with the savages and neared Ailia. Taking her hand, he led her to the surface where Soren and the others were waiting for her. She felt extremely cold now, numb and shaking uncontrollably. Even her fingernails and toenails hurt. Ailia was surprised when she couldn’t see a single living Viking anywhere. They must have killed them all off and pushed them into the ocean. The Nukkern jumped back into the sea and disappeared.
 “You have been under for a few minutes. Erlend will help you warm up.” Soren offered her his hand and pulled Ailia out of the icy water.
Ailia thought he seemed a little too calloused for what he had just put her through. “I could have died!” she said.
“No, I would never have allowed that.” Soren guided Ailia over to Erlend who sat down behind her and wrapped his arms around her body.
Ailia felt excessively cold, but just then, she realized that she could probably have been under the icy water for a lot longer and still have survived as long as she had air. I’m a demi-mortal. Then, she started feeling heat emerging from Erlend. The hotness spread through her body immediately and in an instant, she felt warm again. 
Silya came over with a change of clothes. “This is all I have,” she said. It was one of her Sami outfits.
“Thank you,” Ailia said and took the clothes. 
Just then, Ivar collapsed onto the karve’s deck right next to Ailia.
“Ivar!” Soren called. He rushed over from the mast and knelt down beside the fallen warrior.
“I think those Vikings got me good, Soren,” Ivar said, uncovering his hand from his chest, near his heart, revealing a ghastly wound.
Soren looked at Ailia, his eyes exuding sadness. Immediately she could tell that Soren didn’t think Ivar would survive. 
“I have had an amazing life,” Ivar said. “It is an honor to die this way, defending the Great Sentinor. The gods will—” he took a deep breath and looked at Ailia. “—surely welcome me in to Valhalla.”
“Can’t you do something to help him, Soren?” Ailia asked.
“No,” Soren said.
“Why not?” Ailia asked. 
“I cannot. I am sorry, my friend.” He cradled Ivar’s head in his lap. 
“I am fine. I feel no pain,” Ivar said.
Ailia wondered if Soren was helping relieve Ivar’s discomfort. She didn’t understand why he couldn’t save Ivar’s life. He has healing powers, doesn’t he?
“You must succeed, Ailia. You must. For me, for your mother, your father, for all us fallen for freedom’s sake, for your sake,” Ivar said.
Ailia nodded fervently, her eyes full of tears. She wanted to say I will, but the words got stuck in her throat. I will. I will. 
Ivar’s eyes defocused and Ailia could see that his spirit had left his body. 
“I cannot do all things Ailia. I am not an omnipotent healer.” Soren brushed his fingers over Ivar’s eyes, closing them.
“I’m sorry, I didn’t know,” Ailia said, her hand reaching for his. Though, in her mind, he still seemed to be that which he didn’t think he was.
They took over Erik’s dragonship and sent Ivar’s body out to sea in the karve. When the small longship had drifted a ways, Silya shot ten flaming arrows, setting the karve on fire. She sang a melancholy Sami burial melody.
“What happened to the Nukkern?” Ailia asked after the brief ceremony. “I wanted to personally thank him for helping me.”
“He left. He is not into socializing. He has also now fulfilled his commitment to me and is free to do as he wishes.” Soren came over to Ailia and wrapped his arms around her. They watched the flames of Ivar’s burial ship ascending to heaven. “I am never going to let you go,” he said. “Will you stay with me forever?”
Ailia nodded. “Forever,” she said.
The bottomless sea swallowed up the humble karve and the great warrior, and the living began their journey home in their new dragonship.



35

A New Beginning

Trollsoe bay looked just the same as when Ailia had left—active and iced over. Each seaman worked on either readying his ship to sail or on overseeing his men to do it for him. So busy, Ailia thought, looking at the port. Crowded longships stacked up against each other like fish in a basket. She couldn’t wait to meet the rest of the family—her family.
Sorenhall stood only five longhouses away from the bay and when they arrived, Liv greeted them all back in the courtyard with a generous embrace. “Welcome to our home.” Her barely-there eyebrows moved as she spoke. On their way home from Frostland, Soren had described Liv as the most enthusiastic woman in all of Trollsoe and Ailia could see why. Liv spoke and moved with the energy of an excited five year old.
Small circular wooden tiles coated the sweeping roof and the body of the longhouse was made of stone. It was the largest longhouse Ailia had ever seen.
“Please, please come inside.” Liv looked overjoyed to see them. “You must all be so very exhausted. It’s good to see you again, Silya.”
Silya nodded. “Same here.”
Liv wrapped her arm around Ailia’s shoulders and steered her toward the entrance. “I’ve wanted to meet you since the day I was born!” she said. 
Ailia smiled. “And I, you.”
“Watch your head there,” Liv said as they entered through the low-framed door. “Now, there’s an eastern entrance too, but this entrance is the main one and leads straight into the kitchen. My husband Otto is out fishing today with Erlend Senior. Poor guy. Erlend Senior, I mean, not my husband. He can barely keep up these days. You’ll meet them both later. They’re never late for repast.” 
Liv’s casual demeanor helped put Ailia at ease, which was well needed, after all she had been through this past year. 
“Now, I always, always speak my mind, whether my words make sense to anyone else or not, so please don’t take anything personally or read too much into it. Sometimes, I cannot even make sense of what I’m saying myself,” Liv explained.
Ailia laughed.
“So, here is the kitchen with the hearth. I’ll fix you something to eat in a minute and follow me,” Liv said. “Here is the pantry and storage room and of course—” she waved them all to continue to follow her, “—here is the main room. I’ll have you sleep in here on the benches next to the children over there. Will that suit you?  If not, I can always—”
“It will suit us just fine,” Soren said. 
“Or, maybe you can sleep in the cot, or, no, I have a great idea,” Liv said, not divulging it, but looking very excited about it.
Two hearths glowed in the center of the narrow, long room and low-lying platforms lined the walls on either side. The loom and spinning wheel were situated on the left platform next to where the children slept and three longtables decked the platform on the right.
Soren whispered to Ailia, “If you remember, Liv and Alva are our great grandchildren. Liv married Otto and together, they have three young children. Alva married his wife Eira and together they have two children. You will get to meet them all later.”
Ailia nodded. She wanted to meet them, so she could put faces with their names. My children’s children. 
“Ah, there you are!” Liv said, welcoming the men home. “Erlend Senior and Otto, meet Ailia.”
“Erlend Senior, Freydis’s son and the patriarch of the family, is slowing down at the ripe old age of seventy-three,” Soren whispered. “You will like him.”
Erlend Senior headed straight over to Ailia and hugged her warmly. “I have been waiting all my life, just so I could meet you,” he said. He looked a lot like Soren, but just a lot older and had gray hair instead of dark brown.
“Me too,” Ailia said, tearing up. Freydis’s son.
Otto came over and embraced her next. “It’s an honor to finally meet you. Please, our home is your home. You are welcome to stay—well, forever if you’d like.” His thick, black beard fluttered when his lips moved and his kind hazel eyes seemed to smile even though his full lips did not.
“Thank you,” Ailia said. 
That evening, when everyone had arrived back home, Erlend Senior took it upon himself to formally introduce all the youths of the family to Ailia. “This is Stina, she is seventeen and Vidar here is eighteen and, finally, Erlend Junior, named after me of course. Well, you already met him, but he is nineteen, almost twenty and is the eldest of all the grandchildren,” he said, looking proud, sitting in his chair waiving his crooked, old cane. “Liv and Otto are very blessed to have such lovely children. Aren’t they beautiful?”
“Yes, so very beautiful,” Ailia said. “Glad to meet you all.” They all had a special glow about them, she thought and they all had the same black curly hair as Erlend Junior did, definitely a feature they had gotten from their father, Otto.
“And here are Alva and Eira’s young ones. Martin just turned eighteen and Solvei is the baby of them all,” Erlend Senior said.
“I’m not a baby anymore! I just turned sixteen!” Solvei yelled. “And, who are you exactly?” Solvei asked Ailia, staring her down with an iron glare and less than hospitable demeanor. Her blond, messy hair fit well with her feisty personality.
Ailia laughed warmly. “Well, I’m your—uh—.” She didn’t quite know what to say.
“Aunt will do just fine,” Liv said, standing next to Ailia. She placed her hands on Ailia’s shoulders and looked at Soren and winked.
Soren smiled and nodded. “Aunt Ailia. I like that.”
Ailia gave him a look. 
“Watch out for Solvei,” Erlend Senior said. “She is beautiful as the day is long, but she has a tongue like a dragon!” He laughed until he started hacking and coughing. 
“Erlend’s wife, Ida, died last winter of a sudden onset of pneumonia. That is why he still wears his black mourning clothes,” Soren whispered. 
I wish I would have met her too. Ailia nodded.
That evening, the family sat down for repast to a meal worthy of the gods. 
“Can you tell us a story about one of Lucia’s first lives?” Vidar asked Soren, leaning across the table, as he very inelegantly reached for the breadbasket.
Otto passed the breadbasket to him. “Let the poor man eat his food in peace and quiet. Excuse my son. His manners have seemed to disappear. He threw Vidar a one-eyebrow look. Then, picking up Soren’s empty cup, he said, “Guri, get this man some more to drink! His cup is empty.”
Guri, their thrall, stood up from the end of the table and fetched the ale barrel. She poured Soren’s goblet full.
“I do not mind telling stories. Maybe after we have finished eating?” Soren said.
Vidar smiled, tossing and catching his roll into the air. “I’ll see you later.”
The sun sank into the ocean and eight wall-torches were lit. The satiated group meandered over to the two living hearths. Shadows of gray played on the surface of the bisque oak walls and intensified as the darkness set in.
“Can I have your attention?” Silya asked the gathering. In her hands she held something covered in a black linen cloth. “I was able to locate something of extreme value to everyone here. I apologize for not telling you sooner, but I did not because of the obvious risk factor.”
Ailia looked at Soren and she could tell they were both thinking the same thing. The Aesira Jewel.
Silya had Liv pull up a table and she carefully placed the wrapped item on it. Then, she unfolded the cloth and revealed its contents: a large, multi-faceted, oval Jewel.
“Behold the Aesira Jewel,” Silya said formally. “Bishop Peter gave it to me.”
Everyone came to have a look. 
“The three-hundred and sixty faceted yellow diamond was created in Alvheim by Iluxia,” Silya said. “Iluxia took one forty facet yellow diamond from each of the nine realms and soldered the nine separate pieces into one.”
When Ailia came up to see it, she saw that the Aesira Jewel rested in a gold filigree sunbeam setting and was held in place by seven gold pave-set prongs.
“You have Aesira blood running through your veins, so now the Aesira Jewel is yours,” Silya said to Ailia.
“Now, we need to find the Aesira Scrolls, so I’ll know how to use it,” Ailia said. 
“Or Iluxia. He’ll know too,” Otto said.
“Yes, but we will have to go to him, because I have not seen him since the eternal winter started. He may not be able to enter Midgard because of it,” Soren said.
“That makes things difficult,” Ailia said.
Soren nodded. “But first things first. We need to focus on finding the scrolls and then—” he took Ailia’s hand in his. “Ailia and I have something we’d like to speak to all of you about.” 
All ears perked in the room. 
“Yes?” Erlend Senior said. Guri poured him some more wine.
“I am sure you all know by now that Ailia and I are forming an alliance to fight in Ragnarok, to take the throne back from Eiess and destroy her,” Soren said.
Everyone nodded.
“We both had serious doubts whether or not to ask you what we are about to ask you,” Soren said. “Because we worried that your lives would be a risk.”
Ailia steadied her trembling breath. 
“We both agreed that you, our beloved family, are the best choice to fight at the forefront alongside us.” Soren looked at Ailia.
“There would be no greater honor than to fight and defeat Eiess, with you on our side, at the final battle of Ragnarok,” Ailia said.
Erlend Senior spoke up immediately. “What, are you completely senseless?  Who do you think we are, exactly?”
Ailia’s heart leapt into her throat. “Well, you see—if—” 
“Shut up and listen you two,” Erlend Senior said, wagging his old, crooked finger at them. A vacuous emptiness of words and breaths held the previously merry room hostage. No one moved. Not even the youths.
Erlend Senior leaned forward, his glare fixated in Soren’s. “We will not support you in the final battle,” he paused. “—only. We will support you in every battle from today and until the final battle! We are in!” He lifted his goblet of wine, splashing some of it onto the oak floor. 
Ailia exhaled in relief.
“Hip, hip!” Otto said.
“Hip, hip!” Alva and Eira chimed in.
“Hip, hip!” Everyone in the room cheered with their goblets held high.
“Then the first step of our plan is underway,” Soren said, squeezing Ailia’s hand.
Ailia felt she could breathe again and hope and plan for the future again and now, she had a much grander reason to succeed in her battle against the Empress.
After repast, the youths competed in who could balance the longest on a vertical plank. Erlend Junior looked like he would win.
Soren approached Ailia. “Would you like to take a walk with me?”
“I would like that very much,” Ailia said, still not having had any private time with him. They put on their overcoats and told the family they would be heading out for a walk. 
Large, but few, snowflakes sailed from the partly cloudy heavens as they stepped outside. Quietness had fallen over the sleeping Trollsoe like a reverent prayer, as if the gods had granted Ailia and Soren this rare moment of peace.
“I used to think everything was written in the stars,” Ailia said, looking up at the shimmering lights that peeked out in between the clouds. 
“And now?” Soren asked.
“Now, I realize all things are written on my heart.” Ailia grabbed his arm and leaned her head onto his shoulder.
“Even me?”
“Especially you,” Ailia said. “Always you.”
Soren took both of Ailia’s mitten-covered hands in his and walked backwards, pulling her along. “I have something I would like you to read,” he said, pulling out an old, yellowed scroll and handed it to Ailia. 
“What is this?” Ailia asked.
“Just read it,” Soren said. 
Ailia carefully unrolled the fragile scroll, afraid she might damage it. The words on the letter were blurred and streaked as if drops of water had bled the ink. She read,

My dearest Spiritus Amor Soren,

Ailia looked up.
Soren’s eyes urged her to continue and Ailia continued.

Eiess’s longship arrived at the docks today and I have decided to surrender, so that many lives will be spared, including yours and Freydis’s. I suspect Eiess will drown me at sea, since she knows that it is only by that method that I can die. She is still as deceitful as she was when she broke our covenant in Alvheim and descended into mortality in Midgard, violating the deified barrier between mortals and divine beings.

Ailia breathed. “I wrote this.”
Soren nodded and Ailia continued reading.

We should have prepared much better in this, my second life, to conquer her. But how could we have known that she had grown so much stronger than before? You must not blame yourself—promise me you will not. This is not the end, Soren, but the beginning of Ragnarok, which will give us our freedom to love each other forever. This is not the time to mourn our apartness but to plan for the greatest event in our lives. You must continue to plan for my return and gather as many allies as you can. Try to find Eiess’s weakness, so we can use it against her when we need to. I will be back and though I will remember nothing of who I am or of our previous lives when I am reborn, I know that you will help me remember.
Please take care of Freydis. She is so beautiful and I cannot mourn enough that I will not be able to raise her. One day, her children and our descendants may support us and help us in the final battle of Ragnarok.

Ailia started crying. When I wrote this, I was with our daughter. 
Soren stepped behind her, kissed her on the cheek and cradled her in his arms as she continued to read the letter. 

Know that I am always with you, thinking of you wherever I am and waiting to be reunited with you, my Spirit love. Remember what Iluxia said in Alvheim before we clothed ourselves with mortality: our hearts will speak the truth to our souls—that is how we will find each other and remember who we are to each other and to the world. Our love for each other will guide us back and help us overcome the destroyer of all freedom, happiness and light.
My greatest fear, my love, is not to be tortured or to even suffer death again, but rather that I might not be able to fulfill my destiny in life with you by my side. I do not know when I will return, or when you will find me again, but know that I will be waiting for you to awaken my soul with your voice and your loving touch.
The word ‘love’ does not encompass enough of what I feel for you, for it is so much more than mere love that we share. I am in you and you are in me. We are the creation of two spirits sealed together as one, created for each other for the eternities. When our souls join, they burn with more fervor than the Sun, they move more than the strongest of winds and give life, like the fountain of eternal living waters. Together, we will fight the battles of Ragnarok and together, we will triumph. 
I will be waiting for you, Soren, so wait for me and keep your eyes and your heart open for the day when I will return to you. And remember, at the end of all things, it will not be hope that will keep us going, it will only be each other.

All my love and life, Lucia

“We must plan for our new lives now,” Soren said, coming around to face Ailia.
“Yes.” Ailia could barely see through her veil of tears. She tried to imagine how difficult it must have been for Soren to wait for her all these years.
Soren drew Ailia in close. They gazed into each other’s eyes, both smiling, their noses almost touching. He caressed her cheek first and then he traced the edges of her lips with his fingers. Ailia could see in his magnificently blue eyes how much he needed her and though she had imagined this moment countless times before, she had never anticipated how blissful a state his touch would send her. 
Soren started showering Ailia’s neck and face with kisses and she closed her eyes, tilted her head back and let out a sigh. Her spirit merged with his, exalting her into a higher state; yet, she had never felt more true to herself.
Then, finally, he kissed her as he reached behind her head and gently pressed her lips to his. Their faces so near, so sensitive to the touch, sweeping against each other, as their cold noses brushed with each wet kiss. Their breaths mingled warm and close in the chill winter air, together as one. Ailia got lost in her senses, feeling only love coming from him and her loving him back. 
He is mine and I am his. The kiss grew stronger and more passionate and Ailia’s desire for her Sentinor had never been deeper. She could feel in his kiss that he had been waiting for this moment too, and that he wanted and needed her in every possible way. She wondered if it had been this wonderful during her first two lifetimes, if he had been able to arouse her body to this level of absolute need to have the flames quenched. His arms moved across her back, pulling her closer to him—so close, she could feel his heart beating. Ailia reached behind his head now and locked her arms behind his neck. She tasted his sweet lips, but she couldn’t get enough and wanted to be even closer to him still. Her heart dropped a little when she felt him starting to pull away.
Still embracing her body, he looked into her eyes. “I have something else I would like to show you,” he said. 
“What?” Ailia replied, her chest still pounding, her body still yearning so much for his. She didn’t want to be anywhere other than in his arms, kissing him, holding him, making love to him.
“Come with me.” He took her hand and started down the road. 
Next to Sorenhall was an empty lot. In the middle of the snow-blanketed field, Ailia could see timber and the unfinished construction of a longhouse. 
They waded through the snow and finally at the construction site, Soren said, “Our new home.” He guided Ailia in between the sawdust and logs. “I started building it right before I met you. Something told me that I needed to prepare for us again.” He got down on his knees, pulled back his hood and looked up at Ailia. “For three-quarters of a century now, I have been waiting for you and awaiting the day when we could begin our lives anew. The loss of your life has been the hardest thing I have had to endure, but now, having you stand here in front of me, I realize that the pain has all been worth it, just so I can see you and marry you again. Will you, Ailia?  Will you marry me?”
Ailia fell to her knees and kissed him over and over again, tears streaming from her eyes. “Yes, Soren, yes,” she said. “I have always known there was only one person for me, a special someone waiting for me and now, I have finally found you again. I’m already yours, forever.”
Just as she had said the last words, the earth below them started to quake. A worried look flashed across Soren’s face and he quickly lifted Ailia to her feet. “Back to Sorenhall,” he said. By the time they had arrived back home, the quaking had ceased.
“Did you feel the earth move?” Otto asked them when they entered.
“Yes,” Soren said. “It must have been a small earthquake.”
“No,” Erlend Senior bellowed from his rocking chair, his eyes clear as the sun. “Eiess knows you have found each other. She has just sent legions of Surtorians out after you.”
“Erlend Senior can sometimes see events as they unfold, even if they are far away,” Soren said to Ailia.
“How many is in a legion?” Ailia asked.
Soren looked at her. He looked worried. “Six thousand.”
Ailia shrunk. “And how many—legions—?”
“Six,” Erlend Senior said, the whites of his eyes white from the vision.
“Six? That means there will be—” Ailia did the math in her head. “Thirty-six thousand Surtorians.” The only reference she had to these Surtorians was from her mare dreams and that one incident by the Bergendal Stave Church and brushing shoulders with only a few of them had nearly shaken her to pieces.
“We should head for Floraland,” Soren said.
“No!” Erlend Senior approached Ailia and Soren. “You must go to the place where Eiess does not want to go. You need to go south, where the sun shines almost all day and all night,” he said.
Soren nodded and looked at Ailia. “Mumtaz will be delighted to have us.”



Epilogue

Brandersgaard

Unni flopped down by the lit hearth, her body aching and her spirit down. Brander, Sigrid and her had worked on repairing their farm for the last week and the endless piles of rubble blanketing the interior of their longhouse had finally seemed to decrease. The hole in the roof would take major repair and it didn’t look like they would receive help from any neighbors, as the other longhouses were in just as bad of a condition, or worse than Unni’s and Brander’s. 
The night of the raid, the flames had swallowed up most of their wooden benches and any other valuables had been plundered. Fortunately, it had snowed heavily the night the Vikings had invaded Bergendal and the fire on the roof had dwindled on its own.
Brander walked in with soot all over his troubled face. “The last measly chicken just died,” he said, holding the dead fowl up by its legs. “Damn Vikings. And where is Soren and Ivar in all of this?” He sat down on the floor and crossed his legs.
“I don’t know, Brander. They’re probably looking for Ailia,” Unni said. Always so grumpy.
It seemed like half of Bergendal had disappeared with the Vikings. Brander and Unni’s neighboring farm had burned to the ground and they considered themselves lucky to have only major repairs. 
“Has anyone located the Bishop yet?” Unni asked. Strange how he disappeared.
“No, he vanished with the rest of the captives. Rumor has it that they were all transported to Trollsoe immediately to be sold as thralls. He probably joined in that group of unfortunate souls.” Brander wiped his sweaty forehead with his forearm.
“I wanted to go and visit the church today and help with the clean up. It was a miracle the whole church didn’t burn down.” Unni stood up. She took off her apron and reached for her overcoat. “Are you coming with me?” she asked.
“You’re leaving our house to go help someone else?” Brander sounded upset.
“Precisely. Do you have a problem with that?” Unni asked.
Brander grumbled. “I think I’ll stay here and keep working on the roof. As long as the hole is still there, any warm air is going to escape and we are going to freeze.”
Just then, Sigrid limped in. “I’ll help you, Brander. You go, Unni. The church needs it. Besides, maybe there’ll be some clues there that will help you find Ailia.”
“Good, I’ll be back before dark,” Unni said and shut the door behind her. She took a shortcut through the woods and soon came to the stave church. 
A stranger exited the church and greeted Unni on the front steps. “Good day, my sister in Christ,” he said and bowed. “My name is Draper. How may I be of service?”
“Good day. I’m Unni. Nice to meet you. Are you new to the area?  I cannot remember seeing you before,” she said. “Either way, I have come to be of service and I’d like to help clean up, if that’s all right with you.”
“I am new indeed—a few weeks now, as a matter of fact. Any help would be greatly appreciated.” Draper took her by the arm and led her inside. He smiled broadly, watching Unni closely as he spoke. “Are you Ailia’s aunt?”
 “Yes,” Unni said, not wanting to volunteer more information than needed. Strange that he knows. Unni noticed that Draper dressed differently than the other Deacons. He wore a black tunic linen alb, with a purple cloth over his left shoulder. A golden Thor’s hammer was embroidered on the bottom of the purple shawl. His head was shaved in the center like a circle. She didn’t like his flashy outfit.
 “Ah, the Great One,” Draper said, his voice high-pitched and unusually feminine-sounding. 
Unni stopped walking. “What do you know about the Great One?”
“Oh, nothing. I am only a servant of the Lord, my lady,” Draper said, dodging the question and bowing his head humbly.
Unni didn’t feel comfortable around Draper like she did around Bishop Peter.
“The outside of the church is barely touched, but so many things were destroyed inside,” Draper went on.
“Oh, I see,” Unni said, walking through the chapel. More things had vanished than she had initially noticed. She saw that some of the stained-glass windows were broken and the floor was covered in rubble and broken candles. All of the silver had vanished and the statues were either decapitated or were missing. How could they do such a thing to the Lord’s house?
